Undergraduate Texts in Mathematics are generally aimed at third- and fourthyear undergraduate mathematics students at North American universities. These texts strive to provide students and teachers with new perspectives and novel approaches. The books include motivation that guides the reader to an appreciation of interrelations among different aspects of the subject. They feature examples that illustrate key concepts as well as exercises that strengthen understanding.

For Ann

How could I be so lucky!

Preface

Point set topology was my first love in mathematics. I took the course as an undergraduate at Loyola University in New Orleans and my professor, Harry Fledderman, told me to go to the library and solve all the problems in the book while he tutored the other student who had signed up for the course. (Yes, I know it sounds strange today, but there were only two students in the course.) I kept a notebook with my solutions, and once a week I reported for his inspection of my work. I felt like a real mathematician learning real mathematics. It had a great influence on me and made me realize how much I wanted to be a mathematician. Even now I can't tell you whether the love I have for point set topology was the cause of this feeling or whether that love was a consequence of this learning style. I was disappointed to later discover that research in this area had mostly petered out. I found equally attractive research areas in which to sow my oats, but I always retained this youthful love affair.

You can probably guess that I have long wanted to write a book on this topic, but other things took precedence. I am glad that was the case because now I think I have a better approach. I had an epiphany about halfway through my career when I realized I didn't have to teach my students everything I had learned about the subject at hand. I learned mathematics in school that I never used again, and not just because those things were in areas in which I never did research. At least part of this, I suspect, was because some of my teachers hadn't had this insight. Another reason is that many authors write textbooks as though they are writing a monograph directed at other faculty rather than thinking of the students as the audience. Also, mathematics refines and refreshes itself with time. Certain topics that were important at the inception of an area fade in significance, and some that are useful in various areas today must be added. Other topics are important, but only if you are one of that small percentage who specialize in a specific part of research; such things should not be taught to everyone who takes an introductory course. In addition, when a subject is developing, there is an emphasis on finding the intellectual boundaries of the concepts. Unless that viewpoint is abandoned when the subject is taught, it results in a greater prominence of pathology. An examination of early texts in any subject will reveal such an emphasis. With time, however, it is crucial to decide what should be taught in an introductory course such as the kind this book is written for. I see the purpose of a course in point set topology as giving the student a set of tools. The material is used in almost every part of mathematics.

In addition, I have come to believe that in teaching, it is best to go from the particular to the more general, an approach that has dominated my presentations in the last several texts I have written. To begin with, that is the way mathematics evolves. In addition, my experience is that all but the very best students find such an approach more digestible. The present book reflects my belief in this approach.

I see this text as aimed at an undergraduate audience that has had calculus and been exposed to the ideas of basic set theory like subsets, unions, intersections, functions, and little else. Nevertheless, I think it advisable that they have had at least a semester of analysis and been properly exposed to convergence and other topological notions in the real line. (I have included some appendices to help bridge the gap, but I am sure this will not suffice for all.) I also think point set topology is an excellent place to begin learning how to digest and write proofs. Thus, I tend to go slowly at the start of the book, including more detail than is needed for a seasoned student and even more than I include later in the text.

Following my philosophy of beginning with the particular, I start with metric spaces. I believe that these are far easier to connect with students' experience. They also seem to me to be the more prevalent topological spaces used in other areas and are therefore worth extra emphasis. Chapter  defines and develops abstract topological spaces, with metric spaces as the source of inspiration. I narrow the discussion by quickly restricting the focus to Hausdorff spaces. Needless to say, some of the more elementary arguments in topological spaces are the same as those in metric spaces. There is no problem here; I just refer students to the metric space proof and invite them to carry out the analogous argument, which in most cases is almost identical.

Chapter  concentrates on continuous real-valued functions. My belief is that the continuous functions on a space are more important than the underlying space. Maybe that's because I'm an analyst. I know that much of modern topology concentrates on the underlying geometry of a space, but surely that must be saved until after the student has encountered the need.

Biographies. In this book I continue the practice, started in a previous work, of including short biographical notes when a mathematician's result is mentioned. There is no scholarship on my part in this as all the material is from secondary sources, principally what I could find on the Web. In particular, I made heavy use of the site at the University of St Andrews

http://​www-history.​mcs.​st-andrews.​ac.​uk/​history/​BiogIndex.​html

and Wikipedia. I continue my practice of emphasizing personal aspects of these lives over their mathematical achievements, especially if there is something there that interests me.

Style. The longer I am a mathematician and the more I write, the more I ask myself questions about style. There is a difference between what you write and how you speak. That's true in mathematics just as it is outside of mathematics. I think I write more informally than many mathematicians, but there are some rules I try to follow even though they are not universal. One such rule is that with essentially no exceptions I use symbols like   and so on as verbs and only as verbs. (For example, ∈ translates as "is an element of" and not as "in.") I think this consistency expedites reading. I experimented a long time ago with using such symbols only as prepositions but quickly decided this was awkward. When I am having a discussion in my office, I will frequently use them both ways, but when I write I try to stick with using them as verbs. So don't forget to read them that way.

Long ago I realized that every result is not a theorem. The label "Theorem" is reserved for the truly important results. It's not that those labeled "Proposition" are unimportant, but they may be more routine or, perhaps, they just don't have the impact on the development of the subject at hand. A corollary is a direct consequence of a proposition or a theorem. A lemma is a result whose usefulness is usually limited to the proof of the next result.

A Word to Students. If you want to learn mathematics, you cannot approach it as a spectator sport; sitting on the edge of the pool and dipping your toe in will not get you into the subject. You must jump into it with both feet, commit yourself, and do a lot of dirty work and splashing around before you can enter the profession at any level. In the course of this book there are many places where I leave proofs to readers as exercises; do them. When I give such an exercise, I think it is well within the scope of your ability provided you understood the concepts. Doing those exercises will confirm that you understand what has come before; if you cannot do them, it may mean you overlooked something and should go back.

Throughout the text you will see words like Verify ! and Why ? I am trying to put a speed bump in your reading. I want you to be sure you understand what was just said.

Sometimes my exercises ask a question. A basic part of mathematics is deciding whether something is true and then proving it. Mathematicians are constantly trying to discover whether something is true and are seldom, if ever, presented with a known truth and asked to prove it. So when you see such an exercise and you think something is true, you must prove it; if you think it false, you must find a counterexample. (The ability to manufacture examples is a precious talent that you should cultivate.)

For most of you this is the class where you will perfect your ability to execute a proof. A proof is a written explanation of why a particular statement is true. If you're a veteran at writing proofs, ignore the rest of this paragraph. For the less experienced, here is some advice about discovering and writing down a proof. (a) Write down the hypothesis under the label GIVEN. (b) Write down the conclusion under the label TO SHOW. (c) Be sure you understand all the terms used in (a) and (b). (d) If you have difficulty, try drawing a picture. Look at an example. Try rephrasing the hypothesis and conclusion. Try to construct a counterexample. (Sometimes this makes you see why you cannot get a counterexample and, hence, why the proposition is true.) (e) If all this fails, be extremely clever.

Have fun, live long, and prosper.

John B. Conway

Washington, DC

# 1. Metric Spaces

John B. Conway1

(1)

Department of Mathematics, The George Washington University, Washington, DC, USA

Abstract

We want to abstract the idea of distance because this notion often arises in mathematics. The concept must be general enough to encompass the circumstances under which it arises, but it should conform to the intuitive notion we all have of what is meant by the distance between two points. Because this is being done at the start of the first section, before proceeding it would be profitable for readers to reflect on what properties they think should be included in an abstract concept of distance; then they can compare these thoughts with the definition given below.

We want to abstract the idea of distance because this notion often arises in mathematics. The concept must be general enough to encompass the circumstances under which it arises, but it should conform to the intuitive notion we all have of what is meant by the distance between two points. Because this is being done at the start of the first section, before proceeding it would be profitable for readers to reflect on what properties they think should be included in an abstract concept of distance; then they can compare these thoughts with the definition given below.

## 1.1 Definitions and Examples

Here is the accepted mathematical definition of an abstract distance.

Definition 1.1.1.

A metric space is a pair (X, d) where X is a set and d is a function d: X × X → [0, ∞), called a metric, that satisfies the following properties for all x, y, z in X:

(a) d(x, y) = d(y, x);

(b) d(x, y) = 0 if and only if x = y;

(c) (the triangle inequality) d(x, y) ≤ d(x, z) + d(z, y).

These three properties are usually part of what we intuitively would associate with the idea of distance. Condition (a) is sometimes called the reflexive property and says that the distance from x to y is the same as the distance from y to x. The second property says the obvious: the distance from a point to itself is 0 and the only point at a distance zero from x is x itself. The third, the triangle property, says that the shortest distance between two points is the direct one—not a distance involving a third point. One might be tempted to phrase this by saying that the shortest distance between two points is a straight line, but in this abstract setting we have no concept of a line, let alone a straight one.

It is certainly possible that some readers might have additional properties they would want a distance function to have, but the aforementioned properties are the minimal ones. Indeed, in mathematics there are several particular situations where additional axioms for a distance are assumed; those are more specialized theories, and what we are now going to explore is this basic one. Here are some examples of metric spaces.

Example 1.1.2.

(a)

Let ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq1.gif), the set of real numbers, and define d(x, y) = | x − y | . See Exercise 1.

(b)

Let ![
$$X = {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq2.gif), the plane, and define ![
$$d\(\(x_{1},y_{1}\),\(x_{2},y_{2}\)\) = {\[{\(x_{1} - x_{2}\)}^{2} + {\(y_{1} - y_{2}\)}^{2}\]}^{\\frac{1} {2} }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq3.gif). Readers know from the Pythagorean Theorem that this is the straight-line distance, and they can use geometry to verify that this standard notion of the distance between two points satisfies the axioms in the preceding definition. More generally, we can define a metric on q-dimensional Euclidian space ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq4.gif) by

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(x,y\) ={ \\left \[\\sum _{n=1}^{q}{\(x_{ n} - y_{n}\)}^{2}\\right \]}^{\\frac{1} {2} }}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equa.gif)

for x = (x 1,..., x q ) and y = (y 1,..., y q ) in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq5.gif). However, proving that this satisfies the needed axioms, specifically the triangle inequality, requires some effort, and the proof will be given later (Corollary 1.1.5).

(c)

Let ![
$$X = {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq6.gif), q-dimensional Euclidean space, for x, y in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq7.gif) define

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(x,y\) =\\sum _{ n=1}^{q}\\vert x_{ n} - y_{n}\\vert.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equb.gif)

This is easier to verify as a metric (Exercise 2).

(d)

Again let ![
$$X = {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq8.gif), and now define

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(x,y\) =\\max \\{ \\vert x_{n} - y_{n}\\vert: 1 \\leq n \\leq q\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equc.gif)

Once again, ![
$$\({\\mathbb{R}}^{q},d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq9.gif) is a metric space (Exercise 3). It is worth observing that in each of the last three examples, when q = 1, all these metrics are the standard absolute value on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq10.gif).

(e)

Let X be any set, and define

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(x,y\) = \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\quad &0\\quad \\text{ if }x = y,\\\\ \\quad &1\\quad \\text{ if } x\\neq y.\\end{array} \\right.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equd.gif)

It is a simple exercise to verify that (X, d) is a metric space. This is called the discrete metric on X.

(f)

An important class of examples arises as follows. Suppose (X, d) is given. If Y is a nonempty subset of X, then (Y, d) is a metric space and is referred to as a subspace. As a specific instance of this we can take ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq11.gif) and Y = [0, 1].

Now let us show that the function d given in part (b) of the preceding example is indeed a metric. To do this we need a famous inequality. Before presenting this inequality, we introduce the helpful notation that for vectors x = (x 1,..., x q ) and y = (y 1,..., y q ) in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq12.gif), ![
$$\\langle x,y\\rangle =\\sum _{ n=1}^{q}x_{n}y_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq13.gif). Actually, this is more than just "helpful" notation as it denotes the inner or "dot" product in the vector space ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq14.gif). This connection will not be explored here, however, and we will only regard this as notation. It is useful to observe the following properties for all vectors x, y, z in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq15.gif) and all real numbers t.

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\begin{array}{rl} \\langle x,x\\rangle & \\geq 0,\\\\ \\langle x, y\\rangle & =\\langle y,x\\rangle, \\\\ \\langle tx + z,y\\rangle & = t\\langle x,y\\rangle +\\langle z,y\\rangle, \\\\ \\langle x,y + tz\\rangle & =\\langle x,y\\rangle + t\\langle x,z\\rangle. \\end{array} \\quad \\end{array} \\right. }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ1.gif)

(1.1.3)

Theorem 1.1.4 (Cauchy1–Schwarz2 inequality).

If x = (x 1 ,...,x q ) and y = (y 1 ,...,y q ) are vectors in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq16.gif) , then

![
$$\\displaystyle{{\\left \[\\sum _{n=1}^{q}x_{ n}y_{n}\\right \]}^{2} \\leq \\left \[\\sum _{ n=1}^{q}x_{ n}^{2}\\right \]\\left \[\\sum _{ n=1}^{q}y_{ n}^{2}\\right \].}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Eque.gif)

Proof.

First note that if we use the inner product notation introduced previously, then the sought-after inequality becomes

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\langle x,{y\\rangle }^{2} \\leq \\langle x,x\\rangle \\langle y,y\\rangle.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equf.gif)

Using (1.1.3) we have

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} 0& & \\leq \\langle x - ty,x - ty\\rangle {}\\\\ & =& \\langle x,x\\rangle - t\\langle y,x\\rangle - t\\langle x,y\\rangle + {t}^{2}\\langle y,y\\rangle {}\\\\ & =& \\langle x,x\\rangle - 2t\\langle x,y\\rangle + {t}^{2}\\langle y,y\\rangle {}\\\\ & =& \\gamma -2\\beta t + \\alpha {t}^{2} \\equiv q\(t\), {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ2.gif)

where ![
$$\\gamma =\\langle x,x\\rangle,\\beta =\\langle x,y\\rangle,\\alpha =\\langle y,y\\rangle$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq17.gif). Thus q(t) is a quadratic polynomial in the variable t. Since q(t) ≥ 0 for all t, the graph of q(t) stays above the x-axis, except that it might be tangent at a single point; that is, q(t) = 0 has at most one real root. From the quadratic formula we get that ![
$$0 \\geq {4\\beta }^{2} - 4\\alpha \\gamma = 4\({\\beta }^{2} -\\alpha \\gamma \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq18.gif). Therefore,

![
$$\\displaystyle{0 {\\geq \\beta }^{2} -\\alpha \\gamma =\\langle x,{y\\rangle }^{2} -\\langle x,x\\rangle \\langle y,y\\rangle,}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equg.gif)

proving the inequality. ■

Corollary 1.1.5.

If ![
$$d: {\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\times {\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\rightarrow \[0,\\infty \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq19.gif) is defined as in Example 1.1.2(b) , then d is a metric.

Proof.

We begin by noting that ![
$$d\(x,y\) = \\sqrt{\\langle x - y, x - y\\rangle }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq20.gif). Using the Cauchy–Schwarz inequality (1.1.3) and the vector space properties of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq21.gif) we get

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{lll} d{\(x,y\)}^{2}& =& \\langle x - y,x - y\\rangle {}\\\\ & =& \\langle \(x - z\) + \(z - y\),\(x - z\) + \(z - y\)\\rangle {}\\\\ & =& \\langle x - z,x - z\\rangle + 2\\langle x - z,z - y\\rangle +\\langle z - y,z - y\\rangle {}\\\\ &  \\leq &d{\(x,z\)}^{2} + 2\\sqrt{\\langle x - z, x - z\\rangle }\\sqrt{\\langle z - y, z - y\\rangle } + d{\(z,y\)}^{2} {}\\\\ & =& d{\(x,z\)}^{2} + 2d\(x,z\)d\(z,y\) + d{\(z,y\)}^{2} {}\\\\ & =&{ \\left \[d\(x,z\) + d\(z,y\)\\right \]}^{2}. {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ3.gif)

Taking square roots shows that the triangle inequality holds. The remainder of the proof that d defines a metric is straightforward. (Verify!) ■

When x ∈ X and r > 0, we introduce the notation

![
$$\\displaystyle{B\(x;r\) =\\{ y \\in X: d\(x,y\) < r\\},\\quad \\overline{B}\(x;r\) =\\{ y \\in X: d\(x,y\) \\leq r\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equh.gif)

The set B(x; r) is called the open ball about x, or centered at x, of radius r; ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq22.gif) is called the closed ball about x of radius r. If ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq23.gif), then ![
$$B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq24.gif) is the open interval (x − r, x \+ r) and ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq25.gif) is the closed interval [x − r, x \+ r]. If ![
$$X = {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq26.gif), then B(x; r) is the so-called "open" ball or disk centered at x of radius r that does not include the bounding circle, and ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq27.gif) is the corresponding "closed" disk that does include the bounding circle. The use of the words and here will be made clear momentarily. Meanwhile, notice that when s < r, ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;s\) \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq28.gif). This trivial observation will come in handy.

Definition 1.1.6.

If (X, d) is a metric space, then a subset G of X is open if for each x in G there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq29.gif). A subset F of X is closed if its complement, ![
$$X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq30.gif), is open.

Example 1.1.7.

(a)

We observe that X and ∅ are both open and closed sets. In fact, it is clear that X is open and ∅ is open since the condition is vacuously satisfied. That is, since there are no points in ∅, every point in ∅ satisfies the condition needed for ∅ to be an open set. Since both these sets are open, their complements are closed.

(b)

For any r > 0, B(x; r) is open. If ![
$$y \\in B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq31.gif) and 0 < s < r − d(x, y), then ![
$$B\(y;s\) \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq32.gif). In fact, if d(y, z) < s, then ![
$$d\(x,z\) \\leq d\(x,y\) + d\(y,z\) < d\(x,y\) + s < r$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq33.gif). Thus ![
$$B\(y;s\) \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq34.gif).

(c)

For any r > 0, ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq35.gif) is closed. To see this, set ![
$$G = X\\setminus B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq36.gif) and let y ∈ G; thus, d(y, x) > r. Let 0 < s < d(x, y) − r. If d(z, y) < s, then ![
$$r < d\(x,y\) - s < d\(x,y\) - d\(y,z\) \\leq \[d\(x,z\) + d\(z,y\)\] - d\(y,z\) = d\(x,z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq37.gif); that is, ![
$$B\(y;s\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq38.gif). Since this shows that G is open, it follows that ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq39.gif) is closed.

(d)

Any finite subset of X is closed. In fact, if ![
$$F =\\{ x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq40.gif) and ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq41.gif), then we can find a positive radius r < min{d(x, x 1),..., d(x, x n )} and ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq42.gif).

It is important when discussing open and closed sets to be conscious of the universe. When (X, d) is a metric space and ![
$$Y \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq43.gif), we have that (Y, d) is also a metric space [Example 1.1.2(f)]. To say that we have an open set A in (Y, d) does not mean that A is open in X. Note that in such a circumstance when y ∈ Y and r > 0, the open ball about y of radius r is ![
$$B_{Y }\(y;r\) =\\{ z \\in Y: d\(z,y\) < r\\} = B\(y;r\) \\cap Y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq44.gif). This may not be an open set in X. For example, if ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq45.gif) and Y = [0, 1], then ![
$$\[0, \\frac{1} {2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq46.gif) is open as a subset of Y but not as a subset of X. Another example: ![
$$B_{Y }\(\\frac{1} {4}; \\frac{1} {3}\) = \[0,7/12\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq0047.gif). When we want to emphasize the open and closed sets in the subspace metric (Y, d), we will use the terms open relative to Y or relatively open in Y and closed relative to Y or relatively closed in Y. The proof of the next proposition is Exercise 5.

Proposition 1.1.8.

Let (X,d) be a metric space, and let Y be a subset of X.

(a) A subset G of Y is relatively open in Y if and only if there is an open subset U in X with ![
$$G = U \\cap Y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq47.gif) .

(b) A subset F of Y is relatively closed in Y if and only if there is a closed subset D in X such that ![
$$F = D \\cap Y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq48.gif) .

Before getting into the properties of open and closed sets, let us remark that in establishing part (c) of Example 1.1.7 we essentially derived a useful inequality that we now complete. It is often called the reverse triangle inequality.

Proposition 1.1.9.

If (X,d) is a metric space and x,y,z ∈ X, then

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\vert d\(x,y\) - d\(y,z\)\\vert \\leq d\(x,z\).}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equi.gif)

Proof.

In part (c) of Example 1.1.7 we showed that as a consequence of the triangle inequality, d(x, y) − d(y, z) ≤ d(x, z). Now reverse the roles of x and z in this inequality and we get that d(z, y) − d(y, x) ≤ d(z, x), from which we also have that d(y, z) − d(x, y) ≤ d(x, z). ■

Now we return to an examination of open and closed sets. For the remainder of the chapter (X, d) will be a metric space that is always on our radar screen, even though it may not be explicitly mentioned.

Proposition 1.1.10.

(a) If G 1 ,...,G n are open sets, then ![
$$\\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq49.gif) is open.

(b) If {G i : i ∈ I} is a collection of open sets, then ![
$$\\bigcup _{i\\in I}G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq50.gif) is open.

Proof.

(a) Let ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq51.gif). So for 1 ≤ k ≤ n there is an r k > 0 with ![
$$B\(x,r_{k}\) \\subseteq G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq52.gif). If r = min{r 1,..., r n }, then r > 0 and ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq53.gif).

(b) The proof of this is even easier than the proof of part (a). If ![
$$x \\in \\bigcup _{i\\in I}G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq54.gif), then for some j in I, x ∈ G j ; so there is an open ball B(x; r) contained in G j . But then ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq \\bigcup _{i\\in I}G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq55.gif). ■

By taking complements and using De Morgan's laws (Proposition A.1.5), we get the following proposition for closed sets.

Proposition 1.1.11.

(a) If F 1 ,...,F n are closed sets, then ![
$$\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}F_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq56.gif) is closed.

(b) If {F i : i ∈ I} is a collection of closed sets, then ![
$$\\bigcap _{i\\in I}F_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq57.gif) is closed.

We will see that the open and closed sets in a metric space play a central role. The next result is a prelude to this.

Definition 1.1.12.

Let A be a subset of X. The interior of A, denoted by int A, is the set defined by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{int}\\,A =\\bigcup \\{ G: G\\text{ is open and }G \\subseteq A\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equj.gif)

The closure of A, denoted by cl A, is the set defined by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{cl}\\,A =\\bigcap \\{ F: F\\text{ is a closed and }A \\subseteq F\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equk.gif)

The boundary of A, denoted by ∂ A, is the set defined by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\partial A = \\text{cl}\\,A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,\(X\\setminus A\).}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equl.gif)

Let us note that there is always an open set contained in any set A—namely, the empty set, ∅. It may be, however, that ∅ is the only open set contained in A, in which case ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq58.gif). Similarly, X is a closed set containing any set A; but it may be the only such set, in which case ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A = X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq59.gif). (We will have more to say about this latter case subsequently.) It follows from the preceding two propositions that int A is open (though possibly empty) and cl A is closed (though possibly equal to X). We have that int ∅ = ∅ = cl ∅ and int X = X = cl X. Before looking at more meaningful examples, it would be profitable to first prove some properties of the closure and interior of a set.

Proposition 1.1.13.

Let A ⊆ X.

(a) x ∈int A if and only if there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq60.gif) .

(b) x ∈cl A if and only if for every r > 0, ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq61.gif) .

Proof.

(a)

If ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq62.gif), then since B(x; r) is open, we have that ![
$$B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq63.gif) ⊆ int A; hence x ∈ int A. Now assume that x ∈ int A. So there is an open set G such that x ∈ G ⊆ A. But since G is open, there is a radius r > 0 with ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq64.gif), and we have established the converse.

(b)

Suppose x ∈ cl A. If r > 0, then B(x; r) is open and ![
$$X\\setminus B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq65.gif) is closed. It cannot be that ![
$$A \\subseteq X\\setminus B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq66.gif) since, by definition, this implies ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A \\subseteq X\\setminus B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq67.gif), contradicting the fact that ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq68.gif). Thus ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq69.gif). Now assume that ![
$$x\\notin \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq70.gif); that is, ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq71.gif), an open set. By definition there is a radius r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq72.gif). So for this radius, ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap A = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq73.gif).

■

The preceding proposition is very useful as it provides a concrete, one-point-at-a-time method to determine the closure and the interior of a set. We will see this in the following example.

Example 1.1.14.

(a)

Sometimes things can become weird with interiors and closures. Consider the metric space ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq74.gif) and the subset ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq75.gif) of all rational numbers. We are assuming the reader is familiar with the fact that if a and b are two real numbers with a < b, then there is a rational number x with a < x < b (Axiom A.3.2). Note that this says that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,\\mathbb{Q} = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq76.gif). In fact, if ![
$$x \\in \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq77.gif), then ![
$$B\(x;r\) = \(x - r,x + r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq78.gif), and this interval must contain a rational number. By the preceding proposition, ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq79.gif). We also have that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\\mathbb{Q} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq80.gif). To see this again use the preceding proposition and the fact that between any two real numbers there is an irrational number(Axiom A.3.2). This means that when ![
$$x \\in \\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq81.gif), no open ball B(x; r) can be contained in ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq82.gif) so that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\\mathbb{Q} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq83.gif). Using the same reasoning we see that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,\[\\mathbb{R}\\setminus \\mathbb{Q}\] = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq84.gif) and ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\[\\mathbb{R}\\setminus \\mathbb{Q}\] = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq85.gif). (Verify!) It follows that ![
$$\\partial \\mathbb{Q} = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq86.gif).

(b)

Here is a cautionary tale. Since ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;r\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq87.gif) is closed, we have that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;r\) \\subseteq \\overline{B}\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq88.gif). It may not be, however, that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;r\) = \\overline{B}\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq89.gif). In fact, suppose that ![
$$X =\\{ \(0,0\)\\} \\cup \\{ \(a,b\): {a}^{2} + {b}^{2} = 1\\} \\subseteq {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq90.gif). So X consists of the origin in the plane together with the unit circle centered at the origin. Give X the metric it inherits as a subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq91.gif). In this case, ![
$$\\{\(0,0\)\\} = \\text{cl}\\,B\(\(0,0\);1\)\\neq \\overline{B}\(\(0,0\);1\) = X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq92.gif). It is also true that when X is a discrete metric space as defined in Example 1.1.2(e), then ![
$$B\(x;1\) = \\text{cl}\\,B\(x;1\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq93.gif), whereas ![
$$\\overline{B}\(x;1\) = X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq94.gif).

The next proposition contains some useful information about closures and interiors of sets. Its proof is left as Exercise 7.

Proposition 1.1.15.

Let A be a subset of X.

(a) A is closed if and only if A = cl A.

(b) A is open if and only if A = int A.

(c)  ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A = X\\setminus \[\\text{int}\\,\(X\\setminus A\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq95.gif) , ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A = X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,\(X\\setminus A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq96.gif) , and ![
$$\\partial A = \\text{cl}\\,A\\setminus \\text{int}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq97.gif) .

(d) If A 1 ,...,A n are subsets of X, then ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,\\left \[\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}A_{k}\\right \] =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}\\text{cl}\\,A_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq98.gif) .

Part (d) of the preceding proposition does not hold for the interior. For example, if ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq99.gif), a < b < c, A = (a, b], and B = [b, c), then ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\(A \\cup B\) = \(a,c\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq100.gif), while ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A \\cup \\text{int}\\,B = \(a,b\) \\cup \(b,c\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq101.gif). Also see Exercises 8 and 9.

Finally, we want to examine how we put together metric spaces to obtain new metric spaces. We will see later (Theorem 2.6.6) how to combine a sequence of metric spaces to obtain a new one, but now let us concentrate on putting together a finite number. We will focus on showing how to combine just two spaces because the process for a finite number of spaces is exactly the same but with more complicated notation. Recall that the cartesian product of two sets X 1 and X 2 is defined as ![
$$X_{1} \\times X_{2} =\\{ \(x_{1},x_{2}\): x_{1} \\in X_{1},x_{2} \\in X_{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq102.gif).

Definition 1.1.16.

If (X 1, d 1) and (X 2, d 2) are two metric spaces, then define the new metric space ![
$$\(X_{1} \\times X_{2},d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq103.gif) by letting

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(\(x_{1},x_{2}\),\(y_{1},y_{2}\)\) = d_{1}\(x_{1},y_{1}\) + d_{2}\(x_{2},y_{2}\)}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equm.gif)

for all x 1, y 1 in X 1 and x 2, y 2 in X 2.

Verifying that this does define a metric is Exercise 10. We could define the metric in other ways, for example,

![
$$\\displaystyle{d\(\(x_{1},x_{2}\),\(y_{1},y_{2}\) =\\max \\{ d_{1}\(x_{1},y_{1}\),d_{2}\(x_{2},y_{2}\)\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equn.gif)

We will see later that these and other appropriate definitions yield "equivalent metrics." But that must be postponed until we have a bit more background. Right now we will always use the metric given previously in Definition 1.1.16.

Proposition 1.1.17.

Adopt the previously given notation.

(a)

If G 1 ,G 2 are open sets in X 1 ,X 2 , then G 1 × G 2 is open in X 1 × X 2 .

(b)

If F 1 ,F 2 are closed sets in X 1 ,X 2 , then F 1 × F 2 is closed in X 1 × X 2 .

(c)

If G is open in X 1 × X 2 and (x 1 ,x 2 ) ∈ G, then there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x_{1};r\) \\times B\(x_{2};r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq104.gif) .

Proof.

(a)

If (x 1, x 2) ∈ G 1 × G 2, then for k = 1, 2 let r k > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x_{k};r_{k}\) \\subseteq G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq105.gif). It follows that if r = min{r 1, r 2}, then ![
$$B\(\(x_{1},x_{2}\);r\) \\subseteq G_{1} \\times G_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq106.gif).

(b)

![
$$\(X_{1} \\times X_{2}\)\\setminus \(F_{1} \\times F_{2}\) = \\left \[\(X_{1}\\setminus F_{1}\) \\times X_{2}\\right \] \\cup \\left \[X_{1} \\times \(X_{2}\\setminus F_{2}\)\\right \]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq107.gif), and by (a) this is the union of two open sets.

(c)

Let ε > 0 such that ![
$$B\(\(x_{1},x_{2}\);\\epsilon \) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq108.gif). If 0 < r < ε∕2, then this works.

■

We close this section with a topic we will encounter in several places as we proceed through the book.

Definition 1.1.18.

A subset E of a metric space (X, d) is dense if ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,E = X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq109.gif). A metric space (X, d) is separable if it has a countable dense subset.

We made reference to this concept just after defining the closure of a set. So a set E is dense if and only if X is the only closed subset of X that contains E.

Example 1.1.19.

(a)

Every metric space is dense in itself.

(b)

The rational numbers form a dense subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq110.gif), as do the irrational numbers. This is a rephrasing of Example 1.1.14(a). This might also explain the name given to Axiom A.3.2. We note that this implies that ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq111.gif) is separable since ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq112.gif) is countable (Corollary A.5.5).

(c)

The set of all points in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq113.gif) with rational coordinates is dense in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq114.gif). This follows from the preceding example, and it also says that ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq115.gif) is separable by Proposition A.5.4. Also see Exercise 13.

(d)

If X is any set and d is a discrete metric on X [Example 1.1.2(e)], then the only dense subset of X is X itself. In fact, if E is a dense subset of (X, d) and x ∈ X, then it must be that ![
$$B\(x;1/2\) \\cap E\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq116.gif); but from the definition of the discrete metric it follows that B(x; 1∕2) = { x}.

In part (d) of the preceding example we used the next result, and we record it here for future reference.

Proposition 1.1.20.

A set E is dense in (X,d) if and only if for every x in X and every r > 0, ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap E\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq117.gif) .

The proof is an easy application of Proposition 1.1.13.

## Exercises

(1)

Verify the statement in Example 1.1.2(a).

(2)

Verify the statement in Example 1.1.2(c).

(3)

Verify the statement in Example 1.1.2(d).

(4)

In the Cauchy–Schwarz inequality, show that equality holds if and only if the vectors x and y are linearly dependent.

(5)

Prove Proposition 1.1.8. [Hint: if G is a relatively open subset of Y, then for each y in G let r y > 0 such that B Y (y; r y ) ⊆ G. Now consider ![
$$\\bigcup \\{B\(y;r_{y}\): y \\in G\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq118.gif).]

(6)

If Y is a subset of X, then consider the metric space (Y, d) and suppose ![
$$Z \\subseteq Y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq119.gif). (a) Show that H is a relatively open subset of Z if and only if there is a relatively open subset H 1 of Y such that ![
$$H = H_{1} \\cap Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq120.gif). (b) Show that D is a relatively closed subset of Z if and only if there is a relatively closed subset D 1 of Y such that ![
$$D = D_{1} \\cap Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq121.gif). (Hint: use Proposition 1.1.8.)

(7)

Prove Proposition 1.1.15.

(8)

Show that if A 1,..., A n are subsets of X, then ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\\left \[\\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}A_{k}\\right \] =\\bigcap _{ k=1}^{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq122.gif) int A k .

(9)

Show that Proposition 1.1.15(d) does not hold for infinite unions and the preceding exercise does not hold for infinite intersections.

(10)

Verify that the function d given in Definition 1.1.16 is a metric.

(11)

Define ρ: X 1 × X 2 → [0, ∞) by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\rho \(\(x_{1},x_{2}\),\(y_{1},y_{2}\)\) =\\max \\{ d_{1}\(x_{1},y_{1}\),d_{2}\(x_{2},y_{2}\)\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equo.gif)

(a)

Show that ρ is a metric on X 1 × X 2. (b) Show that a set is open in (X 1 × X 2, d) if and only if it is open in (X 1 × X 2, ρ).

(12)

Let ℓ ∞ denote the set of all bounded sequences of real numbers; that is, ℓ ∞ consists of all sequences {x n } such that ![
$$x_{n} \\in \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq123.gif) for all n ≥ 1 and sup n | x n | < ∞. If ![
$$x =\\{ x_{n}\\},y =\\{ y_{n}\\} {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq124.gif), then define ![
$$d\(x,y\) =\\sup _{n}\\vert x_{n} - y_{n}\\vert $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq125.gif). (a) Show that d defines a metric on ℓ ∞ . (b) If e n denotes a sequence with a 1 in the nth place and zeroes elsewhere, show that ![
$$B\(e_{n}; \\frac{1} {2}\) \\cap B\(e_{m}; \\frac{1} {2}\) = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq126.gif) when n ≠ m. (c) Is the set {e n : n ≥ 1} closed?

(13)

If for k = 1, 2, A k is a dense subset of the metric space (X k , d k ), then show that A 1 × A 2 is a dense subset of X 1 × X 2. Hence the product of a finite number of separable spaces is separable.

## 1.2 Sequences and Completeness

Recall that throughout the chapter (X, d) is a given metric space that is under consideration. Here we will discuss sequences as an extension of the same concept encountered in calculus. Recall that a sequence is just a way of enumerating some points in the space X: x 1, x 2,...; this is denoted by {x n }. Precisely or technically this is a function from the natural numbers ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq127.gif) into X: n↦x n . By the way, we will sometimes change the domain of this function to ![
$$\\mathbb{N} \\cup \\{ 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq128.gif) so that we get a sequence ![
$$\\{x_{0},x_{1},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq129.gif); or maybe it might be changed to get {x 2, x 3,...}. There is no essential difference; we have a specific beginning and a countably infinite following. So the set of all integers, ![
$$\\mathbb{Z}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq130.gif), is not permitted as an indexing set.

Definition 1.2.1.

A sequence {x n } in X converges to x if for every ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq131.gif) there is an integer N such that d(x, x n ) < ε when n ≥ N. The notation for this is ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq132.gif) or x = lim n x n .

We note that when ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq133.gif), this is exactly the definition of a convergent sequence learned in calculus. We also emphasize that the integer N in the definition depends on ε. Generally, the smaller the value of ε, the larger we must make N (Exercise 1). We might also mention that the inequality d(x, x n ) < ε can easily be replaced by d(x n , x) ≤ ε. Why?

Example 1.2.2.

(a)

If (X, d) is a discrete metric space, then a sequence {x n } in X converges to x if and only if there is an integer N such that x n = x whenever n ≥ N.

(b)

If (X, d) is the cartesian product of the two metric spaces (X 1, d 1) and (X 2, d 2), then a sequence ![
$$\\{\(x_{n}^{1},x_{n}^{2}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq134.gif) in X converges to ![
$$\({x}^{1},{x}^{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq135.gif) if and only if x n 1 → x 1 and ![
$$x_{n}^{2} \\rightarrow {x}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq136.gif).

The value of sequences and the concept of convergence begins to surface in the next proposition.

Proposition 1.2.3.

A subset F of X is closed if and only if whenever {x n } is a sequence in F and x n → x, it follows that x ∈ F.

Proof.

First assume that F is closed, {x n } is a sequence of elements in F, and ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq137.gif). If it were the case that x∉F, then the fact X∖F is open would mean there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq138.gif). But then there would be an N such that for n ≥ N, d(x n , x) < r, that is, ![
$$x_{n} \\in B\(x;r\) \\subseteq X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq139.gif), a contradiction. Hence it must be that x ∈ F. Now assume the sequential condition is satisfied. If ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq140.gif), then Proposition 1.1.13 implies that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap F\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq141.gif) for every r > 0. In particular, for every natural number n there is a point x n in ![
$$B\(x;{n}^{-1}\) \\cap F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq142.gif). Hence {x n } is a sequence in F and d(x n , x) < n −1; thus ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq143.gif), and so x ∈ F. That is, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,F \\subseteq F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq144.gif), and so F is closed. ■

Definition 1.2.4.

If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq145.gif), then a point x in X is called a limit point of A if for every ε > 0 there is a point a in ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\cap A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq146.gif) with a ≠ x.

The emphasis here is that no matter how small we take ε, we can find such a point a different from x that belongs to ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\cap A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq147.gif). It is not required that x must belong to A for it to be a limit point (more on that later). If x is not a limit point of A and, in addition, belongs to A, then it is called an isolated point of A.

Example 1.2.5.

(a)

Let ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq148.gif) and ![
$$A = \(0,1\) \\cup \\{ 2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq149.gif). Every point in [0, 1] is a limit point of A, but 2 is not. In fact, 2 is an example of an isolated point of A, the only isolated point of A.

(b)

If ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq150.gif) and ![
$$A = \\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq151.gif), then every point of X is a limit point of A and A has no isolated points.

(c)

If X = ℝ and A = { n −1: n ∈ ℕ}, then 0 is a limit point of A, while the points n −1 are all isolated points.

We will also need the idea of a subsequence as in calculus. So if {x n } is a sequence in X, then a subsequence is another sequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{1}},x_{n_{2}},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq152.gif), denoted by ![
$$\\{x_{n_k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq00152.gif), where n 1 < n 2 < ⋯ . Technically, this is the function ![
$$k\\mapsto x_{n_k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq001152.gif). We leave the proof of the following proposition as Exercise 6.

Proposition 1.2.6.

If ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq153.gif) in X and ![
$$\\{x_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq154.gif) is a subsequence, then ![
$$x_{n_{k}}\\,\\rightarrow \\,x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq155.gif) .

Proposition 1.2.7.

Let A be a subset of the metric space X.

(a) A point x is a limit point of A if and only if there is a sequence of distinct points in A that converges to x.

(b) A is a closed set if and only if it contains all its limit points.

(c)  ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A = A \\cup \\{ x: x\\text{ is a limit point of }A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq156.gif) .

Proof.

(a) Suppose {a n } is a sequence of distinct points in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq157.gif). If ε > 0, then there is an N such that ![
$$a_{n} \\in B\(x;\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq158.gif) for n ≥ N. Since the points in {a n } are distinct, there is at least one point different from x. Thus x is a limit point. Now assume that x is a limit point. Let ![
$$a_{1} \\in A \\cap B\(x;1\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq159.gif) such that a 1 ≠ x. Let ![
$$\\epsilon _{2} =\\min \\{ {2}^{-1},d\(x,a_{1}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq160.gif); so there is a point ![
$$a_{2} \\in A \\cap B\(x;\\epsilon _{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq161.gif) with a 2 ≠ x. Note that a 2 ≠ a 1.

Claim.

There is a sequence of positive numbers ![
$$\\{\\epsilon _{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq162.gif) and a sequence of distinct points {a n } in A such that: (i) ε n ≤ n −1; (ii) a n ≠ x for all n ≥ 1; (iii) d(x, a n ) < ε n .

We establish this claim by induction. By taking ε 1 = 1, we have that the claim holds when n = 1. We also showed that the claim holds when n = 2, and that indicates how to carry out the induction step. Here are the details. Assume the claim holds for some integer n, and let ε n+1 = min{(n \+ 1)−1, d(x, a 1),..., d(x, a n )}. So there is a point a n+1 in ![
$$A \\cap B\(x;\\epsilon _{n+1}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq163.gif) with a n+1 ≠ x. Note that parts (i), (ii), and (iii) are satisfied with n \+ 1 in place of n. Also, a n+1 ≠ a k for 1 ≤ k ≤ n since ![
$$d\(x,a_{n+1}\) < \\epsilon _{n+1} \\leq d\(x,a_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq164.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n. By induction we have that the claim is valid; by the claim we have a sequence of distinct points in A that converges to x.

(b) and (c). Clearly (b) will follow once we prove (c). Let B denote the set on the right-hand side of the equation in (c). By Proposition 1.2.3 and part (a) we have that ![
$$B \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq165.gif). On the other hand, if ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq166.gif), then Proposition 1.2.3 implies there is a sequence {a n } in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq167.gif). Either {a n } has an infinite number of distinct terms or a finite number. In the first case there is a subsequence ![
$$\\{a_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq168.gif) of distinct terms; by (a), x is a limit point. Thus x ∈ A. In the second case there is a subsequence ![
$$\\{a_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq169.gif) that is constant; thus ![
$$a_{n_{k}} = x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq170.gif) for all k ≥ 1, and so x ∈ A. ■

Definition 1.2.8.

If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq171.gif) and x ∈ X, then the distance from x to A is

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) =\\inf \\{ d\(x,a\): a \\in A\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equp.gif)

Clearly, when x ∈ A, ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq172.gif). But it is possible for the distance from a point to a set to be 0 when the point is not in the set, as we now see.

Proposition 1.2.9.

If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq173.gif) , then ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A =\\{ x \\in X: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) = 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq174.gif) .

Proof.

If ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq175.gif), then there is a sequence {a n } in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq176.gif); so ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,a_{n}\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq177.gif), and it follows that ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq178.gif). Conversely, if ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq179.gif), then there is a sequence {a n } in A such that ![
$$d\(x,a_{n}\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq180.gif). Thus, ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq181.gif), and so ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq182.gif). ■

Also see Exercise 4.

Definition 1.2.10.

A sequence {x n } in X is a Cauchy sequence if for every ε > 0 there is an integer N such that d(x n , x m ) < ε whenever m, n ≥ N. The metric space X is said to be complete if every Cauchy sequence converges.

It is rather easy to see that in any metric space, every convergent sequence is a Cauchy sequence. In fact, if ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq183.gif) and ε > 0, then choose N such that d(x, x n ) < ε∕2. Hence, when m, n ≥ N, ![
$$d\(x_{n},x_{m}\) \\leq d\(x_{n},x\) + d\(x,x_{m}\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq184.gif). Therefore, complete metric spaces are those for which convergent and Cauchy sequences are the same. The virtue of the concept of being Cauchy, when the space is complete, is that you know the limit exists without having to produce the point x that is the limit.

Before we see some examples, let us establish some facts about Cauchy sequences.

Proposition 1.2.11.

If {x n } is a Cauchy sequence and some subsequence of {x n } converges to x, then ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq185.gif) .

Proof.

Suppose ![
$$x_{n_{k}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq186.gif), and let ε > 0. Choose an integer N 1 such that ![
$$d\(x_{n_{k}},x\) < \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq187.gif) for n k ≥ N 1, and choose an integer N 2 such that ![
$$d\(x_{n},x_{m}\) < \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq188.gif) when m, n ≥ N 2. Set ![
$$N =\\max \\{ N_{1},N_{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq189.gif), and let n ≥ N. Fix any n k ≥ N. Since we have that n k ≥ N 1 and both n and n k are larger than N 2, we get that ![
$$d\(x,x_{n}\) \\leq d\(x,x_{n_{k}}\) + d\(x_{n_{k}},x_{n}\) < \\epsilon /2 + \\epsilon /2 = \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq190.gif). ■

For any set E define its diameter as

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{diam}\\,E =\\sup \\{ d\(x,y\): x,y \\in E\\}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equq.gif)

It is easy to see that diam E = diam [cl E].

Theorem 1.2.12 (Cantor's3 Theorem).

A metric space (X,d) is complete if and only if whenever {F n } is a sequence of nonempty subsets satisfying (i) each F n is closed; (ii) ![
$$F_{1} \\supseteq F_{2} \\supseteq \\cdots $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq193.gif); (iii) ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,F_{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq194.gif) , then ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq195.gif) is a single point.

Proof.

Assume (X, d) is complete and {F n } is as in the statement of the theorem. For each n let x n ∈ F n . If ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq196.gif), then let N be such that ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,F_{n} < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq197.gif) for n ≥ N. Thus, if m, n ≥ N, then (ii) implies ![
$$x_{n},x_{m} \\in F_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq198.gif) and so ![
$$d\(x_{n},x_{m}\) \\leq \\text{diam}\\,F_{N} < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq199.gif). Thus {x n } is a Cauchy sequence; since (X, d) is complete, there is an x in X such that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq200.gif). Since each F n is closed, ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq201.gif). If there is another point y in ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq202.gif), then ![
$$d\(x,y\) \\leq \\text{diam}\\,F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq203.gif) for each n ≥ 1. By (iii), y = x.

Now assume that (X, d) satisfies the stated conditions and ![
$$\\{x_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq204.gif) is a Cauchy sequence. Set ![
$$F_{n} = \\text{cl}\\,\\{x_{n},x_{n+1},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq205.gif). Clearly, (i) and (ii) are satisfied. If ε > 0, then let N be such that d(x n , x m ) < ε for m, n ≥ N. But for k ≥ N, ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,F_{k} =\\sup \\{ d\(x_{n},x_{m}\): m,n \\geq k\\} \\leq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq206.gif). Thus {F n } satisfies the three conditions, so that ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} =\\{ x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq207.gif) for some point x. But for any n ≥ 1, ![
$$d\(x,x_{n}\) \\leq \\text{diam}\\,F_{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq208.gif). Therefore, ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq209.gif), and (X, d) is complete. ■

Example 1.2.13.

(a)

The first example of a complete metric space is ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq210.gif). This is a consequence of various properties of the real number system seen in § A.3 as follows. Let {F n } be a sequence of nonempty subsets of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq211.gif) satisfying the three conditions in Cantor's Theorem. Since each has finite diameter, they must be bounded. By the Completeness Property (Axiom A.3.3) of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq212.gif), ![
$$a_{n} =\\inf F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq213.gif) and b n = supF n exist. Since F n is closed, Corollary A.3.5 implies that a n , b n ∈ F n and so ![
$$0 \\leq b_{n} - a_{n} \\leq \\text{diam}\\,F_{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq214.gif). Since ![
$$F_{n+1} \\subseteq F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq215.gif), it follows that ![
$$a_{n} \\leq a_{n+1} \\leq b_{n+1} \\leq b_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq216.gif). By Proposition A.3.6 there are points ![
$$a$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq217.gif) and b such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow a$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq218.gif) and ![
$$b_{n} \\rightarrow b$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq219.gif). But it must be that a, b ∈ F n for each n and so ![
$$\\vert b - a\\vert \\leq \\text{diam}\\,F_{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq220.gif); so a = b. That is, ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq221.gif) is the singleton {a}. By Cantor's Theorem, ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq222.gif) is complete.

(b)

For any d ≥ 1, ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq223.gif) is complete. In fact if ![
$$x_{n} = \(x_{n}^{1},\\ldots,x_{n}^{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq224.gif) and {x n } is a Cauchy sequence in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq225.gif), then ![
$$\\vert x_{n}^{k} - x_{m}^{k}\\vert \\leq d\(x_{n},x_{m}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq226.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ q and so it follows that each ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq227.gif) is a Cauchy sequence in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq228.gif). By (a), ![
$$x_{n}^{k} \\rightarrow {x}^{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq229.gif) for some real number x k . It follows that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x = \({x}^{1},\\ldots,{x}^{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq230.gif). (Verify!)

The proof of the next proposition is Exercise 8.

Proposition 1.2.14.

If (X,d) is a complete metric space and ![
$$Y \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq231.gif) , then (Y,d) is complete if and only if Y is closed in X.

It is not hard to find examples of metric spaces that are not complete. For example using the preceding proposition we can look at any subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq232.gif) that is not closed. ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq233.gif) is one such and a dramatic one at that.

We close this section by dwelling a bit on the concept of the diameter of a set, introduced just prior to the statement of Cantor's Theorem.

Definition 1.2.15.

Say that a subset A of (X, d) is bounded if ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,A < \\infty $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq234.gif).

Proposition 1.2.16.

(a) A subset A of (X,d) is bounded if and only if for any x in X there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$A \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq235.gif) .

(b) The union of a finite number of bounded sets is bounded.

(c) A Cauchy sequence in (X,d) is a bounded set.

Proof.

(a)

If ![
$$A \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq236.gif), then ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,A \\leq 2r$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq237.gif), so that A is bounded. Conversely, assume that A is bounded with δ as its finite diameter. Fix a point x in X and some point a 0 in A. For any point a in A, ![
$$d\(x,a\) \\leq d\(x,a_{0}\) + d\(a_{0},a\) \\leq d\(x,a_{0}\) + \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq238.gif). If we let ![
$$r = 2\[d\(a_{0},x\) + \\delta \]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq239.gif), then ![
$$A \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq240.gif).

(b)

If A k is bounded for 1 ≤ k ≤ n and x ∈ X, then let r k > 0 such that ![
$$A_{k} \\subseteq B\(x;r_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq241.gif). If we set r = r 1 \+ ⋯ + r n , then ![
$$A_{1} \\cup \\cdots \\cup A_{n} \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq242.gif).

(c)

If {x n } is a Cauchy sequence, then there is an N ≥ 1 such that d(x n , x m ) < 1 for m, n ≥ N. If B = { x n : n ≥ N}, this says that ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,B \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq243.gif), so that B is bounded. On the other hand, A = { x 1,..., x N } is bounded since finite sets are bounded. By part (b), ![
$$\\{x_{n}\\} = A \\cup B$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq244.gif) is bounded.

■

## Exercises

(1)

For each of the following sequences {x n } find the value of the limit, and for each stipulated value of ε find a value of N such that d(x, x n ) < ε when n ≥ N. (a) ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R},x_{n} = {n}^{-1},\\epsilon = 0.0001$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq245.gif). (b) ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R},x_{n} = {e}^{-n},\\epsilon = 0.0001$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq246.gif). (c) ![
$$X = {\\mathbb{R}}^{q+1},x_{n} = \({n}^{-1},{n}^{-2},\\ldots,{n}^{-q},{e}^{-n}\),\\epsilon = 0.0001$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq247.gif). (Are the values for N you found the smallest possible? This has no bearing on the convergence, but it is a bit more challenging to find the smallest possible N.)

(2)

Verify the statements made in Example 1.2.2.

(3)

Suppose {x n } is a sequence in X that converges to x and z 1,..., z m is a finite collection of points in X. Define a new sequence {y n } in X by letting y k = z k for 1 ≤ k ≤ m and y k = x k−m when k ≥ m \+ 1. Show that ![
$$y_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq248.gif).

(4)

If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq249.gif), show that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A =\\{ x: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,X\\setminus A\) > 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq250.gif). Can you give an analogous characterization of ![
$$\\partial A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq251.gif)?

(5)

(a) If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq252.gif), show that ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq253.gif) if and only if x is either a limit point of A or an isolated point of A. (b) Show that if a set has no limit points, it is closed. (c) Give an example of an infinite subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq254.gif) that has no limit points.

(6)

Prove Proposition 1.2.6.

(7)

For the real line, consider the three restrictions (i), (ii), and (iii) placed on the sets {F n } in Cantor's Theorem. (a) Find a sequence of sets {F n } that satisfies (i) and (ii), but ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq255.gif). (b) Find a sequence of sets {F n } that satisfies (i) and (iii), but ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq256.gif). (c) Find a sequence of sets {F n } that satisfies (ii) and (iii), but ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq257.gif). (d) Show that if {F n } is a sequence of bounded sets satisfying (i) and (ii), then ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq258.gif), and give an example where it is not a singleton.

(8)

Prove Proposition 1.2.14.

(9)

Show that a sequence ![
$$\\{\(x_{1}^{n},x_{2}^{n}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq259.gif) in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\times {\\mathbb{R}}^{m}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq260.gif) converges to (x 1, x 2) if and only if the same thing happens when we consider the sequence as belonging to ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q+m}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq261.gif).

(10)

Let (X, d) be the cartesian product of the two metric spaces (X 1, d 1) and (X 2, d 2). (a) Show that a sequence ![
$$\\{\(x_{n}^{1},x_{n}^{2}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq262.gif) in X is a Cauchy sequence in X if and only if ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq263.gif) is a Cauchy sequence in X 1 and ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq264.gif) is a Cauchy sequence in X 2. (b) Show that X is complete if and only if both X 1 and X 2 are complete.

(11)

Show that the metric space ℓ ∞ defined in Exercise 1.1.12 is complete.

## 1.3 Continuity

Here we will extend the concept of a continuous function seen in calculus to a mapping between two metric spaces.

Definition 1.3.1.

If (X, d) and (Z, ρ) are two metric spaces, a function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq265.gif) is continuous at a point a in X if for every ε > 0 there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq266.gif) such that when ![
$$d\(a,x\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq267.gif), it follows that ![
$$\\rho \(f\(a\),f\(x\)\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq268.gif). f is said to be a continuous function if it is continuous at each point of X.

Note that if ![
$$X = Z = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq269.gif), then this becomes the statement that for every ε > 0 there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq270.gif) such that when ![
$$\\vert x - a\\vert < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq271.gif), we have that ![
$$\\vert f\(a\) - f\(x\)\\vert < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq272.gif), the precise definition from calculus. The next result should have a familiar ring if we put it in this calculus setting.

Proposition 1.3.2.

If (X,d) and (Z,ρ) are metric spaces and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq273.gif) , then f is continuous at a if and only if whenever {x n } is a sequence in X and ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow a$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq274.gif) , then ![
$$f\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow f\(a\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq275.gif) in Z.

Proof.

Suppose f is continuous at a and ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow a$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq276.gif) in X. If ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq277.gif), then let ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq278.gif) such that when ![
$$d\(a,x\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq279.gif), it follows that ρ(f(a), f(x)) < ε. Let N ≥ 1 such that ![
$$d\(x_{n},a\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq280.gif) when n ≥ N. Thus, ρ(f(x n ), f(a)) < ε when n ≥ N. Since ε was arbitrary, this says that ![
$$f\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow f\(a\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq281.gif). To prove the converse, assume that f is not continuous at a. So there exists an ε > 0 such that for every ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq282.gif) there is at least one x with ![
$$d\(x,a\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq283.gif), but ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(a\)\) \\geq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq284.gif). In particular, taking ![
$$\\delta = {n}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq285.gif) we have that for every n ≥ 1 there is an x n with ![
$$d\(x_{n},a\) < {n}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq286.gif) and ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x_{n}\),f\(a\)\) \\geq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq287.gif). But this says that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow a$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq288.gif), and {f(x n )} does not converge to f(a). ■

We will not spend much time investigating functions continuous at a single point, but we will have much to say about functions continuous on the entire metric space.

Theorem 1.3.3.

If (X,d) and (Z,ρ) are metric spaces and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq289.gif) , then the following statements are equivalent.

(a) f is a continuous function on X.

(b) If U is an open subset of Z, then f −1 (U) is an open subset of X.

(c) If D is a closed subset of Z, then f −1 (D) is a closed subset of X.

Proof.

(b) is equivalent to (c). Note that

![
$$\\displaystyle{{f}^{-1}\(Z\\setminus U\) = X\\setminus {f}^{-1}\(U\)\\text{ and }{f}^{-1}\(Z\\setminus D\) = X\\setminus {f}^{-1}\(D\).}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equr.gif)

From these equalities the equivalence of the two statements is straightforward.

(a) implies (b). Let a ∈ f −1(U) so that ![
$$\\alpha = f\(a\) \\in U$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq290.gif). Since U is open, there is an ε > 0 such that ![
$$B\(\\alpha;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq291.gif). Since f is continuous there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq292.gif) such that ![
$$d\(a,x\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq293.gif) implies ![
$$\\rho \(f\(a\),f\(x\)\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq294.gif). In other words, ![
$$B\(a;\\delta \) \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(B\(\\alpha;\\epsilon \)\) \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(U\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq295.gif). Since a was an arbitrary point in f −1(U), this says that f −1(U) is open.

(b) implies (a). If a ∈ X and ε > 0, then B(f(a); ε) is open; so by (b) we have that f −1(B(f(a); ε)) is an open set in X that contains a. Thus there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq296.gif) such that ![
$$B\(a;\\delta \) \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(B\(f\(a\);\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq297.gif). That is, ![
$$d\(a,x\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq298.gif) implies ρ(f(a), f(x)) < ε, and so f is continuous at a. ■

Now to generate some examples of continuous functions. We will assume that the reader is already familiar with the continuity of the various functions encountered in calculus, such as the trigonometric functions, the exponential, and the logarithm, as well as various functions defined on subsets of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq299.gif). The first result is in this general area of functions defined on ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq300.gif). (See Exercise 1.2.9.)

Example 1.3.4.

(a)

The function from ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\times {\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\rightarrow {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq301.gif) defined by (x, y)↦x \+ y (vector addition) is continuous. Verifying this is easy if we use Proposition 1.3.2 and Exercise 1.2.10.

(b)

The function from ![
$$\\mathbb{R} \\times {\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\rightarrow {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq302.gif) defined by (t, x)↦tx (scalar multiplication) is continuous.

(c)

If (X, d) is any metric space, then x 0 ∈ X, and we define ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq303.gif) by f(x) = d(x, x 0), then f is continuous. In fact, the reverse triangle inequality (Proposition 1.1.9) says that ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(y\)\\vert \\leq d\(x,y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq304.gif), from which continuity follows from either the definition or Proposition 1.3.2.

(d)

If (X, d) is a discrete metric space, then the only continuous functions from [0, 1] into (X, d) are the constant functions.

Recall the definition of the distance from a point to a set A, ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq305.gif), given in the last section.

Proposition 1.3.5.

If (X,d) is a metric space and ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq306.gif) , then

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\vert \\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) -\\text{dist}\\,\(y,A\)\\vert \\leq d\(x,y\)}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equs.gif)

for all x,y in X.

Proof.

If a ∈ A, then ![
$$d\(x,a\) \\leq d\(x,y\) + d\(y,a\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq307.gif); thus, taking the infimum over all a in A we get ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) \\leq \\inf \\{ d\(x,y\) + d\(y,a\): a \\in A\\} = d\(x,y\) + \\text{dist}\\,\(y,A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq308.gif). Reversing the roles of x and y we have ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(y,A\) \\leq d\(x,y\) + \\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq309.gif), whence we get the inequality. ■

Corollary 1.3.6.

If A is a nonempty subset of X, then ![
$$f: X:\\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq310.gif) defined by ![
$$f\(x\) = \\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq311.gif) is a continuous function.

We also have the following result, possibly expected by the reader, whose proof follows easily by using Proposition 1.3.2.

Proposition 1.3.7.

If (X,d) is a metric space and f and g are continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq312.gif) , then ![
$$f + g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq313.gif) and ![
$$fg: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq314.gif) are continuous, where (f + g)(x) = f(x) + g(x) and (fg)(x) = f(x)g(x) for all x in X. If f(x) ≠ 0 for all x in X, then ![
$${f}^{-1} = 1/f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq315.gif) defined by ![
$${f}^{-1}\(x\) = 1/f\(x\) = {\[f\(x\)\]}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq316.gif) is a continuous function.

The last proposition is a way to combine continuous functions to obtain another continuous function. Here is another. Recall that if ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq317.gif) and ![
$$g: Z \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq318.gif) are functions, then the composition of f and g is the function ![
$$g \\circ f: X \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq319.gif) defined by g ∘ f(x) = g(f(x)).

Proposition 1.3.8.

The composition of two continuous functions is also continuous.

Proof.

If ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq320.gif) and ![
$$g: Z \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq321.gif), then for any subset G of W, then we have that ![
$${\(g \\circ f\)}^{-1}\(G\) = {f}^{-1}\[{g}^{-1}\(G\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq322.gif). Thus, if G is an open subset of W, it follows that (g ∘ f)−1(G) is open in X. Hence g ∘ f is continuous. ■

Later in the book we will present several results on manufacturing continuous functions from a metric space into the real numbers. We have seen examples of such continuous functions on specific metric spaces, but we want to show the existence of continuous functions with specific properties on arbitrary ones. Here we use Corollary 1.3.6 to prove a famous result that we will prove later in a more general context.

Theorem 1.3.9 (Urysohn's4 Lemma).

If A and B are two disjoint closed subsets of X, then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq323.gif) having the following properties:

(a) 0 ≤ f(x) ≤ 1 for all x in X:

(b) f(x) = 0 for all x in A:

(c) f(x) = 1 for all x in B.

Proof.

Define ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq324.gif) by

![
$$\\displaystyle{f\(x\) = \\frac{\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\)} {\\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\) + \\text{dist}\\,\(x,B\)},}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equt.gif)

which is well defined since the denominator never vanishes. (Why?) It is easy to check that f has the desired properties. ■

Corollary 1.3.10.

If F is a closed subset of X and G is an open set containing F, then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq325.gif) such that 0 ≤ f(x) ≤ 1 for all x in X, f(x) = 1 when x ∈ F, and f(x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq326.gif) .

Proof.

In Urysohn's Lemma, take A to be the complement of G and B = F. ■

Recall that a map ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq327.gif) is injective if it is one-to-one; that is, if f(x) = f(y), then x = y. The function is surjective if it is onto; that is, for any z in Z there is a point x in X with f(x) = z. If f is both injective and surjective, then it is said to be bijective. When f is bijective, we can define the function ![
$${f}^{-1}: Z \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq328.gif) by letting f −1(z) equal the unique point x in X such that f(x) = z. The reader may have noticed the possibility of confusing notation with this definition of f −1 and that in Proposition 1.3.7. I am afraid this is something we will have to live with. Usually, the context will make it clear which definition we are talking about. We are coming into contact with mathematical tradition or custom, and we will not fight it. If there is ever the possibility of confusion, we can always stick with the notation 1∕f to denote the reciprocal of a function.

Definition 1.3.11.

If (X, d) and (Z, ρ) are metric spaces, then a map ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq329.gif) is called a homeomorphism if f is bijective and both f and f −1 are continuous. Two metric spaces are said to be homeomorphic if there is a homeomorphism from one onto the other.

Note that a bijection ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq330.gif) is a homeomorphism precisely when a sequence {x n } in X converges to x if and only if ![
$$f\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq331.gif). A homeomorphism identifies the two spaces. That is, via f they have the same convergent sequences, open sets, closed sets, and continuous functions. In fact, homeomorphisms define an equivalence relation (Definition 2.8.1) between metric spaces. To be sure, it is not the only possible equivalence relation, and perhaps the idea of an isometry, a map ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq332.gif) satisfying ρ(f(x), f(y)) = d(x, y) for all x, y in X, seems more natural. Indeed, isometries identify the two structures, and it readily follows that an isometry is a homeomorphism. Nevertheless, we will emphasize homeomorphisms rather than isometries. While an isometry identifies the metric structures of two metric spaces, a homeomorphism identifies the two topological structures. That is, it identifies the open sets, closed sets, etc. This is more in keeping with the subject of this book.

Definition 1.3.12.

If X is a set, then the two metrics d and ρ are said to be equivalent if they define the same convergent sequences. Equivalently, d and ρ are equivalent if the identity map ![
$$i: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(X,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq333.gif) is a homeomorphism.

Exercise 1.1.11 shows that the metric ρ defined there is equivalent to the usual metric.

Proposition 1.3.13.

For any metric space ![
$$\(X,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq334.gif)

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\rho \(x,y\) = \\frac{d\(x,y\)} {1 + d\(x,y\)}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equu.gif)

defines an equivalent metric.

Proof.

The first step is to show that ρ is a metric, and the only point of contention is whether it satisfies the triangle inequality. This we do by an examination of the function f(t) = t∕(1 + t) defined on (−1, ∞). Note that for t > −1, f ′ (t) = (1 + t)−2 > 0 and f ′ ′ (t) = −2(1 + t)−3 < 0. Thus, for s, t > 0 and g(t) = f(s) + f(t) − f(s \+ t), ![
$${g}^{{\\prime}}\(t\) = {f}^{{\\prime}}\(t\) - {f}^{{\\prime}}\(s + t\) > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq335.gif). So g(t) > g(0) = 0. This says that ![
$$f\(s + t\) \\leq f\(s\) + f\(t\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq336.gif) for all s, t in [0, ∞). Since f is increasing, for all x, y, z in X,

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\rho \(x,y\)& =& \\frac{d\(x,y\)} {1 + d\(x,y\)} {}\\\\ & \\leq & \\frac{d\(x,z\) + d\(z,y\)} {1 + d\(x,z\) + d\(z,y\)} {}\\\\ & =& f\[d\(x,z\) + d\(z,y\)\] {}\\\\ & \\leq & \\frac{d\(x,z\)} {1 + d\(x,z\)} + \\frac{d\(z,y\)} {1 + d\(z,y\)} {}\\\\ & =& \\rho \(x,z\) +\\rho \(z,y\), {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ4.gif)

so that ρ satisfies the triangle inequality.

Now to show that the two metrics are equivalent. Clearly, if ![
$$d\(x_{n},x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq337.gif), then ![
$$\\rho \(x_{n},x\) = d\(x_{n},x\){\[1 + d\(x_{n},x\)\]}^{-1} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq338.gif). Conversely, if ![
$$\\rho \(x_{n},x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq339.gif), then, since we always have that ρ(x, y) < 1, ![
$$d\(x_{n},x\) =\\rho \(x_{n},x\){\[1 -\\rho \(x_{n},x\)\]}^{-1} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq340.gif). ■

An important feature of the preceding metric is that it is bounded by 1. This is a warning for us not to put too much stock in the concept of a bounded set.

Example 1.3.14.
