Two equivalent metrics do not necessarily have the same Cauchy sequences; in fact, with one metric it can be complete and with respect to the other it is not. We will show this by defining an equivalent metric ρ on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq341.gif) such that with this metric ![
$$\(\\mathbb{R},\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq342.gif) is not complete. Consider the circle ![
$$X =\\{ \(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: {x}^{2} + {\(y - 1\)}^{2} = 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq343.gif), that is, the circle of radius one in the plane centered at (0, 1). Give X the metric d it has as a subset of the plane. We want to describe a function ![
$$f: \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq344.gif) geometrically. It is possible to do this with a formula, but the geometry makes all we are going to say transparent. For any t in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq345.gif) consider the straight line in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq346.gif) determined by (t, 0) and (0, 2), and let f(t) equal the point on the circle X where this line intersects it. Note several things. If − 1 < t < 1, then f(t) lies on the lower half of the circle, whereas when | t | > 1, f(t) is on the upper half; also, f(1) = (1, 0), f(−1) = (−1, 0). In addition, note that f is injective and ![
$$f\(\\mathbb{R}\) = X\\setminus \\{\(0,2\)\\} \\equiv Y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq347.gif). We use f to put a new metric on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq348.gif) by letting ρ(t, s) = d(f(t), f(s)). It is not hard to see that the metric ρ is equivalent to the standard metric on the real line defined by the absolute value and that ![
$$f: \(\\mathbb{R},\\rho \) \\rightarrow \(Y,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq349.gif) is a bijective isometry. By referring to the geometry it is easy to see that the sequence {n} is a Cauchy sequence in ![
$$\(\\mathbb{R},\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq350.gif), but of course it is not in the usual metric for ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq351.gif). Thus, ![
$$\(\\mathbb{R},\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq352.gif) is not complete.

Definition 1.3.15.

A function ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq353.gif) between two metric spaces is uniformly continuous if for every ε > 0 there is a δ such that ρ(f(x), f(y)) < ε when ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq354.gif).

The reader may have seen this concept in calculus. The difference between a continuous and a uniformly continuous function, as you might have observed, is the following. When given an ε, for uniform continuity we need to find one δ that will work for all pairs of points x, y; for continuity we need to find a δ for only one point x at a time. That is, in the definition of continuity the δ depends on the point x as well as ε, while for uniform continuity the δ depends only on ε. So every uniformly continuous function is continuous, but there are continuous functions that are not uniformly continuous, as we will see in the next example.

Example 1.3.16.

(a)

A function ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq355.gif) is a Lipschitz function if there is a constant M > 0 such that ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(y\)\) \\leq Md\(x,y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq356.gif) for all x, y in X. A ready collection of examples occurs by letting I be an interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq357.gif) and letting ![
$$f: I \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq358.gif) be a continuously differentiable function with | f ′ (x)) | ≤ M for all x in I. Thus, ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(y\)\\vert = \\left \\vert \\int _{y}^{x}{f}^{{\\prime}}\(t\)dt\\right \\vert \\leq \\int _{y}^{x}\\vert {f}^{{\\prime}}\(t\)\\vert dt \\leq M\\vert x - y\\vert $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq359.gif). Every Lipschitz function is uniformly continuous since for any ε > 0 we can take ![
$$\\delta = \\epsilon /M$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq360.gif).

(b)

We note that when ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq361.gif), the function ![
$$x\\mapsto \\text{dist}\\,\(x,A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq362.gif) is a Lipschitz function by Proposition 1.3.5. Thus the distance function gives rise to a plentiful source of uniformly continuous functions on any metric space.

(c)

The function ![
$$f: \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq363.gif) defined by f(x) = x 2 is not uniformly continuous. In fact, for any ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq364.gif), consider x = n, a natural number, and ![
$$y = n + \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq365.gif). So ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(y\)\\vert = {\(n + \\delta \)}^{2} - {n}^{2} > 2n\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq366.gif), and this can be made as large as desired no matter how small we make ![
$$\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq367.gif).

(d)

The function ![
$$f: \(0,1\] \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq368.gif) defined by f(t) = sin(t −1) is continuous but not uniformly continuous. That it is continuous follows from calculus and writing f as the composition of the two functions t↦t −1 and the sine function. The fact that it is not uniformly continuous can be seen as follows. If ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq369.gif), then there are points s and t in the interval ![
$$\(0,\\delta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq370.gif) with f(s) = 1 and f(t) = −1, so that f(s) − f(t) = 2, even though ![
$$\\vert s - t\\vert < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq371.gif).

The proof of part (b) in the next proposition is a good one to truly concentrate on as it has techniques that will surface many times in your future.

Proposition 1.3.17.

(a) If ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq372.gif) is a uniformly continuous function and {x n } is a Cauchy sequence in X, then {f(x n )} is a Cauchy sequence in Z.

(b) If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq373.gif) , (Z,ρ) is a complete metric space, and ![
$$f: A \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq374.gif) is uniformly continuous, then f can be extended to a uniformly continuous function ![
$$f: \\text{cl}\\,A \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq375.gif) .

Proof.

(a)

Let ε > 0; thus, there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq376.gif) such that ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(y\)\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq377.gif) whenever ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq378.gif). If {x n } is a Cauchy sequence in X, then let N be chosen such that ![
$$d\(x_{n},x_{m}\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq379.gif) when m, n ≥ N. Thus, ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x_{n}\),f\(x_{m}\)\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq380.gif) when m, n ≥ N, and therefore {f(x n )} is a Cauchy sequence.

(b)

If ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq381.gif), then there is a sequence {a n } in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq382.gif). By part (a) this implies that {f(a n )} is a Cauchy sequence in ![
$$\(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq383.gif); since (Z, ρ) is complete, there is a z in Z such that ![
$$f\(a_{n}\) \\rightarrow z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq384.gif). We want to define f(x) = z; to do this, we need to show that the point z is independent of which sequence {a n } we choose to converge to x—otherwise, setting f(x) = z is an ambiguous definition. So assume that {b n } is also a sequence in A that converges to x and let ![
$$\\zeta \\in Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq385.gif) such that ![
$$f\(b_{n}\) \\rightarrow \\zeta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq386.gif); we want to show that ![
$$\\zeta = z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq387.gif). Observe that if we define c 2n = a n and ![
$$c_{2n+1} = b_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq388.gif), then ![
$$c_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq389.gif); let ![
$$y =\\lim _{n}f\(c_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq390.gif). Since {f(c n )} has a subsequence that converges to z and another that converges to ![
$$\\zeta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq391.gif), it must be that ![
$$z = y = \\zeta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq392.gif). Thus ![
$$f: \\text{cl}\\,A \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq393.gif) is well defined if we set ![
$$f\(x\) =\\lim _{n}f\(a_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq394.gif) whenever ![
$$\\{a_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq395.gif) is any sequence in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq396.gif). Now we will show that this extended function is uniformly continuous.

Let ε > 0, and choose ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq397.gif) such that ![
$$\\rho \(f\(a\),f\(b\)\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq398.gif) whenever a, b ∈ A and ![
$$d\(a,b\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq399.gif). Assume ![
$$x,y \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq400.gif) and ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq401.gif). So there are sequences {a n } and {b n } of points in A such that ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq402.gif) and ![
$$b_{n} \\rightarrow y$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq403.gif). Let N 1 ≥ 1 such that ![
$$\\rho \(f\(a_{n}\),f\(x\)\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq404.gif) and ![
$$\\rho \(f\(b_{n}\),f\(y\)\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq405.gif) when n ≥ N 1. Thus for n ≥ N 1

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\rho \(f\(x\),f\(y\)\)& \\leq &\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(a_{n}\)\) +\\rho \(f\(a_{n}\),f\(b_{n}\)\) +\\rho \(f\(b_{n}\),f\(y\)\) {}\\\\ & <& \\frac{2\\epsilon } {3} +\\rho \(f\(a_{n}\),f\(b_{n}\)\). {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ5.gif)

Now we can choose N 2 ≥ 1 such that when n ≥ N 2, we have ![
$$d\(a_{n},x\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq406.gif) and ![
$$d\(b_{n},y\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq407.gif). (Why?) Thus, ![
$$d\(a_{n},b_{n}\) \\leq d\(a_{n},x\) + d\(x,y\) + d\(y,b_{n}\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq408.gif). But since a n , b n ∈ A, this implies ![
$$\\rho \(f\(a_{n}\),f\(b_{n}\)\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq00412.gif) whenever n ≥ N 2. From the preceding inequality with ![
$$n \\geq N =\\max \\{ N_{1},N_{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq409.gif) we have that ρ(f(x), f(y)) < ε whenever ![
$$x,y \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq410.gif) and ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\frac{1} {3}\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq411.gif). Therefore, ![
$$f: \\text{cl}\\,A \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq412.gif) is uniformly continuous.

■

The preceding proposition underlines an important distinction between continuous and uniformly continuous functions. We saw in Example 1.3.14 that two equivalent metrics do not necessarily have the same Cauchy sequences, so this shows in dramatic fashion that Proposition 1.3.17(a) fails for continuous functions. If we consider the metric space X = (0, 1] and the continuous function ![
$$f: \(0,1\] \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq413.gif) defined by f(x) = sin(x −1), then we see that part (b) of the preceding proposition fails if we only assume that the function is continuous (Exercise 1). This also shows that sin(x −1) is not uniformly continuous, though we gave a more direct proof of this in Example 1.3.16.

We might also mention that the converse of Proposition 1.3.17(a) does not hold as the function f(x) = x 2 maps Cauchy sequences to Cauchy sequences, but, as we have seen, it is not uniformly continuous (Exercise 10).

## Exercises

(1)

Show that for any number a the function ![
$$f: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq414.gif) defined by f(x) = sin(x −1) when x ≠ 0 and f(0) = a is not continuous at 0.

(2)

If (X, d) is a metric space, ![
$$f: B\(a;r\) \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq415.gif) is continuous at a with f(a) = 0, and ![
$$g: B\(a;r\) \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq416.gif) is a bounded function (but not necessarily continuous), then fg is continuous at a.

(3)

If ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq417.gif) is continuous, A is a dense subset of X, and z ∈ Z such that f(a) = z for every a in A, show that f(x) = z for every x in X.

(4)

If ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq418.gif) is both continuous and surjective and A is a dense subset of X, show that f(A) is a dense subset of Z.

(5)

In Theorem 1.3.3, give an independent proof that shows that conditions (a) and (c) are equivalent. (Here, "independent" means that the proof should not use the equivalence of (a) and (b) or of (b) and (c).)

(6)

Prove the statement made in Example 1.3.4(d). What about a continuous function from ![
$$\[0,1\] \\cup \[2,3\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq419.gif) into X?

(7)

Let q and p be natural numbers, and show that the metric on ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q+p}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq420.gif) is equivalent to the metric it has if we identify ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q+p}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq421.gif) with ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q} \\times {\\mathbb{R}}^{p}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq422.gif).

(8)

If (X 1, d 1) and (X 2, d 2) are two metric spaces, show that the map ![
$$\\pi _{1}: X_{1} \\times X_{2} \\rightarrow X_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq423.gif) defined by π 1(x 1, x 2) = x 1 is continuous.

(9)

If (X, d), (Z 1, ρ 1), (Z 2, ρ 2) are metric spaces and for k = 1, 2, ![
$$\\pi _{k}: Z_{1} \\times Z_{2} \\rightarrow Z_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq424.gif) is the projection ![
$$\\pi _{k}\(z_{1},z_{2}\) = z_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq425.gif), show that a map ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z_{1} \\times Z_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq426.gif) is continuous if and only if π k ∘ f is continuous for k = 1, 2.

(10)

Show that x 2 maps Cauchy sequences in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq427.gif) into Cauchy sequences. (Hint: use Proposition 1.2.16(c).)

(11)

(a) Give an example of two equivalent metrics on a set X that have different sets of uniformly continuous functions. (b) Do the equivalent metrics in Exercise 1.1.11 have the same uniformly continuous functions?

(12)

Is the composition of two uniformly continuous functions a uniformly continuous function?

(13)

Note that Proposition 1.3.7 says that if (X, d) is a metric space and C(X) is the set of all continuous functions ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq428.gif), then C(X) is a vector space over ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq429.gif). Show that C(X) is a finite-dimensional vector space if and only if X is a finite set. (Hint: use Urysohn's Lemma.)

(14)

Let (X, d) be a metric space, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq430.gif) denote the set of all uniformly continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq431.gif). (a) If ![
$$f,g\\in \\mathcal {U}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq000152.gif) and we define ![
$$\(f + g\): X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq432.gif) by (f \+ g)(x) = f(x) + g(x) for all x in X, show that ![
$$f + g \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq433.gif). In words, ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq434.gif) is a vector space over ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq435.gif). (b) If ![
$$f,g \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq436.gif) and we define ![
$$\(fg\): X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq437.gif) by (fg)(x) = f(x)g(x) for all x in X, show by an example that it does not necessarily follow that ![
$$fg \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq438.gif). If, however, the functions are also bounded, then ![
$$fg \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq439.gif). [A function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq440.gif) is bounded if f(X) is a bounded subset of Z.] (c) Can you give some conditions under which the quotient of two uniformly continuous functions is uniformly continuous?

(15)

Is the function π 1 defined in Exercise 8 uniformly continuous?

## 1.4 Compactness

If ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq441.gif) is a collection of subsets of X and ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq442.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq443.gif) is a cover of E if ![
$$E \\subseteq \\bigcup \\{ G: G \\in \\mathcal{G}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq444.gif). A subcover of E is a subset ![
$$\\mathcal{G}_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq445.gif) of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq446.gif) that is also a cover of E. Finally, we say that ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq447.gif) is an open cover of E if ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq448.gif) is a cover and every set in the collection ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq449.gif) is open.

Definition 1.4.1.

A subset K of the metric space (X, d) is said to be compact if every open cover of K has a finite subcover.

We mention that the term "open cover" in this definition can be replaced by "cover by subsets of K that are relatively open" (Exercise 2).

It is easy to find examples of sets that are not compact. Specifically, the open interval (0, 1) is not compact since if we set G n = (n −1, 1), then ![
$$\\mathcal{G} =\\{ G_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq450.gif) is an open cover of the interval that has no finite subcover. Similarly, ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq451.gif) is not compact since ![
$$\\{\(-n,n\): n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq452.gif) is an open cover of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq453.gif) that has no finite subcover. We can easily see that every finite subset of X is compact, but finding nontrivial examples of compact sets requires us to first prove some results.

Proposition 1.4.2.

Let (X,d) be a metric space.

(a) If K is a compact subset of X, then K is closed and bounded.

(b) If K is compact and F is a closed set contained in K, then F is compact.

(c) The continuous image of a compact subset is a compact subset.

Proof.

(a)

If x∉K, then for each z in K let r z , s z > 0 such that ![
$$B\(z;r_{z}\) \\cap B\(x;s_{z}\) = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq454.gif). Now ![
$$\\{B\(z;r_{z}\): z \\in K\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq455.gif) is an open cover of K. Since K is compact, there are points z 1,..., z n in K such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}B\(z_{k};r_{z_{k}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq456.gif). Let ![
$$s =\\min \\{ s_{z_{k}}: 1 \\leq k \\leq n\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq457.gif). Note that ![
$$B\(x;s\) \\cap K = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq458.gif); in fact, if there is a y in ![
$$B\(x;s\) \\cap K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq459.gif), then there is a k such that ![
$$y \\in B\(x;s\) \\cap B\(z_{k};r_{z_{k}}\) \\subseteq B\(x;s_{z_{k}}\) \\cap B\(z_{k};r_{z_{k}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq460.gif), which contradicts the choice of the numbers ![
$$s_{z_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq461.gif) and ![
$$r_{z_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq462.gif). Therefore, ![
$$B\(x;s\) \\subseteq X\\setminus K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq463.gif). Since x was arbitrary, this says that ![
$$X\\setminus K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq464.gif) is open. Also, for any point x 0 in X, ![
$$\\{B\(x_{0};n\): n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq465.gif) is an open cover of K; hence there is a finite subcover. But the sets in this cover are increasing, so there is an integer n such that ![
$$K \\subseteq B\(x_{0};n\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq466.gif) and K is bounded.

(b)

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq467.gif) be an open cover of F, and observe that since F is closed, ![
$$\\{X\\setminus F\\} \\cup \\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq468.gif) is an open cover of K. The existence of a finite subcover of K implies there is a finite subcollection of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq469.gif) that covers F.

(c)

Let ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq470.gif) be a continuous function, and assume K is a compact subset of X; we want to show that f(K) is a compact subset of Z. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq471.gif) be an open cover of f(K) in ![
$$\(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq472.gif). Since f is continuous, it follows that ![
$$\\mathcal{G} =\\{ {f}^{-1}\(U\): U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq473.gif) is an open cover of ![
$$K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq474.gif). Therefore, there are sets U 1,..., U n in ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq475.gif) such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}{f}^{-1}\(U_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq476.gif). It follows that ![
$$f\(K\) \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}U_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq477.gif).

■

Now for a result from calculus that is an easy consequence of what we have done. This is sometimes called the extreme value property or theorem.

Corollary 1.4.3.

If (X,d) is a compact metric space and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq478.gif) is a continuous function, then there are points a and b in X such that f(a) ≤ f(x) ≤ f(b) for all x in X.

Proof.

We have from Proposition 1.4.2 that f(X) is a closed and bounded subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq479.gif). Put ![
$$\\alpha =\\inf \\{ f\(x\): x \\in X\\},\\beta =\\sup \\{ f\(x\): x \\in X\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq480.gif). Since f(X) is closed, ![
$$\\alpha,\\beta \\in f\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq481.gif) by the Completeness Property (Axiom A.3.3) of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq482.gif); this proves the corollary. ■

We need two more definitions.

Definition 1.4.4.

Say that a subset K of the metric space (X, d) is totally bounded if for any radius r > 0 there are points x 1,..., x n in K such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}B\(x_{k};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq483.gif). A collection ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq484.gif) of subsets of K has the finite intersection property (FIP) if whenever F 1,..., F n ∈ ℱ, ![
$$\\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}F_{k}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq485.gif).

The following theorem is the main result on compactness in metric spaces.

Theorem 1.4.5.

The following statements are equivalent for a closed subset K of a metric space ![
$$\(X,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq486.gif) .

(a) K is compact.

(b) If ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq487.gif) is a collection of closed subsets of K having the FIP, then it holds that ![
$$\\bigcap _{F\\in \\mathcal{F}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq488.gif) F≠∅.

(c) Every sequence in K has a convergent subsequence.

(d) Every infinite subset of K has a limit point.

(e) (K,d) is a complete metric space that is totally bounded.

Proof.

(a) implies (b). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq489.gif) be a collection of closed subsets of K having the FIP. Suppose ![
$$\\bigcap \\{F: F \\in \\mathcal{F}\\} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq490.gif). If ![
$$\\mathcal{G} =\\{ X\\setminus F: F \\in \\mathcal{F}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq491.gif), then it follows that ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq492.gif) is an open cover of X and, therefore, of K. By (a), there are F 1,..., F n in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq493.gif) such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{j=1}^{n}\(X\\setminus F_{j}\) = X\\setminus \\left \[\\bigcap _{j=1}^{n}F_{j}\\right \]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq494.gif). But since each F j is a subset of K, this implies ![
$$\\bigcap _{j=1}^{n}F_{j} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq495.gif), contradicting the fact that ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq496.gif) has the FIP.

(b) implies (a). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq497.gif) be an open cover of K, and put ![
$$\\mathcal{F} =\\{ K\\setminus G: G \\in \\mathcal{G}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq498.gif). Since ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq499.gif) covers ![
$$K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq500.gif), ![
$$\\bigcap \\{K\\setminus G: G \\in \\mathcal{G}\\} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq501.gif). Thus ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq502.gif) cannot have the FIP and there must be a finite number of sets G 1,..., G n in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq503.gif) with ![
$$\\varnothing =\\bigcap _{ j=1}^{n}\(K\\setminus G_{j}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq504.gif). But this implies that {G 1,..., G n } is a finite cover of K. Hence K is compact.

(d) implies (c). Assume {x n } is a sequence of distinct points in K. By (d), {x n } has a limit point; since K is closed, that limit point must be in K. We are tempted here to invoke Proposition 1.2.7(a), but we must manufacture an actual subsequence of the original sequence. This takes a little bit of care and effort, which we leave to the interested reader.

(c) implies (d). If S is an infinite subset, then S has a sequence of distinct points {x n }; by (c), there is a subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq505.gif) that converges to some point x. It follows that x is a limit point of S. (Details?)

(a) implies (d). Assume that (d) is false. So there is an infinite subset S of K with no limit point; it follows that there is an infinite sequence {x n } in S with no limit point. Thus, for each n ≥ 1, ![
$$F_{n} =\\{ x_{k}: k \\geq n\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq506.gif) contains all its limit points and is therefore closed. Also, ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq507.gif). But each finite subcollection of {F 1, F 2,...} has nonempty intersection, contradicting (b), which is equivalent to (a).

(a) implies (e). First, let {x n } be a Cauchy sequence in K. Since (a) implies (d), which is equivalent to (c), there is an x in K and a subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq508.gif) such that ![
$$x_{n_{k}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq509.gif). But this implies ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq510.gif) by Proposition 1.2.11. Hence, (K, d) is complete. To show that K is totally bounded, just note that ![
$$\\{B\(x;r\): x \\in K\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq511.gif) is an open cover of K for any r > 0.

(e) implies (c). Fix an infinite sequence {x n } in K, and let {ε n } be a decreasing sequence of positive numbers such that ![
$$\\epsilon _{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq512.gif). By (e), there is a covering of K by a finite number of balls of radius ε 1. Thus there is a ball ![
$$B\(y_{1};\\epsilon _{1}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq513.gif) that contains an infinite number of points from {x n }; let ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{1} =\\{ n \\in \\mathbb{N}: d\(x_{n},y_{1}\) < \\epsilon _{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq514.gif). Now consider the sequence ![
$$\\{x_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq515.gif) and balls of radius ε 2. As we just did, there is a point y 2 in K such that ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{2} =\\{ n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{1}: d\(y_{2},x_{n}\) < \\epsilon _{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq516.gif) is an infinite set. Using induction we can show that for each k ≥ 1 we get a point y k in K and an infinite set of positive integers ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq517.gif) such that ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{k+1} \\subseteq \\mathbb{N}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq518.gif) and ![
$$\\{x_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{k}\\} \\subseteq B\(y_{k};\\epsilon _{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq519.gif). If ![
$$F_{k} = \\text{cl}\\,\\{x_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq520.gif), then ![
$$F_{k+1} \\subseteq F_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq521.gif) and ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,F_{k} \\leq 2\\epsilon _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq522.gif). Since K is complete, Cantor's Theorem implies that ![
$$\\bigcap _{k=1}^{\\infty }F_{k} =\\{ x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq523.gif) for some point x in X. Now, using a small induction argument, pick integers n k in ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq524.gif) such that ![
$$n_{k} < n_{k+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq525.gif). It follows that ![
$$\\{x_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq526.gif) is a subsequence of the original sequence and ![
$$x_{n_{k}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq527.gif).

(e) implies (a). We first prove the following claim.

Claim 1.4.6.

If K satisfies (c) and ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq528.gif) is an open cover of K, then there is an r > 0 such that for each x in K there is a G in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq529.gif) such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq530.gif).

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq531.gif) be an open cover of K, and suppose the claim is false; thus, for every n ≥ 1 there is an x n in K such that ![
$$B\(x_{n};{n}^{-1}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq532.gif) is not contained in any set G in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq533.gif). By (c), there is an x in K and a subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{k}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq534.gif) such that ![
$$x_{n_{k}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq535.gif). Since ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq536.gif) is a cover, there is a G in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq537.gif) such that x ∈ G; choose a positive ε such that ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq538.gif). Let ![
$$n_{k} > 2{\\epsilon }^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq539.gif) such that ![
$$x_{n_{k}} \\in B\(x;\\epsilon /2\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq540.gif). If ![
$$y \\in B\(x_{n_{k}};n_{k}^{-1}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq541.gif), then ![
$$d\(x,y\) \\leq d\(x,x_{n_{k}}\) + d\(x_{n_{k}},y\) < \\epsilon /2 + n_{k}^{-1} < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq542.gif), so that ![
$$y \\in B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq543.gif). Thus ![
$$B\(x_{n_{k}};n_{k}^{-1}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq544.gif), contradicting the restriction imposed on ![
$$x_{n_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq545.gif). This establishes the claim.

From here it is easy to complete the proof. We know that (e) implies (c), so for an open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq546.gif) of K let r > 0 be the number guaranteed by Claim 1.4.6. Now let ![
$$x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n} \\in K$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq547.gif) such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}B\(x_{k};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq548.gif), and for 1 ≤ k ≤ n let ![
$$G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq549.gif) such that ![
$$B\(x_{k};r\) \\subseteq G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq550.gif). ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq551.gif) is the sought-after finite subcover.

(c) implies (e). If {x n } is a Cauchy sequence in K, then (c) implies it has a convergent subsequence; by Proposition 1.2.11, the original sequence converges. Thus (K, d) is complete. Now fix an r > 0. Let x 1 ∈ K; if ![
$$K \\subseteq B\(x_{1};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq552.gif), then we are done. If not, then there is a point x 2 in ![
$$K\\setminus B\(x_{1};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq553.gif). Once again, if ![
$$K \\subseteq B\(x_{1};r\) \\cup B\(x_{2};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq554.gif), we are done; otherwise, pick an x 3 in ![
$$K\\setminus \[B\(x_{1};r\) \\cup B\(x_{2};r\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq555.gif). Continue. If this process does not stop after a finite number of steps, then we produce an infinite sequence {x n } in K with ![
$$d\(x_{n},x_{m}\) \\geq r$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq556.gif) whenever n ≠ m. But this implies that this sequence can have no convergent subsequence, contradicting (c). ■

Compactness is one of the most important properties; many good things follow from it, as we shall see in this book, and readers will continue to see as they continue their careers. The point is that compact sets are almost finite in a very technical sense, and this approximation of finiteness often suffices to allow us to carry out an argument for a compact set that we can easily make for a finite set. We already saw this in Corollary 1.4.3.

The next result gives the converse of Proposition 1.4.2(a) for ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq557.gif), but first we need a lemma.

Lemma 1.4.7.

If −∞ < a < b < ∞ in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq558.gif) , then [a,b] is compact.

Proof.

Clearly a closed interval, being a closed subset of a complete metric space, is complete. To show [a, b] is compact, it therefore suffices to show it is totally bounded [Theorem 1.4.5(e)]. But if r > 0, then we can easily find ![
$$a = x_{1} < x_{2} < \\cdots < x_{n} = b$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq559.gif) such that x k−1 − x k < r, and from here it is routine to show that ![
$$\[a,b\] \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}B\(x_{k};r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq560.gif). ■

Theorem 1.4.8 (Heine5–Borel6 Theorem).

A subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq561.gif) is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.

Proof.

If K is compact, then K is closed and bounded by Proposition 1.4.2. Now assume that K is closed and bounded. It follows that there are bounded intervals ![
$$\[a_{1},b_{1}\],\\ldots,\[a_{q},b_{q}\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq562.gif) in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq563.gif) such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \[a_{1},b_{1}\] \\times \\cdots \\times \[a_{q},b_{q}\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq564.gif). Suppose {x n } is a sequence in K with ![
$$x_{n} = \(x_{n}^{1},\\ldots,x_{n}^{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq565.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq566.gif) is a sequence in [a 1, b 1], so the preceding lemma implies it has a convergent subsequence. The notation in this proof could become grotesque if we do the standard things, so we depart from the standard. Denote the convergent subsequence by ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{1}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq567.gif), where ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{1} \\subseteq \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq568.gif) and ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq569.gif) has its natural ordering. We have that the limit exists, so put ![
$${x}^{1} =\\lim _{n\\in \\mathbb{N}_{1}}x_{n}^{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq570.gif). Now consider the sequence ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{2}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq571.gif) in ![
$$\[a_{2},b_{2}\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq572.gif). It has a convergent subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n}^{2}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq573.gif) with ![
$${x}^{2} =\\lim _{n\\in \\mathbb{N}_{2}}x_{n}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq574.gif). Continue, and we get ![
$$\\mathbb{N}_{q} \\subseteq \\cdots \\subseteq \\mathbb{N}_{1} \\subseteq \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq575.gif) and ![
$${x}^{k} =\\lim _{n\\in \\mathbb{N}_{k}}x_{n}^{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq576.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ q. It follows that ![
$$\\{x_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}_{q}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq577.gif) is a subsequence of the original sequence and it converges to ![
$$x = \({x}^{1},\\ldots,{x}^{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq578.gif); it must be that x ∈ K since K is closed. By Theorem 1.4.5, K is compact. ■

Example 1.4.9.

For the metric space ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq579.gif), if ![
$$a,b \\in \\mathbb{Q},a < b$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq580.gif), then the set ![
$$F =\\{ x \\in \\mathbb{Q}: a \\leq x \\leq b\\} = \\mathbb{Q} \\cap \[a,b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq581.gif) is closed and bounded but not compact. In fact, there is a sequence {x n } in F that converges to an irrational number, and so this sequence has no subsequence that converges to a point of F. Thus, in an arbitrary metric space, a closed and bounded set need not be compact, and the Heine–Borel Theorem is particular to Euclidean space.

Note that the metric used for ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq582.gif) in the Heine–Borel Theorem must be the standard one, or the result may fail even for an equivalent metric. For example, if we use the metric ![
$$d\(x,y\) = \\vert x - y\\vert {\(1 + \\vert x - y\\vert \)}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq583.gif) on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq584.gif), then all of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq585.gif) is closed and bounded but not compact. This furnishes another example of a closed and bounded set that is not compact.

Theorem 1.4.10.

If (X,d) is a compact metric space and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq586.gif) is a continuous function, then f is uniformly continuous.

Proof.

Suppose f is not uniformly continuous. So there is an ε > 0 such that for every ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq587.gif) there are points x and y in X with ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq588.gif), but ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(y\)\) \\geq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq589.gif). In particular, for each natural number n there are points x n , y n in X such that ![
$$d\(x_{n},y_{n}\) < {n}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq590.gif), but ![
$$\\rho \(f\(x_{n}\),f\(y_{n}\)\) \\geq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq591.gif). Since X is compact, there is a point x in X and a subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{j}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq592.gif) such that ![
$$x_{n_{j}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq593.gif). Since ![
$$d\(y_{n_{j}},x\) \\leq n_{j}^{-1} + d\(x_{n_{j}},x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq594.gif), we have that ![
$$y_{n_{j}} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq595.gif). Therefore, since f is a continuous function,

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\epsilon \\leq \\rho \(f\(x_{n_{j}}\),f\(y_{n_{j}}\)\) \\leq \\rho \(f\(x_{n_{j}}\),f\(x\)\) +\\rho \(f\(x\),f\(y_{n_{j}}\)\) \\rightarrow 0.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equv.gif)

This contradiction proves the theorem. ■

Proposition 1.4.11.

A compact metric space is separable.

Proof.

For each natural number n we can find a finite set F n such that ![
$$X =\\bigcup \\{ B\(x;{n}^{-1}\): x \\in F_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq596.gif). Put ![
$$F =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq597.gif); we will show that this countable set F is dense in X. In fact, if x 0 is an arbitrary point in X and ε > 0, then choose n such that n −1 < ε. Thus, there is a point x in ![
$$F_{n} \\subseteq F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq598.gif) with d(x 0, x) < n −1 < ε, proving that ![
$$x_{0} \\in \\text{cl}\\,F$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq599.gif). ■

## Exercises

(1)

Show that the union of a finite number of compact sets is compact.

(2)

If K is a subset of ![
$$\(X,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq600.gif), show that K is compact if and only if every cover of K by relatively open subsets of K has a finite subcover.

(3)

Show that the closure of a totally bounded set is totally bounded.

(4)

Show that a totally bounded set is bounded. Is the converse true?

(5)

If {E n } is a sequence of totally bounded sets such that ![
$$\\text{diam}\\,E_{n} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq601.gif), show that ![
$$\\bigcup _{n=1}^{\\infty }E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq602.gif) is totally bounded.

(6)

If (X, d) is a complete metric space and ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq603.gif), show that E is totally bounded if and only if ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,E$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq604.gif) is compact.

(7)

(a) If G is an open set and K is a compact set with ![
$$K \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq605.gif), show that there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq606.gif) such that ![
$$\\{x: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,K\) < \\delta \\} \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq607.gif). (b) Find an example of an open set G in a metric space X and a closed, noncompact subset F of G such that there is no ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq608.gif) with ![
$$\\{x: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,F\) < \\delta \\} \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq609.gif).

(8)

If ![
$$\(X_{1},d_{1}\),\(X_{2},d_{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq610.gif) are metric spaces, show that X 1 × X 2 is compact if and only if both X 1 and X 2 are compact.

(9)

Give an example of a noncompact metric space (X, d) and a continuous function ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq611.gif) such that f(X) is compact.

(10)

For two subsets A and B of X, define the distance from A to B by ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(A,B\) =\\inf \\{ d\(a,b\): a \\in A,b \\in B\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq612.gif). (a) Show that ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(A,B\) = \\text{dist}\\,\(B,A\) = \\text{dist}\\,\(\\text{cl}\\,A,\\text{cl}\\,B\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq613.gif). (b) If A and B are two disjoint closed subsets of X such that B is compact, then dist (A, B) > 0. (c) Give an example of two disjoint closed subsets A and B of the plane ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq614.gif) such that ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(A,B\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq615.gif). (d) Is this exercise related to Exercise 7?

(11)

Consider the metric space ℓ ∞ (see Exercise 1.1.12) and show that

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\left \\{x =\\{ x_{n}\\} {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }:\\sup _{ n}\\vert x_{n}\\vert \\leq 1\\right \\}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equw.gif)

is not totally bounded and, therefore, not compact.

(12)

Say that a metric space is σ-compact if it can be written as the union of a countable number of compact sets. (a) Give three examples of σ-compact metric spaces that are not compact. (b) Show that a σ-compact metric space is separable.

## 1.5 Connectedness

In this section, we introduce and explore another important concept for metric spaces.

Consider the following two examples of subsets of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq616.gif). The first is the set X = [0, 1] ∪ (2, 3), and the second is Y = [0, 1] ∪ (1, 2). In X there are two distinct "parts," [0, 1] and (2, 3). In the second we have written Y as the union of two sets, but these two sets are not really separate "parts." (The term "parts" will be made technically precise soon, though we will not use that term.) In a sense, writing Y as the union of those two sets is just accidental. We could just as well have written Y = [0, 1) ∪ [1, 2) or ![
$$Y = \[0, \\frac{1} {2}\] \\cup \(\\frac{1} {2},2\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq617.gif) or even Y = [0, 2). What is the true difference between the two sets X and Y?

Note that in the metric space ![
$$\(X,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq618.gif) of the last paragraph, the set [0, 1] is simultaneously both an open and a closed set. For example, ![
$$B_{X}\(1; \\frac{1} {2}\) =\\{ x \\in X: \\vert x - 1\\vert < \\frac{1} {2}\\} = \(\\frac{1} {2},1\] \\subseteq \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq619.gif). It follows that [0, 1] is open in X as well as closed; similarly, (2, 3) is open in X as well as closed. The set X is an example of what we now define as a set that is not connected or, more succinctly, a disconnected set.

Definition 1.5.1.

A metric space (X, d) is connected if there are no subsets of X that are simultaneously open and closed other than X and ∅. If ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq620.gif), we say that E is connected if (E, d) is connected. If E is not connected, then we will say that it is disconnected or a nonconnected set.

An equivalent formulation of connectedness is to say that (X, d) is connected provided that when X = A ∪ B, where ![
$$A \\cap B = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq621.gif) and both A and B or open (or closed), then either A = ∅ or B = ∅. This is the sense of our use of the term "parts" in the introduction of this section; for the set X there, A = [0, 1] and B = (2, 3) are two disjoint, nontrivial sets that are both open and closed in X.

The first result can be considered an example, but it is much more than that.

Proposition 1.5.2.

A subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq622.gif) is connected if and only if it is an interval.

Proof.

Assume that X = [a, b], and let us show that X is connected. (The proof that other types of intervals are connected is Exercise 1.) Assume that [a, b] = A ∪ B, where both A and B are open and ![
$$A \\cap B = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq623.gif). One of these sets contains the point a; suppose a ∈ A. Note that A is also closed. We want to show that A = X. Since A is open, there is an ε > 0 such that ![
$$\[a,a + \\epsilon \) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq624.gif). Put ![
$$r =\\sup \\{ \\epsilon: \[a,a + \\epsilon \) \\subseteq A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq625.gif). We claim that ![
$$\[a,a + r\) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq626.gif). In fact, if a ≤ x < a \+ r, then the definition of the supremum implies there is an ε > 0 such that ![
$$\\epsilon < r$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq627.gif), ![
$$\[a,a + \\epsilon \) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq628.gif), and ![
$$a \\leq x < a + \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq629.gif); thus x ∈ A. Now A is also closed and ![
$$\[a,a + r\) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq630.gif), so it must also be that ![
$$a + r \\in A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq631.gif). If ![
$$a + r\\neq b$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq632.gif), then the fact that A is open implies that there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq633.gif) such that ![
$$\(a + r -\\delta,a + r + \\delta \) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq634.gif). But this means that ![
$$\[a,a + r + \\delta \) = \[a,a + r\) \\cup \(a + r -\\delta,a + r + \\delta \) \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq635.gif), contradicting the definition of r. Therefore, a \+ r = b, and we have that A = [a, b] = X. (So B = ∅.)

Let us begin the converse by deciding how we recognize an interval: a subset E of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq636.gif) is an interval if and only if when a, b ∈ E and a < b, then c ∈ E whenever a < c < b; equivalently, ![
$$\[a,b\] \\subseteq E$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq637.gif) when a, b ∈ E (Exercise 2). So assume that X is a nonempty connected subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq638.gif) and a, b ∈ X with a < b; suppose a < c < b. If c∉X, then let ![
$$A = X \\cap \(-\\infty,c\),B = X \\cap \(c,\\infty \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq639.gif). Clearly A and B are open subsets relative to X; since ![
$$c\\notin X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq640.gif), we also have that ![
$$A = X \\cap \(-\\infty,c\],B = X \\cap \[c,\\infty \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq641.gif), and they are also closed relative to X (Exercise 1.1.6). Since a ∈ A and b ∈ B, neither is empty, contradicting the assumption that X is connected. ■

Theorem 1.5.3.

The continuous image of a connected set is connected.

Proof.

Let ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(Z,\\rho \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq642.gif) be a continuous function and E a connected subset of X; we want to show that f(E) is a connected subset of Z. By replacing X with E, we may assume X = E is connected; by replacing Z with f(E), we may assume that f is surjective. We must now show that Z is connected. If D is a subset of Z that is both open and closed, then the continuity of f implies f −1(D) is both open and closed in X. Since X is connected, f −1(D) is either ∅ or X. But since f is surjective, this implies D is either ∅ or Z. Thus Z is connected. ■

The preceding theorem allows us to deduce a standard result from calculus, which here is placed in a more general setting.

Corollary 1.5.4 (Intermediate Value Theorem).

If ![
$$f: \(X,d\) \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq643.gif) is continuous, X is connected, a,b ∈ f(X) with a < b, then for any number c in the interval [a,b] there is a point x in X with f(x) = c.

Proof.

We know that f(X) is a connected subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq644.gif) so that it must be an interval (Proposition 1.5.2). Since a, b ∈ f(X), it must be that ![
$$\[a,b\] \\subseteq f\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq645.gif). ■

Example 1.5.5.

(a)

If ![
$$x,y \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq646.gif), then the straight line segment ![
$$\[x,y\] \\equiv \\{ ty + \(1 - t\)x: 0 \\leq t \\leq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq647.gif) is connected. In fact, t↦ty \+ (1 − t)x is a continuous function from the unit interval into ℝ q . Since the unit interval is connected, so is its image under this continuous mapping.

(b)

In ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq648.gif), the balls B(x; r) are connected. In fact, let A be a nonempty subset of B(x; r) that is both relatively open and closed, and fix a point y in A. If ![
$$z \\in B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq649.gif), then the line segment ![
$$\[y,z\] \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq650.gif) and [y, z] is connected by part (a). But it follows that ![
$$A \\cap \[y,z\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq651.gif) is a nonempty subset of this line segment that is both relatively open and relatively closed. Thus ![
$$\[y,z\] \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq652.gif); in particular, the arbitrary point z from B(x; r) belongs to A, so that ![
$$A = B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq653.gif).

(c)

If (X, d) is a discrete metric space, then X is not connected if X has more than one point. In fact, each singleton set {x} is a nonempty set that is both open and closed.

Proposition 1.5.6.

Let (X,d) be a metric space.

(a) If ![
$$\\{E_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq654.gif) is a collection of connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{i} \\cap E_{j}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq655.gif) for all i,j in I, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{i\\in I}E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq656.gif) is connected.

(b) If {E n : n ≥ 1} is a sequence of connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{n} \\cap E_{n+1}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq657.gif) for each n, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq658.gif) is connected.

Proof.

(a)

Let A be a nonempty subset of E that is relatively open and closed. If i ∈ I, then ![
$$A \\cap E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq659.gif) is a relatively closed and open subset of ![
$$E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq660.gif); if ![
$$A \\cap E_{i}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq661.gif), then the fact that E i is connected implies ![
$$E_{i} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq662.gif). Now since A is nonempty, there is at least one i such that ![
$$E_{i} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq663.gif). But then for every j in I, the hypothesis implies there is a point in E j that belongs to A; thus ![
$$E_{j} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq664.gif). Therefore, A = E and E must be connected.

(b)

Let A be a nonempty relatively open and closed subset of E. Since A ≠ ∅, there is some integer N with ![
$$A \\cap E_{N}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq665.gif). But ![
$$A \\cap E_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq666.gif) is both relatively open and closed in E N , so ![
$$E_{N} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq667.gif) by the connectedness of E N . By hypothesis, ![
$$E_{N-1} \\cap E_{N}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq668.gif), so ![
$$E_{N-1} \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq669.gif), and it follows that ![
$$E_{N-1} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq670.gif). Continuing, we get that ![
$$E_{n} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq671.gif) for 1 ≤ n ≤ N. Since ![
$$E_{N} \\cap E_{N+1}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq672.gif), similar arguments show that ![
$$E_{N+1} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq673.gif). Continuing, we get that ![
$$E_{n} \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq674.gif) for all n ≥ 1. That is, E = A, and so E is connected.

■

Corollary 1.5.7.

The union of two intersecting connected subsets of a metric space is connected.

Definition 1.5.8.

If (X, d) is a metric space, then a component of X is a maximal connected subset of X.

The word maximal in the definition means that there is no connected set that properly contains it. Thus, if C is a component of X and D is a connected subset of X with ![
$$C \\subseteq D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq675.gif), then D = C.

A component is the correct interpretation of the word part used in the introduction of this section. The set X there has two components. Notice that a connected metric space has only one component. In a discrete metric space, each singleton set is a component.

Proposition 1.5.9.

For any metric space, every connected set is contained in a component, distinct components are disjoint, and the union of all the components is the entire space.

Proof.

Fix a connected subset D of X, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq676.gif) denote the collection of all connected subsets of X that contain D. According to Proposition 1.5.6(a), ![
$$C =\\bigcup \\{ A: A \\in \\mathcal{C}_{D}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq677.gif) is connected. Clearly, C is a component and contains D. By taking D = { x} in what was just established, we have that every point of X is contained in a component so that the union of all the components is X. Finally, note that if C and D are two components and ![
$$C \\cap D\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq678.gif), then ![
$$C \\cup D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq679.gif) is connected by Corollary 1.5.7; so it must be that ![
$$C = C \\cup D = D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq680.gif) by the maximality of C and D. That is, distinct components are disjoint sets. ■

One consequence of the preceding proposition is that the components form a partition of X—they divide the space X into a collection of pairwise disjoint connected sets. The next result says that the components are all closed, the proof of which emphasizes once again that, when discussing relatively open and closed sets, you must be aware of what the universe is.

Proposition 1.5.10.

If C is a connected subset of the metric space X and ![
$$C \\subseteq Y \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq681.gif) , then Y is connected.

Proof.

Let A be a nonempty subset of Y that is both relatively open and closed, and fix a point x 0 in A. By Exercise 1.1.6, there is an open subset G of X such that ![
$$A = Y \\cap G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq682.gif). Since ![
$$x_{0} \\in \\text{cl}\\,C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq683.gif) and x 0 ∈ G, there must be a point x in ![
$$G \\cap C = A \\cap C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq684.gif); that is, ![
$$A \\cap C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq685.gif) is a nonempty relatively open subset of C. Since A is relatively closed in Y, Exercise 1.1.6 and an analogous argument imply that ![
$$A \\cap C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq686.gif) is also relatively closed in C. Since C is connected, ![
$$C = C \\cap A \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq687.gif). That is, ![
$$C \\subseteq A \\subseteq Y \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq688.gif), so that ![
$$A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq689.gif) is both closed in Y and dense in Y; hence A = Y, and it must be that Y is connected. ■

Corollary 1.5.11.

The closure of a connected set is connected and each component is closed.

In light of the preceding proposition and Example 1.5.5(b), if ![
$$x \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq690.gif) and ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq E \\subseteq \\overline{B}\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq691.gif), then E is connected. Here is an example that will illustrate additional properties as we proceed. In fact this example is used so often it has a name, the topologist's sine curve.

Example 1.5.12.

![
$$X =\\{ \(x,\\sin {x}^{-1}\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: 0 < x \\leq 1\\} \\cup \\{ \(0,0\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq692.gif) is connected. In fact, ![
$$f: \(0,1\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq693.gif) defined by ![
$$f\(x\) = \(x,\\sin {x}^{-1}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq694.gif), is a continuous function, so C = f((0, 1]) is connected. Since ![
$$C \\subseteq X \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,C$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq695.gif), X is connected by Proposition 1.5.10. The space X consists of the graph of the function sinx −1 for 0 < x ≤ 1 together with the origin. Note that instead of the origin, we could have added to the graph any point or any subset of {(0, y): −1 ≤ y ≤ 1}, and the resulting set would still be connected.

Definition 1.5.13.

If E is a subset of X, x, y ∈ E, and ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq696.gif), say that there is an ε-chain from x to y in E when there is a finite number of points x 1,..., x n in E such that: (i) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, ![
$$B\(x_{k};\\epsilon \) \\subseteq E$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq697.gif); (ii) for 2 ≤ k ≤ n, ![
$$x_{k-1} \\in B\(x_{k};\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq698.gif); (iii) x 1 = x and x n = y.

The concept of an ε-chain has limited value in an arbitrary metric space. For one thing, note that conditions (i) and (ii) of the definition imply that only points in the interior of E can be linked by an ε-chain.

Example 1.5.14.

If r > 0 and ![
$$z \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq699.gif), then for any pair of points x and y in B(z; r) and all sufficiently small ε there is an ε-chain in B(z; r) from x to y. In fact, observe that the straight line segment from x to y is contained in B(z; r), and using this it is easy to construct the ![
$$\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq700.gif)-chain.

Proposition 1.5.15.

Consider the metric space ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq701.gif) .

(a) If G is an open subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq702.gif) , then every component of G is open and there are countably many components.

(b) An open subset G of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq703.gif) is connected if and only if for any x,y in G there is an ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq704.gif) such that there is an ε-chain in G from x to y.

Proof.

(a)

If H is a component of G and x ∈ H, choose r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq705.gif). Since B(x; r) is also connected (Example 1.5.5), Corollary 1.5.7 implies ![
$$H \\cup B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq706.gif) is connected. Since this is also a subset of G, it follows that ![
$$H = H \\cup B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq707.gif), so ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\subseteq H$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq708.gif), and H is open. Because ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq709.gif) is separable, there is a countable dense subset D. Now since each component is open, each component contains an element of D and different components contain different points. If there are uncountably many components, this would show that there is an uncountable subset of D (Why?), which is nonsense.

(b)

Assume that G satisfies the stated condition, and let us prove that G is connected. Fix x and let H be the component of G that contains x; we want to show that H = G. By part (a), we know that H is open. If y ∈ G, then there is an ε > 0 such that there is an ε-chain x 1,..., x n in G from x to y. Since ![
$$x_{k-1} \\in B\(x_{k-1};\\epsilon \) \\cap B\(x_{k};\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq710.gif) for 2 ≤ k ≤ n, Proposition 1.5.6(b) says ![
$$B =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}B\(x_{k};\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq711.gif) is connected. Condition (i) of the definition of an ε-chain implies ![
$$B \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq712.gif), and so ![
$$B \\subseteq H$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq713.gif). In particular, y ∈ H. Since y was arbitrary, H = G and G is connected.

Now assume that G is connected. Fix a point x in G, and let

![
$$\\displaystyle{D =\\{ y \\in G: \\text{ there is an }\\epsilon > 0\\mbox{ and an $\\epsilon $-chain in $G$ from }x\\text{ to }y\\}}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equx.gif)

The strategy of the proof will be to show that D is both relatively open and closed in G; since it is not empty (x ∈ D), it will then follow that D = G, and so G will have been shown to satisfy the condition. If y ∈ D, then let ε > 0 and let x 1,..., x n be an ε-chain from x to y. It follows from the definition of an ε-chain that ![
$$B\(y;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq714.gif). Thus, D is open. Now suppose ![
$$z \\in G \\cap \\text{cl}\\,D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq715.gif)—this is the relative closure of D in G. (Why?) Choose r > 0 such that ![
$$B\(z;r\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq716.gif), so ![
$$B\(z;r\) \\cup D \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq717.gif). Since ![
$$z \\in \\text{cl}\\,D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq718.gif), there is a point y in ![
$$B\(z;r\) \\cap D$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq719.gif). Let ![
$$x_{0} = x,x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq720.gif) be an ε-chain from x to y. By Exercise 8, there is an ![
$${\\epsilon }^{{\\prime} }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq721.gif)-chain from x to y in G whenever ![
$$0 <{ \\epsilon }^{{\\prime}} < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq722.gif). Applying this with ![
$$0 <{ \\epsilon }^{{\\prime}} <\\min \\{ \\epsilon,r\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq723.gif), we may assume ε < r. Using Example 1.5.14 we see that this implies there is an ε-chain in G from x to z. Thus z ∈ D, and so D is relatively closed in G.

■

We note that (i) of the definition of an ε-chain was used to establish that the condition in part (b) was sufficient for connectedness. Without this, the result is false, as we see in the following example.

Example 1.5.16.

Let ![
$$X =\\{ \(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: y > {x}^{-1}\\} \\cup \\{ \(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: y < 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq724.gif). Clearly, X is not connected; in fact, it has two components. It is also easy to see that if ![
$$\\bar{x} = \(x,y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq725.gif) with y < 0 and ![
$$\\bar{y} = \(w,z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq726.gif) with z > w −1, then for all sufficiently small ε there are points ![
$$\\bar{x}_{1},\\ldots,\\bar{x}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq727.gif) in X such that for 2 ≤ k ≤ n, ![
$$\\bar{x}_{k-1} \\in B\(\\bar{x}_{k};\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq728.gif), and ![
$$\\bar{x}_{1} =\\bar{ x}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq729.gif) and ![
$$\\bar{x}_{n} =\\bar{ y}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq730.gif).

Here is another example.

Example 1.5.17.

If ![
$$X = \\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq731.gif), the set of rational numbers, then ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq732.gif) is not connected. For example, ![
$$A = \\mathbb{Q} \\cap \(\\pi,\\infty \)$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq733.gif) is a subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq734.gif) that is both open and closed. On the other hand, ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq735.gif) satisfies the condition stated in Proposition 1.5.15(b). Thus, the assumption there that G is open is essential.

We close this section with a circumspective remark. We did not use sequences in this section. Connectedness is one of the only properties of a metric space I know whose examination never uses the concept of a convergent sequence.

## Exercises

(1)

Prove that open and half-open intervals are connected, completing the proof of half of Proposition 1.5.2.

(2)

Is a proof required of the fact, used in the proof of Proposition 1.5.2, that a subset E of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq736.gif) is an interval if and only if when a, b ∈ E and a < b, then c ∈ E whenever a < c < b?

(3)

If (X, d) is connected and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq737.gif) is a continuous function such that | f(x) | = 1 for all x in X, show that f must be constant.

(4)

If A is a subset of X, define the characteristic function of A as the function ![
$$\\chi _{A}: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq738.gif) such that χ A (x) = 1 when x ∈ A and χ A (x) = 0 when x∉A. Show that A is simultaneously open and closed if and only if χ A is continuous.

(5)

Look at the preceding exercise for the definition of the characteristic function on a set X. (a) If A and B are subsets of X, which function is χ A χ B ? (b) Which function is χ A +χ B ? (c) What is the characteristic function of the empty set?

(6)

Let I be any nonempty set and for each i in I let X i be a copy of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq739.gif) with the metric d i (x, y) = | x − y | . Let X be the disjoint union of the sets X i . (That is a verbal description that can be used in any circumstance, but if you want precision, you can say ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R} \\times I$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq740.gif), the cartesian product where I has the discrete metric.) Define a metric d on X by letting d agree with d i on X i ; and when x ∈ X i , y ∈ X j , and i ≠ j, then d(x, y) = 1. (Is this the product metric on ![
$$\\mathbb{R} \\times I$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq741.gif)?) (a) Show that d is indeed a metric on X. (b) Show that ![
$$\\{X_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq742.gif) is the collection of components of X and each of these components is an open subset of X. (c) Show that (X, d) is separable if and only if I is a countable set.

(7)

Can you think of any way to generalize Proposition 1.5.6(a) and obtain a theorem whose conclusion is that ![
$$E =\\bigcup \\{ E_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq743.gif) is connected.

(8)

If E is a subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq744.gif), x, y ∈ E, and ε > 0 such that there is an ε-chain from x to y, show that for any ε ′ with 0 < ε ′ < ε there is an ε ′ -chain from x to y.

(9)

A polygon ![
$$\[x,x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n-1},y\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq745.gif) in Euclidean space is the union of straight line segments

![
$$\\displaystyle{\[x,x_{1}\],\[x_{1},x_{2}\],\\ldots,\[x_{n-1},y\].}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equy.gif)

(a)

Show that a polygon is a connected subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq746.gif). (b) Show that an open subset G of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq747.gif) is connected if and only if for any two points x and y in G there is a polygon ![
$$\[x,x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n-1},y\]$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq748.gif) contained in G.

## 1.6 The Baire Category Theorem

The Baire Category Theorem is a useful result that can prove the existence, in fact profusion, of certain objects. That is an abstract statement that has little meaning unless you see some specific instances. One such instance is that it can be used to show that there is a continuous nowhere differentiable function on an interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq749.gif). In fact, it actually shows that the set of such functions is dense in the metric space C[0, 1]. See [5, Theorem 17.8].

It is also used to go from statements that involve one point at a time in a metric space to statements that give a uniform condition. Again, that is rather bare of meaning without a specific instance, but supplying one is beyond the scope of this book. Trust me. If you stick with mathematics a little longer, you will see such things as the Principle of Uniform Boundedness in functional analysis whose proof is a standard application of the Baire Category Theorem. (For example, see [2].) We will encounter a use of the Baire Category Theorem below in Exercise 2.2.6, however, when we explore abstract topological spaces in the next chapter.

Theorem 1.6.1 (Baire7 Category Theorem).

If (X,d) is a complete metric space and {U n } is a sequence of open subsets of X each of which is dense, then ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq750.gif) is dense.

Proof.

To show that ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq751.gif) is dense, it suffices to show that if G is a nonempty open subset of X, then ![
$$G \\cap \\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq752.gif). (Why?) Since U 1 is dense and open, there is an open ball B(x 1; r 1) with r 1 < 1 such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x_{1};r_{1}\) \\subseteq G \\cap U_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq753.gif). This is the first step in an induction argument that establishes that for each n ≥ 2 there is an open ball B(x n ; r n ) with r n < n −1 such that

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{cl}\\,B\(x_{n};r_{n}\) \\subseteq B\(x_{n-1};r_{n-1}\) \\cap U_{n} \\subseteq G.}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equz.gif)

The details of this induction argument are left as Exercise 1. Observe that this implies that when n > N,

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\text{cl}\\,B\(x_{n};r_{n}\) \\subseteq B\(x_{N};r_{N}\) \\cap U_{N} \\subseteq G \\cap U_{N}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_Equ6.gif)

(1.6.2)

Let N be an arbitrary positive integer. It follows from (1.6.2) that for n, m ≥ N, d(x n , x m ) < 2N −1, and so {x n } is a Cauchy sequence. Since X is complete, there is an x in X such that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq754.gif). The same observation (1.6.2) reveals that when n > m > N, ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,B\(x_{n};r_{n}\) \\subseteq B\(x_{m};r_{m}\) \\subseteq G \\cap U_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq755.gif). Since N was arbitrary, ![
$$x \\in G \\cap \\bigcap _{N=1}^{\\infty }U_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq756.gif). Thus, this last set is nonempty, and so we have established density. ■

The next result is the form of the theorem that is used very often and is also referred to as the Baire Category Theorem.

Corollary 1.6.3.

If (X,d) is a complete metric space and {F n } is a sequence of closed subsets such that ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq757.gif) , then there is an n ![
$$\\text{int}\\,$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq758.gif) F n ≠ ∅.

Proof.

Suppose int F n = ∅ for each n ≥ 1, and put ![
$$U_{n} = X\\setminus F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq759.gif). It follows that each U n is open and dense, so the theorem implies that ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq760.gif) is dense. But the hypothesis of this corollary implies that this intersection is empty, a dramatic contradiction. ■

Why is the word category used in the name of this theorem? Please excuse the author while he takes a little time to rant. Mathematics loves tradition; largely this is good and has my support. However, occasionally it adopts a word, makes it a definition, and promulgates it far beyond its usefulness. Such is the case here. There is a concept in topology called a set of the first category. Sets that are not of the first category are said to be of the second category. What are the definitions? I won't tell you. If you are curious, you can look them up, but it will not be helpful. I will say, however, that using this terminology the Baire Category Theorem says that a complete metric space is of the second category. My objection stems from the fact that this "category" terminology does not convey any sense of what the concept is. There is another pair of terms that is used: meager or thin and comeager or thick. These at least convey some sense of what the terms mean. But I see no reason to learn additional terminology. The theorem as stated previously says what it says, end of story—and end of rant.

## Exercises

(1)

Supply the details of the induction argument used in the proof of Theorem 1.6.1.

(2)

Say that a set E in a metric space is nowhere dense if ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\[\\text{cl}\\,E\] = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq761.gif). (Why is the term nowhere used?) If (X, d) is a complete metric space and ![
$$A =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq762.gif), where each E n is nowhere dense, show that ![
$$X\\setminus A$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq763.gif) is dense in X.

(3)

If (X, d) is a complete metric space and ![
$$A_{1},A_{2},\\ldots$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq764.gif) are subsets of X such that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,\\left \[\\bigcup _{n=1}^{\\infty }A_{n}\\right \]\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq765.gif), then there is an integer n such that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A_{n}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq766.gif).

(4)

Show that a closed interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq767.gif) cannot be written as a countable union of closed subsets that are pairwise disjoint.

Footnotes

1

Augustin Louis Cauchy was born in Paris in August 1789, a month after the storming of the Bastille. He was educated in engineering, and his first job was in 1810 working on the port facilities at Cherbourg in preparation for Napoleon's contemplated invasion of England. In 1812 he returned to Paris, and his energies shifted toward mathematics. His contributions were monumental, with a plethora of results bearing his name. His collected works fill 27 published volumes. As a human being he left much to be desired. He was highly religious with a totally dogmatic personality, often treating others with dismissive rudeness. Two famous examples were his treatment of Abel and Galois: he refused to consider their highly significant works, which they had submitted to him. Both Abel and Galois died young. Perhaps better treatment by Cauchy would have given them some recognition that would have resulted in a longer life and a productive career to the betterment of mathematics; we'll never know. He had two doctoral students, one of whom was Bunyakowsky. Cauchy died in 1857 in Sceaux near Paris.

2

Hermann Amandus Schwarz was a German mathematician born in 1843 in Hermsdorf, Silesia, now part of Poland. He began his studies at Berlin in chemistry but switched to mathematics and received his doctorate in 1864 under the direction of Weierstrass. He held positions at Halle, Zurich, Göttingen, and Berlin. His work centered on various geometry problems that were deeply connected to analysis. This included work on surfaces and conformal mappings in analytic function theory, any student of which will see his name in prominence. He died in Berlin in 1921.

3

Georg Cantor was the child of an international family. His father was born in Denmark and his mother was Russian; he himself was born in 1845 in St. Petersburg, where his father was a successful merchant and stockbroker. Cantor is recognized as the father of set theory, having invented cardinal and ordinal numbers and proved that the irrational numbers are uncountable. He received his doctorate from the University of Berlin in 1867 and spent most of his career at the University of Halle. His work was a watershed event in mathematics, but it was condemned by many prominent contemporary mathematicians. The work was simply too radical, with counterintuitive results such as ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq191.gif) and ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_1_Chapter_IEq192.gif) having the same number of points. He began to suffer from depression around 1884. This progressed and plagued him the rest of his life. He died in a sanatorium in Halle in 1918.

4

Pavel Samuilovich Urysohn was born in 1898 in Odessa, Ukraine. He was awarded his habilitation in June 1921 from the University of Moscow, where he remained as an instructor. He began his work in analysis but switched to topology, in which he made several important contributions, especially in developing a theory of dimension. His work attracted attention from the mathematicians of the day, and in 1924 he set out for a tour of the major universities in Germany, Holland, and France, meeting with Hausdorff, Hilbert, and others. That same year, while swimming off the coast of Brittany, France, he drowned. He is buried in Batz-sur-Mer in Brittany. In just 3 years he left his mark on mathematics.

5

Heinrich Eduard Heine was born in 1821 in Berlin, the eighth of nine children. He received his doctorate in 1842 from Berlin; in 1844 he received the habilitation at Bonn, where he was appointed a privatdozent. In 1856 he was made professor at the University of Halle, where he remained for the rest of his career. In 1850 he married Sophie Wolff from Berlin, and over the years they had five children. He worked on partial differential equations and then on special functions—Legendre polynomials, Lamé functions, and Bessel functions. He made significant contributions to spherical harmonics and introduced the concept of uniform continuity. It was in 1872 that he gave a proof of the present theorem, and it requires scholarship to discover the difference in contribution to this result between him and Borel, who published it in 1895. He died in 1881 in Halle.

6

Emile Borel was born in Saint Affrique in the south of France in 1871. He published his first two papers in 1890, 2 years before receiving his doctorate in Paris and joining the faculty at Lille. He returned to Paris in 1897. In 1909 a special Chair in the Theory of Functions was created for him at the Sorbonne. During World War I he was very supportive of his country and was put in charge of a central department of research. He also spent time at the front, and in 1918 he was awarded the Croix de Guerre. In 1928 he set up the Institute Henri Poincaré. He was one of the founders of the modern theory of functions, along with Baire and Lebesgue, and he also worked on divergent series, complex variables, probability, and game theory. He continued to be very active in the French government, serving in the French Chamber of Deputies (1924–1936) and as Minister of the Navy (1925–1940). He died in 1956 in Paris.

7

René-Louis Baire was born in Paris in 1874. His father was a tailor and his family was poor. He won a scholarship, however, that enabled him to receive an excellent education, and he distinguished himself from the start. Soon he gained admission to the prestigious École Normale Supérieure. After this education and further study in mathematics, he obtained a position as a professor at a lycée and began the research that led him to the introduction of his classification of functions of a real variable. He was awarded another scholarship that enabled him to study in Italy, where he met Volterra. His dissertation on discontinuous functions earned him a doctorate in 1899, and in 1901 he secured a position on the faculty at the University of Montpelier. Throughout his life he suffered from poor health, though this did not keep him from his research. In 1907 he was promoted to a professorship at Dijon, but his health interfered with his teaching duties. In 1914 he requested a leave and traveled to Lausanne, Switzerland. While he was there, World War I began, and he was unable to return to France.
