His health deteriorated further, depression ensued, and he spent the rest of his life on the shores of Lac Léman in Switzerland. It was there that he received the Chevalier de la Legion d'Honneur, and in 1922 he was elected to the Académie des Sciences. He published significant works on number theory and functions. He died in 1932 at Chambéry near Geneva.
John B. ConwayUndergraduate Texts in MathematicsA Course in Point Set Topology201410.1007/978-3-319-02368-7_2

© Springer International Publishing Switzerland 2014

# 2. Topological Spaces

John B. Conway1

(1)

Department of Mathematics, The George Washington University, Washington, DC, USA

Abstract

In this chapter we will abstract certain properties of a metric space and thus create a new structure that occurs frequently in mathematics. Then we will extend to this new structure the notions of a continuous function, compactness, and connectedness. As we progress through this chapter we will frequently use metric spaces to illustrate the new concepts, and readers are encouraged to pursue such issues on their own.

In this chapter we will abstract certain properties of a metric space and thus create a new structure that occurs frequently in mathematics. Then we will extend to this new structure the notions of a continuous function, compactness, and connectedness. As we progress through this chapter we will frequently use metric spaces to illustrate the new concepts, and readers are encouraged to pursue such issues on their own.

Some good references for this material, as well as for what is in the next chapter, are [4] and [6]. A source of examples and counterexamples besides what is in these references is [11].

## 2.1 Definitions and Examples

Definition 2.1.1.

A topological space is a pair of objects ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq1.gif), where X is a set and ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq2.gif) is a collection of subsets of X satisfying the following conditions:

(a)

![
$$\\varnothing,X \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq3.gif);

(b)

if ![
$$\\{G_{i}: i \\in I\\} \\subseteq \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq4.gif), then ![
$$\\bigcup _{i\\in I}G_{i} \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq5.gif);

(c)

if ![
$$G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n} \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq6.gif), then ![
$$\\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq7.gif).

The collection ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq8.gif) is called the topology on X and sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq9.gif) are called open sets.

Example 2.1.2.

(a)

If (X, d) is a metric space and ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq10.gif) denotes the open sets defined by d as in § 1.1, then ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq11.gif) is a topological space.

(b)

If X is any set and ![
$$\\mathcal{T} = {2}^{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq12.gif), the collection of all subsets of X, then ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq13.gif) is a topological space. This topology is called the discrete topology on X. In fact, this example is a special case of the preceding one if we let d be the discrete metric on X.

(c)

If X is any set and ![
$$\\mathcal{T} =\\{ \\varnothing,X\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq14.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq15.gif) is a topology on X called the trivial topology. This topology does not arise from a metric if X has at least two points. (Why?)

(d)

Let ![
$$\\{\(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\): i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq16.gif) be a collection of topological spaces, where the sets {X i : i ∈ I} are pairwise disjoint subsets of some larger set that we can take to be ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{i\\in I}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq17.gif). (This idea may strike the reader as a bit strange, but we will see more specific examples of this phenomenon as we progress.) Let ![
$$\\mathcal{T} =\\{ G \\subseteq X: G \\cap X_{i} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{i}\\text{ for each }i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq18.gif). ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq19.gif) is a topological space.

(e)

Let X be any set, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq20.gif) be the collection of all subsets G such that ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq21.gif) is finite. It follows that ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq22.gif) is a topology on X called the cofinal topology. We won't see much of this topology.

(f)

If ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq23.gif) is a topological space, ![
$$Y \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq24.gif), and ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{Y } \\equiv \\{ Y \\cap G: G \\in \\mathcal{T}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq25.gif), then ![
$$\(Y,\\mathcal{T}_{Y }\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq26.gif) is a topological space. ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{Y }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq27.gif) is called the subspace topology or relative topology defined by ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq28.gif) on Y. We note that this is consistent with what we did when discussing subspaces of a metric space. That is, if (X, d) is a metric space, ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq29.gif) denotes the open sets in X, and ![
$$Y \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq30.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{Y }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq31.gif) is precisely the set of open subsets of Y obtained by restricting the metric d to Y. See Proposition 1.1.8.

Definition 2.1.3.

![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq32.gif) is called a 1 space provided for any pair of distinct points x, y in X where there are disjoint open sets U, V such that x ∈ U and y ∈ V.

It is easy to see that the trivial topological space is not a Hausdorff space, nor is the cofinal topology on any infinite set, but every metric space is Hausdorff. In fact, if (X, d) is a metric space, x ≠ y, and ![
$$0 < r < \\frac{1} {2}d\(x,y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq33.gif), then ![
$$B\(x;r\) \\cap B\(y;r\) = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq34.gif). Topological spaces that are not Hausdorff arise naturally in certain parts of mathematics. Most mathematicians, however, will not encounter them very often; analysts never will. When a space fails to be Hausdorff, pathologies prevail. So we are going to avoid them and settle on the following agreement.

Agreement. All topological spaces encountered in this book will be assumed to be Hausdorff.

Incidentally, another term for Hausdorff is a T2-space. The reason for this term is part of what are called separation axioms or properties. Yes, there is a T1-space and even a T0-space as well as T3 and T4. It is a long story. We are going to avoid this terminology; it just strikes me as not conveying anything except, possibly, a hierarchical code. Nevertheless, the underlying concepts will be seen subsequently in Sects. 3.​2 and 3.​3.​ If you are interested in a more thorough inspection of these properties, an examination of [4] or [6] will satisfy.

The reader may have expected this definition: a subset F of ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq35.gif) is closed if ![
$$X\\setminus F \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq36.gif). Again, we have taken the definition straight from metric spaces.

Proposition 2.1.4.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq37.gif) be a topological space, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq38.gif) denote the collection of closed subsets of X.

(a)  ![
$$\\varnothing,X \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq39.gif) .

(b) If ![
$$\\{F_{i}: i \\in I\\} \\subseteq \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq40.gif) , then ![
$$\\bigcap _{i\\in I}F_{i} \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq41.gif) .

(c) If ![
$$F_{1},\\ldots,F_{n} \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq42.gif) , then ![
$$\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}F_{k} \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq43.gif) .

(d)  ![
$$\\{x\\} \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq44.gif) for every x in X.

The proof of this is rather straightforward and left to the reader, though we will mention that the proof of (d) uses the Hausdorff property, whereas the proofs of the first three do not.

We continue to use the metric space development from the preceding chapter as a guide for developing the theory of topological spaces. This will be our pattern for the near future. When a proof of something for a topological space is similar to the proof of the corresponding result in metric spaces, we will not present it but refer to its metric space counterpart. Here we begin with a verbatim definition.

Definition 2.1.5.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq45.gif) be a topological space, and let A be a subset of X. The interior of A, denoted by ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq46.gif), is the set defined by ![
$$\\text{int}\\,A =\\bigcup \\{ G: G\\text{ is open and }G \\subseteq A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq47.gif). The closure of A, denoted by ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq48.gif), is the set defined by ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A =\\bigcap \\{ F: F\\text{ is a closed set and }A \\subseteq F\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq49.gif). The boundary of A, denoted by ![
$$\\partial A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq50.gif), is the set defined by ![
$$\\partial A = \\text{cl}\\,A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,\(X\\setminus A\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq51.gif).

Proposition 2.1.6.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq52.gif) be a topological space, and assume that ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq53.gif).

(a)

![
$$x \\in \\text{int}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq54.gif) if and only if there is an open set G with ![
$$x \\in G \\subseteq A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq55.gif) .

(b)

![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq56.gif) if and only if for every open set G that contains x we have that ![
$$G \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq57.gif) .

(c)

![
$$\\text{int}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq58.gif) is the largest open set contained in A.

(d)

![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq59.gif) is the smallest closed set that contains A.

For the proof of (a) and (b) look at the proof of Proposition 1.1.13. The proof of (c) and (d) is Exercise 5.

Proposition 2.1.7.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq60.gif) be a topological space, and let A be a subset of X.

(a) A is closed if and only if ![
$$A = \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq61.gif) .

(b) A is open if and only if ![
$$A = \\text{int}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq62.gif) .

(c) If ![
$$A_{1},\\ldots,A_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq63.gif) are subsets of X, then ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,\\left \[\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}A_{k}\\right \] =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}\\text{cl}\\,A_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq64.gif) .

Consult Proposition 1.1.15 and Exercise 1.1.7.

Definition 2.1.8.

A subset E of a topological space ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq65.gif) is dense if ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq66.gif) E = X. A topological space is separable if it has a countable dense subset.

Using Proposition 2.1.6 we easily get the following extension of Proposition 1.1.20.

Proposition 2.1.9.

A set A is dense in ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq67.gif) if and only if for every x in X and every open set G that contains x we have that ![
$$G \\cap E\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq68.gif) .

We also have the corresponding definition of a limit point.

Definition 2.1.10.

If ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq69.gif), a point x in X is called a limit point of A if for every open set G that contains x there is a point a in ![
$$G \\cap A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq70.gif) different from x. In other words, ![
$$\[G\\setminus \\{x\\}\] \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq71.gif).

We can define the concept of a convergent sequence in a topological space as follows: ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq72.gif) in ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq73.gif) if and only if for each open set G containing x there is an N such that x n ∈ G for all n ≥ N. This, however, has only marginal value. For example, the sequential characterization of limit points given in Proposition 1.2.7 is not true in this more general setting. Later we will generalize the idea of a sequence, and this will turn out to have the utility in a topological space that sequences have in a metric space. Right now we want to extend Proposition 1.2.7 to a topological space, but we need to supply a proof because the one given for this result in the metric space setting relies on sequences. A convenient term in a topological space is a neighborhood of a point x, which is an open set G that contains x. (By a neighborhood of x some authors mean a set E that contains x in its interior. This has value and convenience, but we will stick with the definition given before this comment.)

Proposition 2.1.11.

Let A be a subset of X.

(a) The set A is closed if and only if it contains all its limit points.

(b)  ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A = A \\cup \\{ x: x\\text{ is a limit point of }A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq74.gif) .

Proof.

Part (a) will follow from (b) if we first show that a limit point of the set of limit points of A is a limit point of A (Exercise 8). So we only prove (b). Let ![
$$B = A \\cup \\{ x: x\\text{ is a limit point of }A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq75.gif). Assume x is a limit point of A. If G is a neighborhood of x, then ![
$$G \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq76.gif), so by definition ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq77.gif). Thus ![
$$B \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq78.gif). On the other hand, if ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq79.gif), then for every neighborhood G of x, ![
$$G \\cap A\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq80.gif). If x is not a limit point, then for some neighborhood G we have that ![
$$G \\cap A =\\{ x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq81.gif), implying x ∈ A. Thus, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,A \\subseteq B$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq82.gif). ■

Proposition 2.1.12.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq83.gif) be a topological space, let Y be a subset of X, and give Y its subspace topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{Y }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq84.gif) .

(a) A subset A of Y is closed in Y if and only if there is a closed subset F of X such that ![
$$A = F \\cap Y$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq85.gif) .

(b) If ![
$$A \\subseteq Y$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq86.gif) , ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,_{Y }A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq87.gif) denotes the closure of A in Y, and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,_{X}A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq88.gif) denotes the closure of A in X, then ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,_{Y }A = Y \\cap \\text{cl}\\,_{X}A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq89.gif) .

(c) If ![
$$A \\subseteq Y$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq90.gif) , ![
$$\\text{int}\\,_{Y }A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq91.gif) denotes the interior of A in Y, and ![
$$\\text{int}\\,_{X}A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq92.gif) denotes the interior of A in X, then ![
$$Y \\cap \\text{int}\\,_{X}A \\subseteq \\text{int}\\,_{Y }A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq93.gif) .

Proof.

Exercise 9, which also has a comment about part (c). ■

We close this section with a brief definition to be discussed more fully later.

Definition 2.1.13.

A topological space ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq94.gif) is metrizable if there is a metric defined on X such that the open sets defined by this metric are precisely the sets ![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq95.gif).

Needless to say, every metric space is metrizable, but inherent conditions that are necessary and sufficient for a topological space to be metrizable are a challenge to discover. This will be discussed at the end of § 3.7. There are Hausdorff topological spaces that are not metrizable, but we will have to wait for that as well. In Exercise 2.2.6, an example of a space is given that has both a metric ρ and a topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq96.gif)​​, and, with considerable effort, it is shown that the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq97.gif) is not defined by the metric ρ. In fact, that topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq98.gif) is not metrizable, but, as far as I know, showing this requires techniques beyond the scope of this book. In fact, mathematics is filled with topological spaces that are not metrizable, but showing this lack of metrizability is involved.

## Exercises

(1)

Prove that for any topological space X and any point x in X, {x} is a closed set.

(2)

Verify the statement in Example 2.1.2(d).

(3)

Verify the statements made in Example 2.1.2(f).

(4)

Prove Proposition 2.1.4(d).

(5)

Prove Proposition 2.1.6.

(6)

Prove Proposition 2.1.7.

(7)

For the moment disregard the agreement at the start of this section that all topological spaces are Hausdorff. Show that a topological space ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq99.gif) is Hausdorff if and only if for any two distinct points x and y there is an open set G such that y ∈ G and ![
$$x\\notin \\text{cl}\\,G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq100.gif).

(8)

If A is a subset of X and L is the set of limit points of A, prove that any limit point of L is a limit point of A. Is L a closed set?

(9)

(a) Prove Proposition 2.1.12. (b) Find an example of a topological space ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq101.gif), a subset Y of X, and a nonempty set U in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{Y }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq102.gif) such that ![
$$\\text{int}\\,_{X}U = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq103.gif). [Thus, we have a dramatic example showing that equality in part (c) of Proposition 2.1.12 does not always occur.]

(10)

Let X denote the set of all sequences of real numbers ![
$$\\{x_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq104.gif), and let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq105.gif) consist of all subsets G of X satisfying the following condition: for each x = { x n } in G there are integers ![
$$n_{1} < \\cdots < n_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq106.gif) and an ε > 0 such that ![
$$\\left \\{y =\\{ y_{n}\\} \\in X: \\vert x_{n_{k}} - y_{n_{k}}\\vert < \\epsilon \\text{ for }1 \\leq k \\leq N\\right \\} \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq107.gif). (a) Show that ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq108.gif) is a topological space. (b) Is there a metric on X such that ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq109.gif) is the collection of open sets for this metric?

## 2.2 Base and Subbase for a Topology

We want to invent ways to generate a topology on a set. This is the purpose of the present section, and here is the first of two ways we will do this.

Definition 2.2.1.

If X is a set, a collection ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq110.gif) of subsets of X is a base for a topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq111.gif) if every set G in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq112.gif) is the union of some collection of sets belonging to ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq113.gif).

This is a start, but it is not quite what we are aiming for. We will see the virtues of this concept and a few of its drawbacks and then encounter another way to generate topologies.

Example 2.2.2.

(a)

If ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq114.gif) is a topological space, then ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq115.gif) is a base for itself.

(b)

If (X, d) is a metric space, then ![
$$\\mathcal{B} = \\left \\{B\(x;r\): x \\in X,r > 0\\right \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq116.gif) is a base for the topology on X. So we certainly have that the concept of a base covers our important collection of examples.

(c)

If (X, d) is a metric space, then ![
$$\\mathcal{B} = \\left \\{B\(x;r\): x \\in X,r > 0\\text{ and }r \\in \\mathbb{Q}\\right \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq117.gif) is a base for the topology on X. Thus we see that a base is not unique. Rather than use the positive numbers in ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq118.gif) we could have only chosen the radii n −1 with ![
$$n \\in \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq119.gif); or we could have restricted the possibilities for x so that they come from some prechosen dense set. Thus we have even more varieties of a base. We revisit this in Corollary 2.2.4 below.

The next result presents a base in a form we can use to generate a topology.

Proposition 2.2.3.

If ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq120.gif) is a base for a topology on X, then ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq121.gif) satisfies the following:

(a)

![
$$\\bigcup \\{B: B \\in \\mathcal{B}\\} = X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq122.gif) ;

(b)

if ![
$$B_{1},B_{2} \\in \\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq123.gif) and ![
$$x \\in B_{1} \\cap B_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq124.gif) , then there is a B in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq125.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in B \\subseteq B_{1} \\cap B_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq126.gif) ;

(c)

if x and y are distinct points in X, then there are sets A,B in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq127.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in A$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq128.gif) , y ∈ B, and ![
$$A \\cap B = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq129.gif) .

Conversely, if ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq130.gif) is a collection of subsets of X satisfying these three conditions, then ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq131.gif) is a base for a unique Hausdorff topology on X.

Proof.

Assume ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq132.gif) is a base for the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq133.gif). Clearly (a) holds since ![
$$X \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq134.gif). Also ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\subseteq \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq135.gif), so when ![
$$B_{1},B_{2} \\in \\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq136.gif), ![
$$B_{1} \\cap B_{2} \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq137.gif) (though possibly not in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq138.gif)) and is thus the union of sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq139.gif); hence (b) holds. If x and y are distinct points in X, then the fact that ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq140.gif) is Hausdorff implies there are neighborhoods G and H of x and y, respectively, such that ![
$$G \\cap H = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq141.gif). From the definition of a base there are A, B in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq142.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in A \\subseteq G,y \\in B \\subseteq H$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq143.gif). Thus (c) holds.

Now for the converse. Assume ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq144.gif) is a collection of subsets of X satisfying the three properties, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq145.gif) be the collection of all subsets of X that are the union of some subcollection of sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq146.gif). Clearly, ![
$$X \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq147.gif), and by taking the union of the sets belonging to the empty subcollection of ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq148.gif) we have that ![
$$\\varnothing \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq149.gif). (Do you understand this last statement?) By using condition (b) and doing an induction argument we see that ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq150.gif) is closed under finite intersections. If ![
$$\\{G_{i}: i \\in I\\} \\subseteq \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq151.gif), then each G i is the union of sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq152.gif) so that the same holds for ![
$$\\bigcup _{i\\in I}G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq153.gif). Finally, (c) implies that the Hausdorff property holds. Therefore, ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq154.gif) is a topology. An examination of how ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq155.gif) is defined shows that it is the only topology for which ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq156.gif) is a base. (Or we could use Exercise 1.) ■

When ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq157.gif) satisfies the three conditions of the preceding proposition we call ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq158.gif) the topology generated by ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq159.gif). Recall the definition of a separable metric space (Definition 1.1.18).

Corollary 2.2.4.

If (X,d) is separable, ![
$$\\{a_{1},a_{2},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq160.gif) is a dense subset of X, and ![
$$\\{r_{1},r_{2},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq161.gif) is an enumeration of the rational numbers in the open unit interval, then ![
$$\\mathcal{B} =\\{ B\(a_{n};r_{m}\): n,m \\geq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq162.gif) is a base for the topology on X.

Proof.

We show that the three conditions of the preceding proposition are satisfied. Clearly (a) holds. If ![
$$x \\in B\(a_{j};r_{p}\) \\cap B\(a_{k};r_{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq163.gif), choose an r m with ![
$$0 < r_{m} < \\frac{1} {2}\\text{dist}\\,\[x,X\\setminus \(B\(a_{j};r_{p}\) \\cap B\(a_{k};r_{q}\)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq164.gif). Because ![
$$\\{a_{1},a_{2},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq165.gif) is dense, there is an a n in B(x; r m ). It follows that ![
$$B\(a_{n};r_{m}\) \\subseteq B\(a_{j};r_{p}\) \\cap B\(a_{k};r_{q}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq166.gif). Thus, (b) holds. The proof that (c) is true follows in a similar way and is left as an exercise. This shows that ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq167.gif) is a base and, therefore, generates some topology. The fact that it generates the topology defined by the metric is left for the reader to verify. ■

The proof of the next corollary is immediate.

Corollary 2.2.5.

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq168.gif) be any collection of subsets of an arbitrary set X such that:

(a)  ![
$$X =\\bigcup \\{ S: S \\in \\mathcal{S}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq169.gif) ;

(b) for any pair of distinct points x,y in X there are disjoint sets S,T in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq170.gif) such that x ∈ S and ![
$$y \\in T$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq171.gif) .

If ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq172.gif) consists of all finite intersections of sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq173.gif) , then ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq174.gif) is a base for a topology.

This leads to the following definition, which is our second way of generating a topology.

Definition 2.2.6.

If ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq175.gif) is any collection of subsets of an arbitrary set X having properties (a) and (b) in the preceding corollary, then ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq176.gif) is called a subbase. The collection ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq177.gif) of all finite intersections of sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq178.gif) is called the base generated by ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq179.gif) (see the preceding corollary). The topology defined by this base is called the topology generated by ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq180.gif).

Once again, the topology generated by a subbase is unique (Exercise 2). The virtue of a subbase is that it is so easy to find a collection of sets that satisfies the definition. This facilitates inventing examples, though it might make it more difficult to verify that a topology generated by a subbase has some specific property. Therefore, when we introduce concepts for a topological space, we should try to give an equivalent formulation of the concept in terms of a subbase as well as a base.

Example 2.2.7.

(a)

Every base for a topology is a subbase.

(b)

If ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq181.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq182.gif) consists of all the intervals of the form (−∞, a) and (a, ∞), then ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq183.gif) is a subbase for the usual topology on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq184.gif). Of course, we can restrict the numbers a in these intervals to be rational or irrational, and we still have a subbase that generates the usual topology. Observe that ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq185.gif) is not a base for the topology of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq186.gif).

## Exercises

(1)

Show that the topology generated by a base ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq187.gif) is the intersection of all topologies that contain ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq188.gif).

(2)

Prove that the topology generated by a subbase is the intersection of all topologies that contain it.

(3)

For a topological space ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq189.gif) give a necessary and sufficient condition on a collection of subsets ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq190.gif) of X that ![
$$\\mathcal{B} =\\{ X\\setminus C: C \\in \\mathcal{C}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq191.gif) is a base for the topology of X. Can we say that such a collection ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq192.gif) is a base for the closed subsets of ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq193.gif)?

(4)

Consider the plane ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq194.gif), and define an open half-plane to be a set of the form ![
$$\\{\(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: ax + by < c\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq195.gif) for some choice of constants a, b, c. (a) If ![
$$a,b,c \\in \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq196.gif), show that ![
$$\\{\(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: ax + by > c\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq197.gif) is an open half-plane. (b) Show that the collection of all open half-planes is a subbase for the usual topology on ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq198.gif).

(5)

A partially ordered set is a pair (X, ≤ ), where X is a set and ≤ is a relation on X such that: (i) x ≤ x for all X; (ii) if x ≤ y and y ≤ z, then x ≤ z; (iii) if x ≤ y and y ≤ x, then x = y. (Also see Definition A.4.1.) Say that a partially ordered set is linearly ordered if whenever x and y are in X, either x ≤ y or y ≤ x. (For example, ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq199.gif) or any of its subsets is linearly ordered.) (a) If X is a partially ordered set and ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq200.gif) is the collection of all sets having the form of either ![
$$\\{y: y \\leq x\\text{ and }y\\neq x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq201.gif) or ![
$$\\{y: x \\leq y\\text{ and }y\\neq x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq202.gif), show that ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq203.gif) is a subbase for a topology on X, though it may not satisfy the Hausdorff property. This is called the order topology on X. (b) Show that if (X, ≤ ) is linearly ordered, then the order topology satisfies the Hausdorff property. Can you find another condition on the ordering such that the order topology has the Hausdorff property? (c) When a and b are elements of a partially ordered space, let ![
$$\(a,b\) =\\{ x \\in X: a < x < b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq204.gif). If (X, ≤ ) is linearly ordered, show that ![
$$\\{\(a,b\): a,b \\in X\\text{ and }a < b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq205.gif) is a base of the order topology.

(6)

This exercise is taken from [1]. Define the space ℓ 1 of absolutely summable sequences: ![
$${\\ell}^{1} =\\{\\{ a_{n}\\}: a_{n} \\in \\mathbb{R}\\text{ and }\\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }\\vert a_{n}\\vert < \\infty \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq206.gif). (a) Show that ![
$$\\rho \(a,b\) =\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }\\vert a_{n} - b_{n}\\vert $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq207.gif) defines a metric on ℓ 1. (b) Show that if ![
$$a =\\{ a_{n}\\} {\\in \\ell}^{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq208.gif) and ![
$$x =\\{ x_{n}\\} {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq209.gif) (Exercise 1.1.12), then ![
$$\\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }\\vert a_{n}x_{n}\\vert < \\infty $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq210.gif). We use the notation ![
$$\\langle a,x\\rangle =\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }a_{n}x_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq211.gif). When a ∈ ℓ 1, ![
$$x {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq212.gif), and ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq213.gif), let

![
$$\\displaystyle{U_{x,\\epsilon }\(a\) =\\{ b {\\in \\ell}^{1}: \\vert \\langle a - b,x\\rangle \\vert < \\epsilon \\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equa.gif)

(c) Show that ![
$$\\mathcal{S} =\\{ U_{x,\\epsilon }\(a\): a {\\in \\ell}^{1},x {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty },\\epsilon > 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq214.gif) is a subbase for a topology on ℓ 1. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq215.gif) be the topology on ℓ 1 defined by the subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq216.gif). We will show that the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq217.gif) differs from that defined by the metric ρ on ℓ 1. (d) If ![
$$S =\\{ a {\\in \\ell}^{1}:\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }\\vert a_{n}\\vert = 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq218.gif), show that the closure of S in the ![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq219.gif) topology includes the sequence ![
$$0 =\\{ 0,0,\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq220.gif). That is, for ![
$$x_{1},\\ldots,x_{m} {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq221.gif) and positive ![
$$\\epsilon _{1},\\ldots,\\epsilon _{m}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq222.gif), there is an a in S with ![
$$a \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{m}U_{x_{k},\\epsilon _{k}}\(0\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq223.gif). Note that this says that 0 is a limit point of S. (In fact, the closure of S in the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq224.gif) can be shown to be ![
$$\\{a {\\in \\ell}^{1}:\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }\\vert a_{n}\\vert \\leq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq225.gif).) (e) Consider ![
$$X =\\{ x {\\in \\ell}^{\\infty }: d\(x,0\) =\\sup _{n}\\vert x_{n}\\vert \\leq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq226.gif); since ℓ ∞ with its metric d is a complete metric space (Exercise 1.2.11) and X is a closed subset of ℓ ∞ , we note that (X, d) is a complete metric space. (f) For each x in X, ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq227.gif), and positive integer N, define

![
$$\\displaystyle{G\(x;\\delta,N\) =\\{ y \\in X: \\vert x_{n} - y_{n}\\vert < \\delta \\text{ for }1 \\leq n \\leq N\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equb.gif)

Show that ![
$$\\mathcal{B} =\\{ G\(x;\\delta,J\): x \\in X,\\delta > 0,N \\geq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq228.gif) is a base for the topology on (X, d). (g) If a ∈ ℓ 1, show that ![
$$x\\mapsto \\langle a,x\\rangle$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq229.gif) is a continuous function on (X, d). (h) Now assume that {a k } is a sequence in ℓ 1 with ![
$${a}^{k} =\\{ a_{1}^{k},a_{2}^{k},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq230.gif). Show that if ![
$${a}^{k} \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq231.gif) in ![
$${\(\\ell}^{1},\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq232.gif) (recall the definition of a convergent sequence in a topological space given just after Definition 2.1.10), then for every m ≥ 1 and any ![
$$\\epsilon > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq233.gif)

![
$$\\displaystyle{F_{m} =\\{ x \\in X: \\vert \\langle {a}^{k},x\\rangle \\vert \\leq \\epsilon /3\\text{ for }k \\geq m\\}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equc.gif)

is a closed subset of (X, d). Maintain this notation for the rest of the exercise. (i) Use the Baire Category Theorem to show that there is an integer m, a point x in X, a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq234.gif), and a positive integer N such that ![
$$G\(x;\\delta,N\) \\subseteq F_{m}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq235.gif). (j) Conclude that ![
$$\\rho \(a_{k},0\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq236.gif). (k) Use part (d) to conclude that the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq237.gif) on ℓ 1 is different from the topology defined by the metric ρ.

## 2.3 Continuous Functions

Here we extend to the setting of topological spaces the definition of a continuous function given in § 1.3 for metric spaces. The original definition in the metric space environment (Definition 1.3.1) involves the metric, so that will not extend. We could define convergent sequences in a topological space and use that, but it is inadequate for reasons we cannot go into here. Nevertheless, we can use Theorem 1.3.3, which gives an equivalent formulation of continuity in terms of open sets.

Definition 2.3.1.

If ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq238.gif) and ![
$$\(W,\\mathcal{U}\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq239.gif) are topological spaces and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq240.gif), say that f is continuous at a point x if for every neighborhood U of f(x) in W, there is a neighborhood G of x in X such that ![
$$f\(G\) \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq241.gif) (equivalently, ![
$$G \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(U\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq242.gif)). Say that f is continuous on X if it is continuous at each point.

As we said in discussing continuous functions on a metric space, we will not focus on functions continuous at a point, but we will put all our energy into understanding functions that are continuous on the entire topological space.

Proposition 2.3.2.

If ![
$$f: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow \(W,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq243.gif) , then the following statements are equivalent:

(a) f is continuous.

(b) For every open set U in W, f −1 (U) is open in X.

(c) For every closed set C in W, f −1 (C) is closed in X.

(d) If ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq244.gif) is a base for the topology of W, then ![
$${f}^{-1}\(B\) \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq245.gif) for every A in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq246.gif) .

(e) If ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq247.gif) is a subbase for the topology of W, then ![
$${f}^{-1}\(S\) \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq248.gif) for every S in  ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq249.gif) .

Proof.

It is left to the reader to show that (a), (b), and (c) are equivalent. (See the proof of Theorem 1.3.3, which can essentially be lifted to this setting.) Since a base is contained in the topology, (b) implies (d); since a subbase is contained in a base, (d) implies (e). If (e) holds, observe that ![
$$\\{U \\in \\mathcal{U}: {f}^{-1}\(U\) \\in \\mathcal{T}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq250.gif) is closed under finite intersections and arbitrary unions and contains ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq251.gif); hence it equals ![
$$\\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq252.gif). This implies that (b) holds and completes the proof of the proposition. ■

Since the open balls in a metric space form a basis for the topology, the equivalence of (a) and (c) can be considered the extension of the ![
$$\\epsilon \\,-\\,\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq253.gif) definition of continuity given for a function from one metric space into another.

Proposition 2.3.3.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \),\(W,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq254.gif) , and ![
$$\(Y,\\mathcal{V}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq255.gif) be topological spaces. If ![
$$f: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow \(Y,\\mathcal{V}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq256.gif) and ![
$$g: \(Y,\\mathcal{V}\) \\rightarrow \(W,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq257.gif) are continuous functions, then so is the composition ![
$$g \\circ f: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow \(W,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq258.gif) .

Proof.

If ![
$$U \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq259.gif), then ![
$${\(g \\circ f\)}^{-1}\(U\) = {f}^{-1}\\left \[{g}^{-1}\(U\)\\right \]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq260.gif). But ![
$${g}^{-1}\(U\) \\in \\mathcal{V}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq261.gif), so that ![
$${\(g \\circ f\)}^{-1}\(U\) \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq262.gif), whence the continuity of g ∘ f. ■

Even though the next result is almost longer to state than it is to prove, it is a useful way to put together continuous functions to form another.

Proposition 2.3.4.

Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq263.gif) and ![
$$\(W,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq264.gif) be topological spaces with subsets A and B such that X = A ∪ B, suppose ![
$$g: A \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq265.gif) and ![
$$h: B \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq266.gif) are continuous functions such that g(x) = h(x) when ![
$$x \\in A \\cap B$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq267.gif) , and define ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq268.gif) by letting f(x) = g(x) when x ∈ A and f(x) = h(x) when x ∈ B. If both A and B are open or if both are closed, then the function f is continuous.

Proof.

Assume both A and B are open. If U is an open subset of W, then it is easily verified that ![
$${f}^{-1}\(U\) = {g}^{-1}\(U\) \\cup {h}^{-1}\(U\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq269.gif). Now g −1(U) and h −1(U) are open subsets of A and B, respectively, and, since A and B are open in X, f −1(U) is open. Therefore, f is continuous. The proof where the sets A and B are closed is similar. ■

In conjunction with the last proposition, see Exercises 2 and 3.

Now we want to define the cartesian product of a finite number of topological spaces, similar to our definition (§ 1.1) of the product of a finite number of metric spaces. Here, however, there is a more "canonical" approach to the topology than there was in the metric space setting. Recall that when we defined the product of metric spaces, we chose one metric on the product space. Later we saw some equivalent metrics. Of course, when metrics are equivalent, they generate the same topology. That is the key to this more general setting, though here there is no middleman.

Proposition 2.3.5.

If ![
$$\(X_{k},\\mathcal{T}_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq270.gif) is a topological space for 1 ≤ k ≤ n and ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq271.gif) , then ![
$$\\mathcal{B} =\\{ G_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}: G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{k}\\text{ for }1 \\leq k \\leq n\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq272.gif) is a base for a topology on X. If ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq273.gif) is the topology defined by ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq274.gif) , then ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq275.gif) is the smallest of all the topologies ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq276.gif) on X such that for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq277.gif) the projections ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{U}\) \\rightarrow \(X_{k},\\mathcal{T}_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq278.gif) defined by ![
$$\(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\)\\mapsto x_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq279.gif) are continuous.

Proof.

Clearly, ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq280.gif) covers X. Suppose ![
$$\(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\) = x\\neq y = \(y_{1},\\ldots,y_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq281.gif). So for some integer k, ![
$$x_{k}\\neq y_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq282.gif), and there must be disjoint sets G k , H k belonging to ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq283.gif) with x k in G k and y k in H k . For notational convenience, assume k = 1. If ![
$$G_{1}^{{\\prime}} = G_{1} \\times X \\times \\cdots \\times X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq284.gif) and ![
$$H_{1}^{{\\prime}} = H_{1} \\times X \\times \\cdots \\times X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq285.gif), then x ∈ G 1 ′ , y ∈ H 1 ′ , and ![
$$G_{1}^{{\\prime}}\\cap H_{1}^{{\\prime}} = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq286.gif). So ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq287.gif) has the Hausdorff property. Now let ![
$$x = \(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\) \\in G \\cap H$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq288.gif), where ![
$$G = G_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq289.gif), H = H 1 ×⋯ × H n , and each ![
$$G_{k},H_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq290.gif) belongs to ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq291.gif). If ![
$$U = \(G_{1} \\cap H_{1}\) \\times \\cdots \\times \(G_{n} \\cap H_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq292.gif), then ![
$$U \\in \\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq293.gif) and ![
$$x \\in U \\subseteq G \\cap H$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq294.gif). Therefore ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq295.gif) is a base.

Let ![
$$\\pi _{1}: X \\rightarrow X_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq296.gif) be the map defined by ![
$$\\pi _{1}\(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\) = x_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq297.gif). Note that if ![
$$U_{1} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq298.gif), then ![
$$\\pi _{1}^{-1}\(U_{1}\) = U_{1} \\times X_{2} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq299.gif). Thus, π 1 is continuous; similarly, each π k is continuous. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq300.gif) be another topology on X such that ![
$$\\pi _{k}: \(X,\\mathcal{U}\) \\rightarrow X_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq301.gif) is continuous for 1 ≤ k ≤ n. If ![
$$G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq302.gif) for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq303.gif), then we have that ![
$$G_{1}\\times \\cdots \\times G_{n} = \(G_{1}\\times X_{2}\\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}\)\\cap \\cdots \\cap \(X_{1}\\times X_{2}\\times \\cdots \\times X_{n-1}\\times G_{n}\) =\\pi _{ 1}^{-1}\(G_{1}\)\\cap \\cdots \\pi _{n}^{-1}\(G_{n}\) \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq304.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\subseteq \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq305.gif), and it follows that ![
$$\\mathcal{T} \\subseteq \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq306.gif). ■

Definition 2.3.6.

With the notation as in the preceding proposition, the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq307.gif) is called the product topology on X and the maps ![
$$\(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\)\\mapsto x_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq308.gif) are called the coordinate projections, or simply projections. Usually each projection map will be denoted by π k , as in the preceding proof.

It is left to the reader to verify that the product topology that was just defined agrees with the product topology defined previously on the product of metric spaces (Exercise 5). Later (§ 2.6) we will define the product topology on an infinite product of topological spaces, but first we will explore finite products. We want to establish an important property of the projection maps, but this requires another definition. To facilitate this discussion and later ones, we are going to start an economy of expression by not necessarily specifying the name of the topology on a topological space. So instead of saying, "Let ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq309.gif) be a topological space," we will just say, "Let X be a topological space." There will seldom be a problem, but we can always return to the old phrasing if there is some ambiguity or a need for specificity.

Definition 2.3.7.

If X and Z are topological spaces and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq310.gif), then f is an open map provided f(G) is open in Z whenever G is open in X.

Proposition 2.3.8.

If ![
$$X_{1},\\ldots,X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq311.gif) are topological spaces and ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq312.gif) has the product topology, then each projection map is an open map.

Proof.

Let G be an open subset of X, let 1 ≤ k ≤ n, and suppose x k ∈ π k (G); to show that π k (G) is open, we need to show that there is an open subset G k of X k such that ![
$$x_{k} \\in G_{k} \\subseteq \\pi _{k}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq313.gif). Now there is an x in G with π k (x) = x k ; that is, ![
$$x = \(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq314.gif), and the kth coordinate is precisely the same x k we started with. By Proposition 2.3.5, there are open sets G j in X j , 1 ≤ j ≤ n, with ![
$$x = \(x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}\) \\in G_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times G_{n} \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq315.gif). It follows that ![
$$x_{k} \\in G_{k} =\\pi _{k}\(G_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}\) \\subseteq \\pi _{k}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq316.gif). ■

Proposition 2.3.9.

If ![
$$X_{1},\\ldots,X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq317.gif) are topological spaces, ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq318.gif) has the product topology, Y is another topological space, and ![
$$f: Y \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq319.gif) , then f is continuous if and only if ![
$$\\pi _{k} \\circ f: Y \\rightarrow X_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq320.gif) is continuous for 1 ≤ k ≤ n.

Proof.

If f is continuous, then π k ∘ f is the composition of two continuous functions, and this is continuous by Proposition 2.3.4. Now assume that π k ∘ f is continuous for 1 ≤ k ≤ n. If G k is open in X k for each k, then ![
$${f}^{-1}\(G_{1}\\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}\) =\\{ y \\in Y: f\(y\) \\in G_{1}\\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}\\} =\\{ y \\in Y:\\pi _{k}\(f\(y\)\) \\in G_{k}\\text{ for }1 \\leq k \\leq n\\} =\\bigcap _{ k=1}^{n}{\(\\pi _{k}\\circ f\)}^{-1}\(G_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq321.gif). Since each π k ∘ f is continuous, each ![
$${\(\\pi _{k} \\circ f\)}^{-1}\(G_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq322.gif) is open, and so ![
$${f}^{-1}\(G_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times G_{n}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq323.gif) is open. By Proposition 2.3.2, f is continuous. ■

The next proposition could have been presented earlier, but it would have required a different, less efficient proof.

Proposition 2.3.10.

If ![
$$f,g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq324.gif) are continuous functions, then so are ![
$$f + g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq325.gif) and ![
$$fg: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq326.gif) .

Proof.

First consider the function ![
$$s: \\mathbb{R} \\times \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq327.gif) defined by ![
$$s\(a,b\) = a + b$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq328.gif). We leave the reader to use sequences to show that s is continuous. Now note that ![
$$\(f + g\): X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq329.gif) is the composition of s and the function ![
$$\\phi: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R} \\times \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq330.gif) defined by ϕ(x) = (f(x), g(x)). An easy application of the preceding proposition shows that ϕ is continuous. Thus ![
$$f + g$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq331.gif) is continuous. The proof that fg is continuous is similar (Exercise 6). ■

Here are other ways to manufacture continuous functions from old ones, provided the range of the functions is the real numbers. If ![
$$f,g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq332.gif), define

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \(f \\vee g\)\(x\)& =& \\max \\{f\(x\),g\(x\)\\}, {}\\\\ \(f \\wedge g\)\(x\)& =& \\min \\{f\(x\),g\(x\)\\}, {}\\\\ \\vert f\\vert \(x\)& =& \\vert f\(x\)\\vert {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ1.gif)

for all x in X. It is useful to note that ![
$$f \\wedge g = -\[\(-f\) \\vee \(-g\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq333.gif) and ![
$$f \\vee g = -\[\(-f\) \\wedge \(-g\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq334.gif), which can be verified by evaluating at a point x in X and using the corresponding equalities for real numbers. Hence many arguments involving one operation can be obtained immediately from the other. We do this in the next proof.

Proposition 2.3.11.

If ![
$$f,g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq335.gif) are continuous functions, then so are ![
$$f \\vee g$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq336.gif) , f ∧ g, and |f|.

Proof.

That the map ![
$$x\\mapsto \(f\(x\),g\(x\)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq337.gif) is continuous from X into ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq338.gif) is immediate from Proposition 2.3.9. We will show that the map ![
$$\(s,t\)\\mapsto s \\vee t \\equiv \\max \\{ s,t\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq339.gif) is a continuous map from ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq340.gif) into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq341.gif). Once this is done, we see that ![
$$f \\vee g$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq342.gif) is the composition of two continuous functions and is thus continuous. We use a sequential argument. Suppose ![
$$s_{n} \\rightarrow s$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq343.gif) and ![
$$t_{n} \\rightarrow t$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq344.gif). If t > s, then there is an integer N such that t n − s n > 0 for all n ≥ N. Thus, for n ≥ N, ![
$$s_{n} \\vee t_{n} = t_{n} \\rightarrow t = s \\vee t$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq345.gif). Similarly, when s > t, ![
$$s_{n} \\vee t_{n} \\rightarrow s \\vee t$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq346.gif). Assume s = t, so s ∨ t = t and ![
$$\\lim \(t_{n} - t\) = 0 =\\lim _{n}\(s_{n} - t\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq347.gif). If ε > 0, then there is an N ≥ 1 such that for n ≥ N, ![
$$t -\\epsilon < t_{n},s_{n} < t + \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq348.gif), and thus ![
$$s \\vee t -\\epsilon = t -\\epsilon < s_{n} \\vee t_{n} < t + \\epsilon = s \\vee t + \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq349.gif). Hence this map is continuous.

As we observed prior to the statement of this proposition, ![
$$f \\wedge g = -\[\(-f\) \\vee \(-g\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq350.gif), so it too is continuous. Finally, | f | is the composition of the continuous functions f and t↦ | t | defined on ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq351.gif). ■

Definition 2.3.12.

If X and Z are topological spaces, then a homeomorphism between X and Z is a bijection ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq352.gif) that is continuous and has a continuous inverse ![
$${f}^{-1}: Z \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq353.gif).

We note that this extends the definition of a homeomorphism between two metric spaces given in § 1.3. Also, a bijection f is a homeomorphism if and only if f is both continuous and open. (Why?) As in the situation of metric spaces, it is easy to see that topological spaces being homeomorphic is an equivalence relation (Definition 2.8.1), the basic notion of equivalence among topological spaces.

## Exercises

(1)

Given the fact that the collection of all open infinite intervals (−∞, a), (a, ∞) forms a subbase for the topology of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq354.gif), does the equivalence of continuity and condition (d) in Proposition 2.3.2 remind you of anything for functions ![
$$f: \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq355.gif)?

(2)

In Proposition 2.3.4, construct a counterexample to the conclusion if you do not assume that A and B are both open or both closed.

(3)

Suppose ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq356.gif) and ![
$$\(W,\\mathcal{U}\)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq357.gif) are topological spaces, ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{i}A_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq358.gif), for each ![
$$i$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq359.gif) there is a continuous function ![
$$g_{i}: A_{i} \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq360.gif) such that ![
$$g_{i}\(x\) = g_{j}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq361.gif) when ![
$$x \\in A_{i} \\cap A_{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq362.gif), and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq363.gif) is defined by ![
$$f\(x\) = g_{i}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq364.gif) when x ∈ A i . Can Proposition 2.3.4 be extended to this situation?

(4)

If ![
$$\(X_{k},\\mathcal{T}_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq365.gif) is a topological space for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq366.gif) and ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq367.gif), show that ![
$$\\{\\pi _{k}^{-1}\(G_{k}\): 1 \\leq k \\leq n\\text{ and }G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq368.gif) is a subbase for the product topology on X.

(5)

Let (X k , d k ) be a metric space for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq369.gif) be the resulting topology. Show that the topology defined on the cartesian product ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq370.gif) by the product metric as in Definition 1.1.16 is the same as the product topology defined on X after Proposition 2.3.5. (Readers may assume that n = 2 if they so desire.)

(6)

Give the details showing that fg in Proposition 2.3.10 is continuous.

(7)

If X k , Z k are topological spaces for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, ![
$$X = X_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq371.gif), and ![
$$Z = Z_{1} \\times \\cdots \\times Z_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq372.gif), show that X and Z are homeomorphic if and only if ​, after some renumbering of the spaces, X k and Z k are homeomorphic for 1 ≤ k ≤ n.

(8)

Is an open map also a closed map, that is, a function that maps closed sets into closed sets?

(9)

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq373.gif) denote all the functions from a set X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R},$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq374.gif) and consider the binary operations on ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq375.gif) defined by ![
$$\\vee: \\mathcal{F}\\times \\mathcal{F}\\rightarrow \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq376.gif) and ![
$$\\wedge: \\mathcal{F}\\times \\mathcal{F}\\rightarrow \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq377.gif). (a) Are these operations associative? (b) Are they distributive: ![
$$f \\vee \(g \\wedge h\) = \(f \\vee g\) \\wedge \(f \\vee h\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq378.gif) or ![
$$f \\wedge \(g \\vee h\) = \(f \\wedge g\) \\vee \(f \\wedge h\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq379.gif)?

## 2.4 Compactness and Connectedness

In this section we will revisit two concepts we saw for metric spaces, compactness and connectedness, and we will also introduce an additional form of connectedness. In the following section, we will see a stronger form of connectedness. We start with compactness, whose definition in a topological space is the same as for a metric space.

Definition 2.4.1.

A subset K of a topological space X is compact if every open cover of K has a finite subcover.

In this section, when we prove Theorem 2.4.6 on compactness below, we will use Zorn's Lemma (Theorem A.4.6). If you are not familiar with this result, you must study § A.4, and I advise doing so before you start the present section. This material will be used in the future as we progress, and no notice such as this will be given again.

We start with some basic properties of compactness. It is clear that a subset is compact if and only if it is compact as a topological space when it is given its relative topology, that is, if and only if every cover by relatively open sets has a finite subcover. The proof of the first proposition (Exercise 1) is an imitation of the proof of Proposition 1.4.2 as is its corollary, but where open sets replace open balls. Note that there is no concept of a bounded set in an abstract topological space.

Proposition 2.4.2.

Let X be a topological space, and let ![
$$K \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq380.gif) .

(a) If K is a compact subset of X, then K is closed.

(b) If K is compact and F is a closed set contained in K, then F is compact.

(c) The continuous image of a compact set is compact.

Corollary 2.4.3.

If X is a compact space and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq381.gif) is a continuous function, then there are points a and b in X such that ![
$$f\(a\) \\leq f\(x\) \\leq f\(b\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq382.gif) for all x in X.

We will use the concept of a collection of subsets of a topological space having the finite intersection property (FIP). Because the definition is the same for a topological space as for a metric space, we will not repeat it.

Proposition 2.4.4.

If K is a closed subset of a topological space X, then K is compact if and only if every collection of closed subsets of K having the FIP has a nonempty intersection.

The proof is exactly the same as the proof of the equivalence of (a) and (b) in Theorem 1.4.5. The other parts of Theorem 1.4.5 are not true in a nonmetric space; indeed, some, such as the condition involving total boundedness, do not even make sense. Also, in an abstract topological space, the idea of completeness is nonsensical since we cannot define a Cauchy sequence. Later, when we define the extension of the concept of a sequence, we will see the natural extension of the equivalence with compactness of the parts of Theorem 1.4.5 that involve sequences.

Proposition 2.4.5.

If ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq383.gif) is a base for the topology for X and ![
$$K \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq384.gif) , then K is compact if and only if every cover of K by sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq385.gif) has a finite subcover.

Proof.

Since ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq386.gif) is contained in the collection of open sets, the stated condition is easily seen to follow from the assumption that K is compact. Now assume that every cover of K by sets from the base has a finite subcover, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq387.gif) be an open cover of K. If ![
$$G \\in \\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq388.gif), then there is a subset ![
$$\\mathcal{B}_{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq389.gif) of the base ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq390.gif) such that ![
$$G =\\bigcup \\{ B: B \\in \\mathcal{B}_{G}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq391.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\{B: B \\in \\mathcal{B}_{G}\\text{ and }G \\in \\mathcal{C}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq392.gif) is a cover of K by sets from the base. By assumption, there is a finite subcover; that is, there are ![
$$G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq393.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq394.gif) and sets ![
$$B_{1},\\ldots,B_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq395.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{B}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq396.gif) such that ![
$$B_{k} \\subseteq G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq397.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n and ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}B_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq398.gif). Since ![
$$B_{k} \\subseteq G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq399.gif), ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq400.gif) constitutes the sought-after finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq401.gif). ■

Here is where we make use of Zorn's Lemma. The proof of the next theorem will deal with collections of collections of subsets of a topological space X. That is, it will treat sets Γ that are contained in ![
$${2}^{{2}^{X} }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq402.gif), so stay on your toes. To help out, we will reserve capital Greek letters like Γ for subsets of ![
$${2}^{{2}^{X} }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq403.gif), capital script letters like ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq404.gif) for subsets of 2 X or elements of ![
$$\\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq405.gif), and capital roman letters like W for subsets of X. So ![
$$W \\in \\mathcal{W}\\in \\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq406.gif).

Theorem 2.4.6 (Alexander's2 Theorem).

If X is a topological space and ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq407.gif) is a subbase for the topology of X, then X is compact if and only if every cover by sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq408.gif) has a finite subcover.

Proof.

Again, as in the preceding proof, it is clear that if X is compact, then every subbasic cover of X has a finite subcover. To prove the converse, assume it is false: that is, assume that every cover by sets from ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq409.gif) has a finite subcover, but X is not compact. Let ![
$$\\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq410.gif) denote the collection of all open covers of X that do not have a finite subcover; since X is not compact, ![
$$\\varGamma \\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq411.gif). Order Γ by inclusion (§ A.4). It is easy to see that if Λ is a chain in ![
$$\\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq412.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{V} =\\bigcup \\{ \\mathcal{W}: \\mathcal{W}\\in \\varLambda \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq413.gif) is an open cover of X. If ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq414.gif) has a finite subcover ![
$$G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq415.gif), then for 1 ≤ k ≤ n there is a ![
$$\\mathcal{W}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq416.gif) in Γ such that ![
$$G_{k} \\in \\mathcal{W}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq417.gif). But since Λ is a chain, one of the covers ![
$$\\mathcal{W}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq418.gif) contains all the others. That is, all the sets ![
$$G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq419.gif) belong to one of the covers ![
$$\\mathcal{W}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq420.gif); thus, this ![
$$\\mathcal{W}_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq421.gif) has a finite subcover, contradicting the fact that it belongs to ![
$$\\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq422.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\in \\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq423.gif), so that it is an upper bound of the arbitrary chain ![
$$\\varLambda$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq424.gif). By Zorn's Lemma (Theorem A.4.6), Γ has a maximal element ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq425.gif)—an open cover of X without a finite subcover. Note that if G is any nonempty open subset of X and ![
$$G\\notin \\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq426.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\cup \\{ G\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq427.gif) is an open cover of X that is strictly larger than ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq428.gif). Thus ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\cup \\{ G\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq429.gif) has a finite subcover by the maximality of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq430.gif) in ![
$$\\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq431.gif); that is, ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\cup \\{ G\\}\\notin \\varGamma$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq432.gif). (Observe that the finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\cup G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq433.gif) must include the set G.)

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{W} = \\mathcal{C}\\cap \\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq434.gif); that is, ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq435.gif) consists of all the subbasic sets belonging to the cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq436.gif). (Yes, ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq437.gif) is possibly empty.) ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq438.gif) cannot cover X since then, by hypothesis, ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq439.gif) would have a finite subcover, and thus so would ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq440.gif). Let

![
$$\\displaystyle{ x \\in X\\setminus \\bigcup _{W\\in \\mathcal{W}}W, }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ2.gif)

(2.4.7)

and let ![
$$C \\in \\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq441.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in C$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq442.gif). Since ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq443.gif) is a subbase, there are ![
$$S_{1},\\ldots,S_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq444.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq445.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}S_{k} \\subseteq C$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq446.gif). Note that by (2.4.7), none of the subbasic sets S k can belong to the cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq447.gif). Hence for each ![
$$k,1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq448.gif), ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\cup \\{ S_{k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq449.gif) is a cover of X with a finite subcover. Let ![
$$H_{1}^{k},\\ldots,H_{m_{k}}^{k} \\in \\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq450.gif) be such that

![
$$\\displaystyle{ X = S_{k} \\cup \\bigcup _{j=1}^{m_{k} }H_{j}^{k}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equd.gif)

Therefore,

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} X& =& \\bigcap _{r=1}^{n}\\left \(S_{ r} \\cup \\left \[\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}\\bigcup _{ j=1}^{m_{k} }H_{j}^{k}\\right \]\\right \) {}\\\\ & \\subseteq & \\left \(\\bigcap _{r=1}^{n}S_{ r}\\right \) \\cup \\left \[\\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}\\bigcup _{ j=1}^{m_{k} }H_{j}^{k}\\right \] {}\\\\ & \\subseteq & C \\cup \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}\\bigcup _{ j=1}^{m_{k} }H_{j}^{k}. {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ3.gif)

But this says that ![
$$\\{C\\} \\cup \\{ H_{j}^{k}: 1 \\leq j \\leq m_{k},1 \\leq k \\leq n\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq451.gif) is a finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq452.gif), furnishing the sought-after contradiction. ■

We will use Alexander's Theorem when we investigate the infinite product of topological spaces in § 2.6. Readers might look at Exercise 4, where they are asked to use Alexander's Theorem to show that the product of a finite number of compact spaces is compact. Now we turn our attention to connectedness.

Definition 2.4.8.

A topological space X is connected if there are no subsets of X that are both open and closed other than X and the empty set. A subset of X is connected if it is a connected topological space when it has its relative topology.

This extends the definition of connectedness from metric spaces to topological spaces, and so all the examples we saw in § 1.5 remain valid. In particular, we still have that X is connected if and only if whenever X = A ∪ B with ![
$$A \\cap B = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq453.gif) and both A and B open (or closed), then either A or B is the empty set.

Theorem 2.4.9.

The continuous image of a connected set is connected.

The proof of Theorem 1.5.3 applies almost verbatim in a proof of the preceding result. Similarly, an inspection of the proof of Proposition 1.5.6 reveals that only the properties of open and closed sets were used with no reference to a metric, so the same proof works in the present setting. We will state this result for topological spaces, and neither its proof nor those of other extensions will be given.

Proposition 2.4.10.

Let X be a topological space.

(a)

If {E i : i ∈ I} is a collection of connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{i} \\cap E_{j}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq454.gif) for all i,j in I, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{i\\in I}E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq455.gif) is connected.

(b)

If {E n : n ≥ 1} is a sequence of connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{n} \\cap E_{n+1}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq456.gif) for each n, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq457.gif) is connected.

Corollary 2.4.11.

The union of two intersecting connected subsets of a topological space is connected.

As before, we define a component of ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq458.gif) as a maximal connected subset of X.

Proposition 2.4.12.

For any topological space every connected set is contained in a component, distinct components are disjoint, and the union of all the components is the entire space.

Proposition 2.4.13.

If C is a connected subset of the topological space X and ![
$$C \\subseteq Y \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,C$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq459.gif) , then Y is connected.

Corollary 2.4.14.

The closure of a connected set is connected, and each component is closed.

We end this section with a variation on the idea of connectedness.

Definition 2.4.15.

A topological space X is locally connected if for each x in X and every neighborhood G of x there is a neighborhood U of x such that ![
$$U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq460.gif) and U is connected.

This is the first example we have encountered of a "local" property, that is, a property that may not be possessed by the entire topological space but holds in arbitrarily small neighborhoods. (This phrase, "in arbitrarily small neighborhoods," is justified by the part of the definition that says there is a connected neighborhood of x inside any given neighborhood G.) We will encounter this again in the next section as well as in § 3.5 below when we study the idea of local compactness.

Example 2.4.16.

(a)

A connected space is not necessarily locally connected. For example, the topologist sine curve (Example 1.5.12) is connected but not locally connected. (Why?)

(b)

A locally connected space is not necessarily connected. For example, a discrete topological space that has more than a single point is locally connected, as is the union of two disjoint closed intervals in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq461.gif). The reader should have no trouble discovering many more such examples.

(c)

Any open subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq462.gif) is locally connected.

Proposition 2.4.17.

A topological space X is locally connected if and only if it has a base for its topology consisting of connected sets.

Proof.

If X is locally connected and ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq463.gif) is the collection of all open connected subsets of X, then the definition of local connectedness shows that ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq464.gif) is a base for the topology of X. The proof of the converse is left to the reader. ■

Proposition 2.4.18.

If X is locally connected, then every component is both open and closed.

Proof.

In general, components are closed. Now assume X is locally connected and C is a component. If x ∈ C, then there is a neighborhood G of x that is connected. But ![
$$C \\cap G\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq465.gif), so ![
$$C \\cup G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq466.gif) is connected. Since C is a component, this implies ![
$$G \\subseteq C$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq467.gif). Since x was an arbitrary point in C, this shows that C is open. ■

Proposition 2.4.19.

If X and Z are topological spaces with X locally connected and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq468.gif) is continuous, open, and surjective, then Z is locally connected.

Proof.

Let z ∈ Z, and let G be any neighborhood of z; pick any point x in X with f(x) = z. By hypothesis, f −1(G) is a neighborhood of x. Since X is locally connected, there is a connected neighborhood V of x such that ![
$$V \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq469.gif). Since f is continuous and open, f(V ) is a connected neighborhood of z, and it is contained in G. By definition, Z is locally connected. ■

### Exercises

(1)

Give the details of the proof of Proposition 2.4.2.

(2)

Show that the union of a finite number of compact sets is compact.

(3)

If X and Z are compact topological spaces and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq470.gif) is a continuous bijection, show that f is a homeomorphism.

(4)

Use Alexander's Theorem (2.4.6) to prove that the finite product of compact spaces is compact.

(5)

Give the set {0, 1} the discrete topology. Show that X is connected if and only if every continuous function from X into the set {0, 1} is a constant function.

(6)

Give an example of two connected subsets of a topological space whose intersection is not connected.

(7)

Show that the product of a finite number of topological spaces ![
$$X_{1},\\ldots,X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq471.gif) is locally connected if and only if each X k is locally connected.

## 2.5 Pathwise Connectedness

In this section, we study a further refinement of connectedness, but first we need an additional concept.

Definition 2.5.1.

If X is a topological space and p, q ∈ X, a path in X from p to q is a continuous function ![
$$f: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq472.gif) such that f(0) = p and f(1) = q. The point p is called the initial point, and q is the final point. If p = q, then f is called a closed path or loop.

It should be noted that what we call a path is called an arc by some authors.

The reader has already seen many examples of paths in calculus, and some of the examples below are undoubtedly familiar. But first let us note that the use of the unit interval [0, 1] is just a convenience; any interval would work. In fact, if ![
$$a,b \\in \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq473.gif), a < b, and ![
$$\\tau: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow \[a,b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq474.gif) is defined by ![
$$\\tau \(t\) = tb + \(1 - t\)a$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq475.gif), then τ is an order-preserving homeomorphism. So if ![
$$f: \[a,b\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq476.gif) is continuous, then f ∘τ is a path as defined previously. Conversely, if ![
$$g: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq477.gif) is a path, then ![
$$f = g {\\circ \\tau }^{-1}: \[a,b\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq478.gif) is continuous and defines a path in this extended sense. We also note that no matter which interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq479.gif) we use to define a path, the initial and final points are the same. Hence we can define a path in X by giving a continuous function from some other interval than [0, 1]. This will frequently be done for convenience. Note that the direction of the path is important, and this is reflected in the use of the order-preserving map τ when we discussed changing the interval used to define a path.

Example 2.5.2.

(a)

If ![
$$x,y \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq480.gif), then ![
$$f\(t\) = ty + \(1 - t\)x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq481.gif) is a path in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq482.gif) from x to y. In fact, it traces out the straight line segment from x to y. We abbreviate this by [x, y]; note that [x, y] ≠ [y, x] since, unless x = y, they have different initial and final points.

(b)

If ![
$$f: \[0,2\\pi \] \\rightarrow {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
