](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq483.gif) is given by f(t) = (cost, sint), then this defines the path that traces out the circle in the plane starting and ending at (1, 0) and traveling in the counterclockwise direction.

(c)

If ![
$$g: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq484.gif) is any continuous function, then ![
$$f: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R} \\times X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq485.gif) defined by f(t) = (t, g(t)) is a path. In fact, this path traces out the graph of the function g.

Proposition 2.5.3.

If a,b,c are points in a topological space X, f is a path in X from a to b, and g is a path in X from b to c, then

![
$$\\displaystyle{ h\(t\) = \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\quad &f\(2t\)\\ \\quad \\ \\ \\text{ when }0 \\leq t \\leq \\frac{1} {2} \\\\ \\quad &g\(2t - 1\)\\ \\text{ when }\\frac{1} {2} \\leq t \\leq 1 \\end{array} \\right. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Eque.gif)

is a path in X from a to c.

Proof.

An application of Proposition 2.3.4 shows that h is continuous, and you can check that ![
$$h\(0\) = a,h\(1\) = c$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq486.gif). ■

The path h defined in the preceding proposition is sometimes called the product of f, and g and is denoted by

![
$$\\displaystyle{ h = g \\cdot f }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ4.gif)

(2.5.4)

(first follow f and then follow g). Note that the order of these factors is important. Also note that in keeping with the observation that any closed and bounded interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq487.gif) can be used to define a path, we could define

![
$$\\displaystyle{ g\\cdot f\(t\) = \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\quad &f\(t\)\\ \\quad \\ \\ \\text{ when }0 \\leq t \\leq 1,\\\\ \\quad &g\(t - 1\)\\ \\text{ when } 1 \\leq t \\leq 2. \\end{array} \\right. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equf.gif)

Definition 2.5.5.

A topological space X is pathwise connected if for any two points p and q in X there is an arc in X from p to q. Another term used for pathwise connected is arcwise connected.

Needless to say, if E is a subset of a topological space X, then E is said to be pathwise connected if, with its relative topology, it is a pathwise connected topological space. The reader should have no trouble manufacturing examples of pathwise connected proper subsets of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq488.gif).

The reader will see a resemblance between the next proposition and Theorem 2.4.9 and Proposition 2.4.10.

Proposition 2.5.6.

Let X be a topological space.

(a) Every pathwise connected space is connected.

(b) If X is pathwise connected and Z is another topological space such that there is a continuous surjection ![
$$\\phi: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq489.gif) , then Z is pathwise connected.

(c) If ![
$$\\{E_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq490.gif) is a collection of pathwise connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{i} \\cap E_{j}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq491.gif) for all i,j in I, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{i\\in I}E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq492.gif) is pathwise connected.

(d) If ![
$$\\{E_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{Z}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq493.gif) is a sequence of pathwise connected subsets of X such that ![
$$E_{n} \\cap E_{n+1}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq494.gif) for each n, then ![
$$E =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq495.gif) is pathwise connected.

Proof.

(a)

Suppose X is not connected, so there are nonempty disjoint open sets A and B with X = A ∪ B. Pick a in A and b in B. If f is a path in X from a to b, then f −1(A) and f −1(B) are nonempty disjoint open subsets of [0, 1] whose union is all of the interval. This contradicts the connectedness of the interval, so that X cannot be pathwise connected.

(b)

If p and q are two points in Z, let a, b ∈ X such that ![
$$\\phi \(a\) = p,\\phi \(b\) = q$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq496.gif). If f is a path in X from a to b, then ![
$$\\phi \\circ f$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq497.gif) is a path in Z from p to q.

(c)

If p, q ∈ E, let p ∈ E i and q ∈ E j . Fix any point z in ![
$$E_{i} \\cap E_{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq498.gif), let ![
$$f: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow E_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq499.gif) be a path from p to z, and let ![
$$g: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow E_{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq500.gif) be a path from z to q. If h = g ⋅ f, the product of these paths, then it is a path in E from p to q.

(d)

If p, q ∈ E, then there are integers m, n such that p ∈ E m and q ∈ E n ; assume that m < n. By part (c), E m ∪ E m+1 is pathwise connected. Again, (c) implies that ![
$$E_{m} \\cup E_{m+1} \\cup E_{m+2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq501.gif) is pathwise connected. Continue and we get that ![
$$E_{m} \\cup \\cdots \\cup E_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq502.gif) is pathwise connected. Thus, there is a path in ![
$$E_{m} \\cup \\cdots \\cup E_{n} \\subseteq E$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq503.gif) from p to q. Since p and q were arbitrary, this proves that E is pathwise connected.

■

Example 2.5.7.

Let X be the topologist's sine curve (Example 1.5.12). From (Example 1.5.12) we know that X is connected; however, it is not pathwise connected. See Exercise 4 below as well as Exercise 3.

In light of the preceding example and Proposition 2.5.6(a), pathwise connectedness is a properly stronger property than connectedness. Justified by the preceding proposition, we define a pathwise connected component of a topological space as a maximal pathwise connected subset. The next result is the analog of Proposition 2.4.12. The proof is Exercise 5.

Proposition 2.5.8.

For any topological space every pathwise connected set is contained in a pathwise connected component, distinct pathwise connected components are disjoint, and the union of all the pathwise connected components is the entire space.

It is worth pointing out that Proposition 2.4.13 does not carry over to pathwise connected spaces, as a consideration of the topologist's sine curve with the origin deleted demonstrates.

Now for the local version of pathwise connectivity.

Definition 2.5.9.

A topological space X is locally pathwise connected if for each x in X and each neighborhood G of x there is another neighborhood U of x that is pathwise connected and such that ![
$$U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq504.gif).

Example 2.5.10.

(a)

![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq505.gif) is locally pathwise connected, as is every discrete space.

(b)

Every open subset of a locally pathwise connected space with its relative topology is also locally pathwise connected. In particular, every open subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq506.gif) is locally pathwise connected.

(c)

If X is a metric space, then X is locally pathwise connected if for every x in X and every ε > 0 there is a ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq507.gif) such that ![
$$\\delta < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq508.gif) and ![
$$B\(x;\\delta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq509.gif) is pathwise connected.

It follows that the definition of a locally pathwise connected space can be rephrased as the requirement that the space has a base for its topology consisting of pathwise connected sets.

Proposition 2.5.11.

If X is locally pathwise connected, then an open subset is connected if and only if it is pathwise connected.

Proof.

Let G be an open connected subset of X, and fix a point p in G. If U denotes the pathwise connected component of G that contains p, then the fact that X is locally pathwise connected implies U is an open subset of G. Now let x ∈ G such that x is in the relative closure of U. If W is a pathwise connected neighborhood of x contained in G, then ![
$$W \\cap U\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq510.gif). Thus, ![
$$U \\cup W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq511.gif) is pathwise connected and contained in G. By the definition of a pathwise connected component, ![
$$x \\in W \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq512.gif). Thus, U is simultaneously open and relatively closed in G. Since G is connected and U ≠ ∅, U = G, and so G is pathwise connected. ■

Corollary 2.5.12.

An open subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq513.gif) is connected if and only if it is pathwise connected.

The proof of the next result is Exercise 8. Also, compare this proposition with Proposition 2.4.19.

Proposition 2.5.13.

If X is locally pathwise connected and ![
$$\\phi: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq514.gif) is surjective, continuous, and open, then Z is locally pathwise connected.

## Exercises

(1)

Giving an example, show that the requirement in Proposition 2.5.6(b) for f to be surjective is necessary.

(2)

The following subset of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq515.gif) is often called the comb:

![
$$\\displaystyle{ C = \(\\{0\\} \\times \[0,1\]\) \\cup \\{ \({n}^{-1},y\): n \\in \\mathbb{N},y \\in \[0,1\]\\} \\cup \(\[0,1\] \\times \\{ 0\\}\). }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equg.gif)

(Why is it called the comb?) Show that the comb is connected. Is it pathwise connected?

(3)

If C is the comb (the preceding exercise), then the set ![
$$X = C\\setminus \(\\{0\\} \\times \(0,1\)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq516.gif) is called the deleted comb. Show that X is connected but not pathwise connected.

(4)

Show that the topologist's sine curve (Example 2.5.7) is not pathwise connected. (Hint: Example 1.3.16(d).)

(5)

Prove Proposition 2.5.8.

(6)

Verify the statements made in Example 2.5.10.

(7)

Give an example of a pathwise connected space that is not locally pathwise connected.

(8)

Prove Proposition 2.5.13.

(9)

Give an example of a topological space that is not locally pathwise connected but has the property that every point has a neighborhood that is pathwise connected.

## 2.6 Infinite Products

When we defined the product of two sets X 1, X 2 as the set of all pairs (x 1, x 2) such that x j ∈ X j , we could just as easily have defined X 1 × X 2 as the set of functions

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\left \\{x:\\{ 1,2\\} \\rightarrow X_{1} \\cup X_{2}: x\(j\) \\in X_{j}\\text{ for }j = 1,2\\right \\}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equh.gif)

Similarly, we could redefine a sequence of points in a set X as a function ![
$$x: \\mathbb{N} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq517.gif). This may strike you as contrived, but that is because subsets of integers have their natural ordering and we have more experience dealing with n-tuples and sequences than with functions. Now, however, we want to define the product of a set of topological spaces that are indexed by an arbitrary set, not just a subset of the integers. To do this we must adopt the approach using functions. (The reader can also consult § A.2.)

Definition 2.6.1.

If I is a nonempty set and for each i in I we have a nonempty set X i , then the product of these sets is defined by

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\prod _{i\\in I}X_{i} = \\left \\{x: I \\rightarrow \\bigcup _{i\\in I}X_{i}: x\(i\) \\in X_{i}\\text{ for all }i\\text{ in }I\\right \\}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equi.gif)

We will use the notation x, {x i }, or {x(i)} for elements of X, depending on the situation and which notation we find convenient. You can do the same.

Do you have trouble making sense of ![
$$\\bigcup _{i\\in I}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq518.gif)? The first time I saw this as a student, it gave me pause since we are not assuming that the sets X i are all subsets of a common set. If so, think about it for a while, and strictly apply the definition of the union of sets, and I think the trouble will fade. If it does not, use the definition of the disjoint union of sets given in Exercise 1.5.6.

Let us also mention that the Axiom of Choice (§ A.4) is precisely the statement that if X i ≠ ∅ for all i, then ![
$$\\prod _{i\\in I}X_{i}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq519.gif).

When each X i is a topological space, we want to put a topology on the infinite product. To do this, we prove a proposition in a somewhat more general setting. The reader might observe the similarity of the next result with Proposition 2.3.5 for finite products.

Proposition 2.6.2.

Let X be a set, and let ![
$$\\{X_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq520.gif) be a collection of topological spaces. If, for each i in I, ![
$$f_{i}: X \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq521.gif) is a function such that for distinct points x and y in X there is at least one function f i with ![
$$f_{i}\(x\)\\neq f_{i}\(y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq522.gif) , then ![
$$\\mathcal{S} =\\{ f_{i}^{-1}\(G\): i \\in I\\text{ and }G\\text{ is open in }X_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq523.gif) is a subbase for a topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq524.gif) on X. ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq525.gif) is the smallest of all the topologies ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq526.gif) on X such that ![
$$f_{i}: \(X,\\mathcal{U}\) \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq527.gif) is continuous for each i in I.

Proof.

The proof of the first part of this proposition, that ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq528.gif) is a subbase, is straightforward since for any i, ![
$$f_{i}^{-1}\(X_{i}\) = X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq529.gif), and the Hausdorff condition easily follows from the stated assumption about the functions f i and the fact that each space X i satisfies the Hausdorff condition. If ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq530.gif) is the topology defined by this subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq531.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq532.gif) is another topology such that each ![
$$f_{i}: \(X,\\mathcal{U}\) \\rightarrow \(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq533.gif) is continuous, then ![
$$f_{i}^{-1}\(H\) \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq534.gif) for each H in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq535.gif) and each i ∈ I. But this means that ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\subseteq \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq536.gif), so we have that ![
$$\\mathcal{T} \\subseteq \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq537.gif). ■

The topology ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq538.gif) defined by the collection of functions ![
$$\\mathcal{F} =\\{ f_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq539.gif) is called the weak topology defined by ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq540.gif). The reader will note the similarity of the next corollary with Proposition 2.3.9.

Corollary 2.6.3.

Adopt the notation of the preceding proposition. If Z is a topological space and ![
$$g: Z \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq541.gif) is a function, then g is continuous if and only if ![
$$f_{i} \\circ g: Z \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq542.gif) is continuous for every i in I.

Proof.

If g is continuous, then f i ∘ g is the composition of two continuous functions and is therefore continuous. Assume each f i ∘ g is continuous, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{W}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq543.gif) be the topology of Z. To show that g is continuous, we need only show that ![
$${g}^{-1}\(S\) \\in \\mathcal{W}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq544.gif) for every S in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq545.gif). But if i ∈ I and ![
$$G \\in \\mathcal{T}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq546.gif), then ![
$${g}^{-1}\(f_{i}^{-1}\(G\)\) = {\(f_{i} \\circ g\)}^{-1}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq547.gif), and this belongs to ![
$$\\mathcal{W}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq548.gif) since f i ∘ g is continuous. ■

If X = ∏ i ∈ I X i , then for each i we can define the projection map ![
$$\\pi _{i}: X \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq549.gif) by π i (x) = x(i).

Definition 2.6.4.

If {X i : i ∈ I} is a collection of topological spaces, then the weak topology defined on their product X by the projection maps {π i : i ∈ I} is called the product topology on X. The subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq550.gif) that appears in Proposition 2.6.2 is called the standard subbase for the product topology.

Since the subbase only restricts one coordinate, neighborhoods of a point in the product topology only restrict a finite number of coordinates. It might seem strange that a topology is placed on the product space X by only restricting a finite number of coordinates. Indeed, this was the reaction of some famous topologists at the time this idea was introduced and explored by Andrei Tikhonov in 1926. (See the biographical footnote for Theorem 2.6.7 below.) Nevertheless, this is the most advantageous definition of the product topology, the one universally used. The topology where there are no restrictions on the number of coordinate spaces is called the box topology, and some examples are explored in Exercise 3.

We are looking at product spaces, so why did we state and prove Proposition 2.6.2 in a more general setting? Why not proceed with the product and projection maps? The answer is that defining a topology in terms of a collection of functions happens repeatedly in mathematics. In this connection, we call attention to Exercise 2. Also, we will see this subsequently in § 3.4.

The proof of part (a) of the next proposition is like that of Proposition 2.3.8, and part (b) is immediate from Corollary 2.6.3.

Proposition 2.6.5.

Let {X i : i ∈ I} be a collection of topological spaces and give their product X the product topology.

(a) The projection π i onto X i is an open map.

(b) If Z is a topological space and ![
$$g: Z \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq551.gif) , then g is continuous if and only if each ![
$$\\pi _{i} \\circ g: Z \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq552.gif) is continuous for each i in I.

Now we do an about-face from this level of generality and return to metric spaces and use only a countable number of coordinate spaces.

Theorem 2.6.6.

If (X n ,d n ) is a metric space for each n ≥ 1 and ![
$$X =\\prod _{ n=1}^{\\infty }X_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq553.gif) , then the product topology on X is metrizable and defined by the metric

![
$$\\displaystyle{ d\(x,y\) =\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty } \\frac{1} {{2}^{n}} \\frac{d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\)} {1 + d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\)}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equj.gif)

Proof.

Using Proposition 1.3.13 we see that the formula for d(x, y) does indeed define a metric on X. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq554.gif) denote the topology defined by the metric d, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq555.gif) be the product topology on X; we must show that ![
$$\\mathcal{T} = \\mathcal{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq556.gif).

If ![
$$G \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq557.gif), then the definition of the product topology implies that for any x = { x n } in G there are ![
$$n_{1} < \\cdots < n_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq558.gif) and ![
$$\\epsilon _{1},\\ldots,\\epsilon _{N} > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq559.gif) with

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\bigcap _{k=1}^{N}\\pi _{ n_{k}}^{-1}\(B\(x_{ n_{k}};\\epsilon _{k}\) \\subseteq G. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equk.gif)

Choose 0 < ε < 1 such that when ![
$$0 \\leq s < {2}^{N}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq560.gif), then ![
$$s{\(1 - s\)}^{-1} < \\epsilon _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq561.gif) for 1 ≤ n ≤ N. Thus, when ![
$$d\(x,y\) < \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq562.gif), ![
$$d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\){\[1 + d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\)\]}^{-1} < {2}^{N}\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq563.gif) for 1 ≤ n ≤ N; by the choice of ε we have that ![
$$d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\) < \\epsilon _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq564.gif) for 1 ≤ n ≤ N. This implies ![
$$y \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{N}\\pi _{n_{k}}^{-1}\(B\(x_{n_{k}};\\epsilon _{k}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq565.gif). That is, ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq566.gif), and hence we have that ![
$$G \\in \\mathcal{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq567.gif).

Now assume ![
$$D \\in \\mathcal{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq568.gif) and ![
$$x =\\{ x_{n}\\} \\in D$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq569.gif); pick ε > 0 such that ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq D$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq570.gif); choose N ≥ 1 such that ![
$$\\sum _{n=N+1}^{\\infty }{2}^{-n} < \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq571.gif). Let ![
$$\\delta > 0$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq572.gif) such that when ![
$$0 \\leq t < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq573.gif), ![
$$t{\(1 + t\)}^{-1} < \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq574.gif). Thus, if y = { y n } ∈ X and ![
$$d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\) < \\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq575.gif) for 1 ≤ n ≤ N, then

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\sum _{n=1}^{N} \\frac{1} {{2}^{n}} \\frac{d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\)} {1 + d_{n}\(x_{n},y_{n}\)} <\\sum _{ n=1}^{N} \\frac{1} {{2}^{n}} \\frac{\\epsilon } {2} < \\frac{\\epsilon } {2}, }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equl.gif)

so that d(x, y) < ε. That is, ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{N}\\pi _{n}^{-1}\(B\(x_{n};\\delta \)\) \\subseteq B\(x;\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq576.gif), and we have that ![
$$D \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq577.gif). ■

The preceding theorem has something to say about Exercise 2.1.10.

Now for one of the deeper results on infinite products. It is one that you will use often if your study of mathematics persists.

Theorem 2.6.7 (Tikhonov's3 Theorem).

If {X i : i ∈ I} is a collection of topological spaces and their product X is given the product topology, then X is compact if and only if each X i is compact.

Proof.

Since each projection map is continuous, it follows that when X is compact, so are all the coordinate spaces. Now assume each X i is compact and that ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq578.gif) is an open cover of X by elements of the standard subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq579.gif). By Alexander's Theorem, to show that X is compact, it suffices to show that each such cover has a finite subcover.

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq580.gif) be the topology on X i , and for each i let ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{i} =\\{ G \\in \\mathcal{T}_{i}:\\pi _{ i}^{-1}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq581.gif) ![
$$\\in \\mathcal{C}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq582.gif). It is important for later in the proof that we observe that since each element of the cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq583.gif) belongs to the standard subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq584.gif),

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\mathcal{C} =\\bigcup _{i}\\{\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(G\): G \\in \\mathcal{C}_{ i}\\}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ5.gif)

(2.6.8)

Claim.

There is at least one i in I such that ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq585.gif) is a cover of X i .

In fact, suppose this is not the case. It follows that for every i there is a point x i that belongs to ![
$$X_{i}\\setminus \\bigcup \\{G: G \\in \\mathcal{C}_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq586.gif); let x be the element of X defined by x(i) = x i for all i. Hence for every i, ![
$$x\\notin \\pi _{i}^{-1}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq587.gif) for every G in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq588.gif). But from (2.6.8) it then follows that ![
$$x\\notin \\bigcup \\{C: C \\in \\mathcal{C}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq589.gif). That is, ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq590.gif) is not a cover, a contradiction. Therefore, the claim is established.

Fix an i such that ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq591.gif) is a cover of X i . Since X i is compact, there are finitely many sets ![
$$G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq592.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq593.gif) such that ![
$$X_{i} =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq594.gif). It follows that ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(G_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq595.gif), and so ![
$$\\{\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(G_{1}\),\\ldots,\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(G_{n}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq596.gif) is a finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq597.gif). ■

Now we want to prove that the product of connected spaces is connected. First we need a lemma.

Lemma 2.6.9.

If X is the product of the topological spaces {X i : i ∈ I} and a ∈ X, then ![
$$D =\\{ x \\in X: x\(i\) = a\(i\)\\text{ for all but a finite number of }i\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq598.gif) is dense in X.

Proof.

If y ∈ X and G is a neighborhood of y, then the definition of the product topology says that there are ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq599.gif) in I and open sets ![
$$G_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq600.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq601.gif) such that ![
$$y \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}\\pi _{i_{k}}^{-1}\(G_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq602.gif). If we define x in X by setting x(i) = a(i) for ![
$$i\\neq i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq603.gif) and x(i k ) equal to any point in ![
$$G_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq604.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, then ![
$$x \\in G \\cap D$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq605.gif). Therefore, D is dense (Proposition 2.1.9). ■

Theorem 2.6.10.

If {X i : i ∈ I} is a collection of topological spaces and their product X is given the product topology, then X is connected if and only if each X i is connected.

Proof.

If X is connected, then each X i is connected since it is the image of X under the projection map. Now assume that each X i is connected. Let ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\{ 0,1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq606.gif) be a continuous function. According to Exercise 2.4.5, it suffices to show that such a continuous function is constant. So fix a point a in X and let us show that f(x) = f(a) for all x in X. If k ∈ I, define ![
$$g_{k}: X_{k} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq607.gif) by letting

![
$$\\displaystyle{ g_{k}\(y\)\(i\) = \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\quad &y\\quad \\quad \\ \\text{ if }i = k,\\\\ \\quad &a\(i\)\\quad \\;\\text{ if } i\\neq k. \\end{array} \\right. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equm.gif)

We will show that g k is continuous by showing that g k −1(S) is open in X k for every element S of the standard subbasis for the topology on X; that is, we will show that for any i in I ![
$$g_{k}^{-1}\(\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(U_{i}\)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq608.gif) is open in X k whenever U i is open in X i . (This will call for some mental dexterity, so pay attention.)

To begin, we show that if U k is open in X k , then ![
$$g_{k}^{-1}\(\\pi _{k}^{-1}\(U_{k}\)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq609.gif) is open in X k . Note that ![
$$x \\in \\pi _{k}^{-1}\(U_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq610.gif) if and only if x(k) ∈ U k . Thus, for y in X k , ![
$$g_{k}\(y\) \\in \\pi _{k}^{-1}\(U_{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq611.gif) if and only if ![
$$y = g_{k}\(y\)\(k\) \\in U_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq612.gif). That is, ![
$$g_{k}^{-1}\(\\pi _{k}^{-1}\(U_{k}\)\) = U_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq613.gif), which is open. Now assume i ≠ k and U i is open in X i . Here ![
$$x \\in \\pi _{i}^{-1}\(U_{i}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq614.gif) if and only if x(i) ∈ U i . For y ∈ X k , ![
$$g_{k}\(y\) \\in \\pi _{i}^{-1}\(U_{i}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq615.gif) if and only if ![
$$a\(i\) = g_{k}\(y\)\(i\) \\in U_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq616.gif). Hence we have that when a(i) ∈ U i , ![
$$g_{k}^{-1}\(\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(U_{i}\)\) = X_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq617.gif); and when a(i)∉U i , ![
$$g_{k}^{-1}\(\\pi _{i}^{-1}\(U_{i}\)\) = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq618.gif). Therefore, g k is continuous.

This implies that ![
$$f \\circ g_{k}: X_{k} \\rightarrow \\{ 0,1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq619.gif) is continuous; since X k is connected, this function must be constant. But since g k (a(k)) = a, this means that f(g k (y)) = f(a) for all y in X k . Equivalently, for any choice of k in I

![
$$\\displaystyle{ f\(x\) = f\(a\)\\text{ whenever }x\(i\) = a\(i\)\\text{ for }i\\neq k. }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equ6.gif)

(2.6.11)

It follows that f is constantly equal to f(a) on ![
$$D =\\{ x \\in X: x\(i\) = a\(i\)\\text{ for all but a finite number of }i\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq620.gif). In fact if ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n} \\in I$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq621.gif), x ∈ X, and x(i) = a(i) for ![
$$i\\neq i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq622.gif), then by taking k = i 1 in (2.6.11) we have that f(x) = f(a). Since the preceding lemma says D is dense, and since f was assumed continuous, it must be that f is constant on X. Therefore, X is connected. ■

The statement for pathwise connected spaces analogous to the preceding proposition is also valid.

Proposition 2.6.12.

If {X i : i ∈ I} is a collection of topological spaces and their product X is given the product topology, then X is pathwise connected if and only if each X i is pathwise connected.

Proof.

Assume each X i is pathwise connected. Let x = { x i } and y = { y i } be two points in X, and for each i let ![
$$f_{i}: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq623.gif) be a path from x i to y i . Define ![
$$f: \[0,1\] \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq624.gif) by f(t) = { f i (t)}. Note that if π i is the projection of X onto X i , then p i ∘ f = f i . By Proposition 2.6.5, f is continuous, and clearly it is a path from x to y. The converse follows by Proposition 2.5.6(b). ■

### Exercises

(1)

Show that if ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq625.gif) and y = { y i } ∈ X, then for each index j the map ![
$$\\phi: X_{j} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq626.gif) defined by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\phi \(x\)_{i} = \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} x \\quad &\\text{ when }i = j\\\\ y_{ i}\\quad &\\text{ when }i\\neq j\\end{array} \\right.}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_Equn.gif)

is a homeomorphism of X j onto ![
$$\\phi \(X_{i}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq627.gif).

(2)

Let (X, d) be a metric space, and let C(X) be the set of all continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq628.gif). Show that the weak topology defined on X by the functions in C(X) is the given topology on X defined by the metric.

(3)

Let ![
$$\\{\(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\): i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq629.gif) be a collection of topological spaces, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{B}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq630.gif) be the topology on ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq631.gif) with a subbase ![
$$\\{\\prod _{i}G_{i}: G_{i} \\in \\mathcal{T}_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq632.gif). (This is called the box topology on X.) (a) Let ![
$$I = \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq633.gif), and for each n in ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq634.gif) let ![
$$X_{n} = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq635.gif). Give the resulting product space the box topology and define the function ![
$$f: \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq636.gif) by ![
$$f\(x\) = \(x,x,\\ldots \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq637.gif). Show that f is not continuous, even though ![
$$\\pi _{n} \\circ f: \\mathbb{R} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq638.gif) is continuous for each n. [Hint: examine ![
$${f}^{-1}\\left \(\\prod _{n=1}^{\\infty }\(-\\frac{1} {n}, \\frac{1} {n}\)\\right \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq639.gif).] (b) Again let ![
$$I = \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq640.gif), and for each n in ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq641.gif) let X n = { 0, 1}. Show that the box topology on X is the discrete topology. Hence X is not compact, even though each coordinate space is compact.

(4)

Find an example of a collection of connected topological spaces such that the product space with the box topology is not connected.

(5)

Let ![
$$\\{\(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\): i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq642.gif) be a collection of topological spaces, and let ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq643.gif) have the product topology. Show that X is separable if and only if I is countable and each X i is separable.

(6)

Let ![
$$\\{\(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\): i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq644.gif) be a collection of topological spaces, and let X = ∏ i X i have the product topology. If, for each i in I, C i is a component of X i , is C = ∏ i ∈ I C i a component of X?

(7)

Let ![
$$\\{\(X_{i},\\mathcal{T}_{i}\): i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq645.gif) be a collection of topological spaces, and let X = ∏ i X i have the product topology. (a) Show that if X is locally connected, then each X i is locally connected. (Hint: Proposition 2.4.19 may be useful.) (b) Show that the converse is false by giving a counterexample. (See Exercise 2.4.7.) (c) If you assume that each X i is locally connected, can you give an additional hypothesis that implies that X is locally connected?

(8)

Explore the questions raised in the preceding exercise for local pathwise connectedness.

## 2.7 Nets

Now we generalize the notion of a sequence with a concept that works almost as well for an arbitrary topological space as sequences work for metric spaces. Two references where the reader will find all this material and more are [6] and [4]. Recall the definition of a partially ordered set given in § A.4.

Definition 2.7.1.

A directed set is a partially ordered set I with the property that if i, j ∈ I, then there is a k in I with i, j ≤ k.

Example 2.7.2.

(a)

![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq646.gif) is a directed set.

(b)

The set ![
$$\\mathbb{Z}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq647.gif) of all integers is a directed set.

(c)

Let E be any set and order 2 E by inclusion. That is, if A, B ∈ 2 E , then A ≤ B means ![
$$A \\subseteq B$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq648.gif). It follows that 2 E is a directed set.

(d)

Again, if E is any set, ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq649.gif) is the collection of all finite subsets of E, and ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq650.gif) is ordered by inclusion, then ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq651.gif) is a directed set.

(e)

Let X be a topological space, and for some x 0 in X let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq652.gif) denote the collection of all neighborhoods of x 0. It is easy to see that ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq653.gif) is a directed set under reverse inclusion; that is, if ![
$$U,V \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq654.gif), declare U ≥ V to mean that ![
$$U \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq655.gif).

Let us mention something that is immediate from the definition of a directed set and that will be used often: if I is directed and ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n} \\in I$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq656.gif), then there is an i in I with ![
$$i \\geq i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq657.gif). (Verify!)

Definition 2.7.3.

A net in a set X is a pair (x, I), where I is a directed set and x is a function from I into X.

We will often (usually) write the net as {x i : i ∈ I} or {x i } if the directed set I is understood; the function notation {x(i): i ∈ I} will seldom be used to denote a net. Note that every sequence is a net.

Example 2.7.4.

(a)

Let 2 E be the directed set from Example 2.7.2(c), and for each A ∈ 2 E pick a point x A in A. Then {x A } is a net.

(b)

Again let E be a set, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq658.gif) be the collection of all nonempty finite subsets of ![
$$\\mathcal{E}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq659.gif) as in Example 2.7.2(d). If x F ∈ F for each F in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq660.gif), then {x F } is a net.

(c)

If E is any set, ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq661.gif) is as in Example 2.7.2(d), and ![
$$f: E \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq662.gif) is a function, then ![
$$\\{\\sum _{e\\in F}f\(e\): F \\in \\mathcal{F}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq663.gif) is a net. Technically we define ![
$$x: \\mathcal{F}\\rightarrow \\mathbb{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq664.gif) by ![
$$x\(F\) =\\sum _{e\\in F}f\(e\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq665.gif), and ![
$$\(x,\\mathcal{F}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq666.gif) is a net.

(d)

If we consider Example 2.7.2(e) and for each U in ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq667.gif) pick a point x U in U, then ![
$$\\{x_{U}: U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq668.gif) is a net.

Definition 2.7.5.

If {x i } is a net in a topological space X, say that the net converges to x, in symbols ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq669.gif) or ![
$$x =\\lim _{i}x_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq670.gif), if for every open set G containing x there is an i 0 in I such that x i ∈ G for all i ≥ i 0. Say that {x i } clusters at x, in symbols ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq671.gif), if for every open set G containing x and for every j in I there is an i ≥ j with x i in G.

It is easy to see that if we have a sequence, then this definition of convergence is the same as the concept of a convergent sequence (Exercise 1). Similarly, a sequence clusters at a point x in this sense if and only if it clusters in the sense of sequences (Exercise 1).

Example 2.7.6.

The net {x U } defined in Example 2.7.4(d) converges to x 0.

The proof of the next proposition is straightforward (Exercise 2) and a good opportunity to fix the ideas in your mind.

Proposition 2.7.7.

(a) If a net in a topological spaces converges to x, then it clusters at x.

(b) A net can converge to only one point.

Here is one way to define a subnet that extends the idea of a subsequence. If I is a directed set, say that a subset J is cofinal if for every i in I there is a j in J with j ≥ i. It is easily shown that when J is a cofinal subset, then it too is a directed set. So if we have a net ![
$$x: I \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq672.gif), then we could define a subnet to be the restriction of x to some cofinal subset. However, this is NOT the definition of a subnet. The more complicated notion of a subnet is formulated in such a way that various results about sequences and subsequences in a metric space can be extended to nets and subnets in a topological space. For example: if a net clusters at x, then it has a subnet that converges to x; a topological space X is compact if and only if every net in X has a convergent subnet.

The interested reader can consult [4] and [6] for the accepted definition of a subnet. We will not use subnets in this book. I have found that I can usually avoid them and have decided to live my life that way. I am not saying that all the readers should follow my example, but for this introduction we will follow the path of least resistance.

Proposition 2.7.8.

Let X and Z be topological spaces.

(a) If ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq673.gif) and x ∈ X, then f is continuous at x if and only if whenever ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq674.gif) in X, ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq675.gif) in Z.

(b) If ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq676.gif) is continuous at x and ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq677.gif) , then ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq678.gif) .

(c) A subset F of X is closed if and only if whenever we have a net {x i } of points in F that converges to a point x, we have that x ∈ F.

Proof.

(a)

Suppose f is continuous at x and ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq679.gif). If W is a neighborhood of f(x) in Z, then there is a neighborhood G of x with ![
$$G \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(W\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq680.gif). Thus, there is an i 0 such that x i ∈ G for all i ≥ i 0; this says that ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\in f\(G\) \\subseteq W$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq681.gif) for all i ≥ i 0. Since W was arbitrary, this implies ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq682.gif).

Now assume the stated condition is satisfied, and let us show f is continuous at x. We must show that for every neighborhood W of f(x) there is a neighborhood G of x with ![
$$G \\subseteq {f}^{-1}\(W\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq683.gif). Suppose this is not the case. Then there is a neighborhood W of f(x) such that if ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq684.gif) is the set of neighborhoods of x in X, then ![
$$G\\setminus {f}^{-1}\(W\)\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq685.gif) for every G in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq686.gif); for each G in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq687.gif) let ![
$$x_{G} \\in G\\setminus {f}^{-1}\(W\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq688.gif). Now ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq689.gif) is a directed set under inclusion (Verify!), and we have that ![
$$x_{G} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq690.gif), as in Example 2.7.6. However, ![
$$\\{f\(x_{G}\): G \\in \\mathcal{T}_{x}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq691.gif) does not converge to f(x) (Why?), giving the desired contradiction.

(b)

The proof of this is similar to the first part of the proof of (a) and is Exercise 3.

(c)

Assume F is closed, {x i } is a net in F, and ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq692.gif). If G is a neighborhood of x, then there is an i 0 such that x i ∈ G for i ≥ i 0. This says that ![
$$G \\cap F\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq693.gif) for every neighborhood G of x, hence ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,F = F$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq694.gif). Now assume the stated condition holds and ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,F$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq695.gif). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq696.gif) be the set of all neighborhoods of x and order it by inclusion. Since ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,F$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq697.gif), for every G in ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq698.gif) there is a point x G in ![
$$G \\cap F$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq699.gif). But ![
$$\\{x_{G}: G \\in \\mathcal{T}_{x}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq700.gif) is a net that converges to x, so x ∈ F by assumption. Thus ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,F \\subseteq F$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq701.gif), and F is closed.

■

Also see Exercise 4 for an extension of part (c) of the preceding proposition. The plot thickens.

Theorem 2.7.9.

A topological space X is compact if and only if every net in X has a cluster point.

Proof.

Suppose X is compact and {x i } is a net in X. For every j in I let F j be the closure of ![
$$\\{x_{i}: i \\geq j\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq702.gif). If ![
$$j_{1},\\ldots,j_{n} \\in I$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq703.gif), then there is an i ≥ j k for 1 ≤ k ≤ n; hence ![
$$x_{i} \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}F_{j_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq704.gif). So {F i : i ∈ I} has the FIP; since X is compact, there is an x in ![
$$\\bigcap _{i}F_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq705.gif). If G is an open set containing x and i 0 ∈ I, then ![
$$x \\in F_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq706.gif), so that ![
$$G \\cap \\{ x_{i}: i \\geq i_{0}\\}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq707.gif). That is, there is an i ≥ i 0 with x i ∈ G. By definition, this says that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq708.gif).

Now assume X is not compact, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq709.gif) be an open cover of X without a finite subcover. Let I be the set of all finite subsets of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq710.gif) and order I by inclusion as in Example 2.7.2(d). By assumption, for every ![
$$i =\\{ G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq711.gif) in I there is a point x i in X such that ![
$$x_{i}\\notin \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq712.gif); {x i } is a net in X. This net does not have a cluster point. In fact, if there is a cluster point x, then there is a G in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq713.gif) that contains x. But {G} ∈ I, and so there is an ![
$$i =\\{ G,G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\} \\geq \\{ G\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq714.gif) with x i ∈ G. But by definition, ![
$$x_{i}\\notin G \\cup \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq715.gif), a contradiction. ■

The next theorem is very useful.

Theorem 2.7.10.

If X is a compact space and {x i : i ∈ I} is a net in X with a unique cluster point x, then {x i } converges to x.

Proof.

Fix a proper open set G containing x. If it were the case that the net did not converge to x, then for every i 0 in I, there would be an i in I with i ≥ i 0 and x i in ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq716.gif). This says that ![
$$J =\\{ j \\in I: x_{j} \\in X\\setminus G\\}\\neq \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq717.gif). In fact, this also says that J is directed. Indeed, if j 1, j 2 ∈ J, let i 0 ∈ I such that ![
$$i_{0} \\geq j_{1},j_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq718.gif). By what we have said, there is a j in J with ![
$$j \\geq i_{0} \\geq j_{1},j_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq719.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\{x_{j}: j \\in J\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq720.gif) is a net in ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq721.gif). Since ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq722.gif) is compact, there is a y in ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq723.gif) such that ![
$$\\{x_{j}: j \\in J\\} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}y$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq724.gif). The reader can check that this implies that y is also a cluster point of the original net. Since ![
$$y\\neq x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq725.gif), we have a contradiction. ■

### Exercises

(1)

Show that if {x n } is a sequence in a topological space, then ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq726.gif) as a sequence if and only if ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq727.gif) as a net. Show that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq728.gif) if and only if x is a limit point of ![
$$\\{x_{1},x_{2},\\ldots \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq729.gif).

(2)

Prove Proposition 2.7.7.

(3)

Prove Proposition 2.7.8(b). Is the converse true?

(4)

Show that a subset F of X is closed if and only if whenever {x i } is a net in F and x is a cluster point of this net, it follows that x ∈ F.

(5)

If (X, d) is a metric space, {x i } is a net in X, and x is a cluster point of this net, show that there are {i n : n ≥ 1} such that i 1 ≤ i 2 ≤ ⋯ and ![
$$x =\\lim _{n}x_{i_{n}}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq730.gif).

(6)

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq731.gif) be a subbase for the topology on X. (a) Show that a net {x i } in X converges to x if and only if for every S in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq732.gif) that contains x, there is an i 0 such that x i ∈ S for all i ≥ i 0. (b) Find an example of a topological space X, a subbase ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq733.gif) for the topology of X, a net {x i } in X, and a point x such that for each S in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq734.gif) that contains x and each i there is a j ≥ i such that x j ∈ S, but the net {x i } does not cluster at x.

(7)

Let ![
$$\\{{X}^{\\alpha }: \\alpha \\in A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq735.gif) be a family of topological spaces, let ![
$$X =\\prod _{\\alpha }{X}^{\\alpha }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq736.gif), let ![
$$\\{x_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq737.gif) be a net in X with each ![
$$x_{i} =\\{ x_{i}^{\\alpha }\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq738.gif), and let ![
$$x =\\{ {x}^{\\alpha }\\} \\in X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq739.gif). (a) Show that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq740.gif) if and only if ![
$$x_{i}^{\\alpha } \\rightarrow {x}^{\\alpha }$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq741.gif) for each α in A. (b) Find an example of a sequence ![
$$\\{x_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq742.gif) in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq743.gif) with ![
$$x_{n} = \(x_{n}^{1},x_{n}^{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq744.gif) and a point ![
$$x = \({x}^{1},{x}^{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq745.gif) such that ![
$$x_{n}^{1} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}{x}^{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq746.gif) and ![
$$x_{n}^{2} \\rightarrow _{\\mathrm{cl}}{x}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq747.gif) but {x n } does not cluster at x.

(8)

Find an example of a net in a topological space X that has a unique cluster point but that does not converge. See Theorem 2.7.10.

## 2.8 Quotient Spaces

We begin this section by examining the concept of an equivalence relation on a set, which is the starting point for discussing quotient spaces. I suspect some readers, perhaps many, have already encountered quotient spaces, though maybe not in their abstract formulation. Quotient spaces are frequently covered in a first course in linear algebra, for example. Nevertheless, this concept often causes students difficulty, so let us take the time to present it carefully.

Definition 2.8.1.

An equivalence relation on a set X is a relation ∼ between elements of X that satisfies the following properties: (reflexivity) x ∼ x for all x in X; (symmetry) if x ∼ y, then y ∼ x; (transitivity) if x ∼ y and y ∼ z, then x ∼ z.

By the way, if you want a formal definition of a relation, it is just a subset E of the cartesian product X × X, and we write x ∼ y when (x, y) ∈ E. When we have an equivalence relation, this imposes additional restrictions on the set E. As we progress through examples, you and a classmate, perhaps over a cup of coffee or a nice glass of Merlot, might ask what is a necessary and sufficient condition on a subset E of the product space for the corresponding relation to be an equivalence relation. In this book, we will use the definition of an equivalence relation given previously and avoid discussing the subset E of X × X.

Example 2.8.2.

(a)

Equality is an equivalence relation.

(b)

If ![
$$\\mathcal{X}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq748.gif) is a vector space over ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq749.gif), ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq750.gif) is a vector subspace, and we define x ∼ y to mean that ![
$$x - y \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq751.gif), then ∼ is an equivalence relation.

(c)

(Assuming you know the definition of a group) If G is a group, H is a subgroup, and we define x ∼ y to mean that xy −1 ∈ H, then this is an equivalence relation.

(d)

If X and Z are two sets, ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq752.gif), and we define x ∼ y to mean that f(x) = f(y), then this is an equivalence relation.

(e)

Let X be a set, and let ![
$$A \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq753.gif). Define ∼ as follows: (i) if x, y ∈ A, x ∼ y; (ii) if x∉A, then the only point y such that y ∼ x is y = x.

A partition of a set X is a collection ![
$$\\mathcal{P}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq754.gif) of nonempty subsets of X such that ![
$$P \\cap R = \\varnothing $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq755.gif) for distinct elements P and R of ![
$$\\mathcal{P}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq756.gif) and ![
$$\\bigcup \\{P: P \\in \\mathcal{P}\\} = X$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq757.gif). The proof of the next proposition is Exercise 3.

Proposition 2.8.3.

If X is a set with an equivalence relation and for each x in X we let ![
$$P_{x} =\\{ y \\in X: x \\sim y\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq758.gif) , then the collection {P x : x ∈ X} is a partition of X. Conversely, if ![
$$\\mathcal{P}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq759.gif) is a partition of X and we define x ∼ y to mean that there is a P in ![
$$\\mathcal{P}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq760.gif) such that x,y ∈ P, then ∼ is an equivalence relation on X.

So this gives us another method of defining an equivalence relation—just specify a partition. We will often talk of an equivalence relation and its associated or corresponding partition, or we will talk about a partition and its corresponding equivalence relation. For an equivalence relation on a set, the sets in the corresponding partition are called the equivalence classes of the relation.

Definition 2.8.4.

When ∼ is an equivalence relation on X, we define the quotient space X∕ ∼ to be the collection of equivalence classes. If the equivalence relation is defined by a partition ![
$$\\mathcal{P}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq761.gif), we might denote the quotient space by ![
$$X/\\mathcal{P}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq762.gif). The map ![
$$q: X \\rightarrow X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq763.gif) defined by letting ![
$$q\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq764.gif) be the equivalence class that contains x is called the quotient map or the natural map.

Note that if ![
$$\\xi \\in X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq765.gif), then q −1(ξ) is the set {x ∈ X: x ∈ ξ}. Actually, this is just the set ξ, but viewed as a subset of X rather that an element of the quotient space. So if we consider the equivalence relation defined in Example 2.8.2(b), when ![
$$x \\in \\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq766.gif), we have that ![
$$q\(x\) = x + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq767.gif).

Now let us look at what happens when we have an equivalence relation on a topological space. Unfortunately, it is not a pretty picture. We will define a topology on the quotient space, except that it will not always satisfy the Hausdorff property. There are some abstract conditions under which this topology is Hausdorff, though I have never had to resort to them to prove a quotient space Hausdorff—the quotient spaces I have encountered are usually clearly Hausdorff or clearly they are not. There are two points worth making about this. First, many quotient spaces are Hausdorff, and it is not so difficult to verify this. (See Example 3.1.7 in the next chapter. Also, Exercise 3.1.7 gives an example of a quotient space that is not Hausdorff.) Second, even when the quotient space is Hausdorff, it may not inherit other topological properties possessed by X.

The proof of the following basic result is straightforward (Exercise 5).

Proposition 2.8.5.

Let X be a topological space with an equivalence relation ∼. If ![
$$q: X \\rightarrow X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq768.gif) is the quotient map, then ![
$$\\mathcal{U} =\\{ U \\subseteq X/\\sim: {q}^{-1}\(U\)\\text{ is open in }X\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq769.gif) is a possibly non-Hausdorff topology on X∕∼ and q becomes a continuous mapping.

The topology ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq770.gif) defined in the preceding proposition is called the quotient topology on X∕ ∼ . Whenever X is a topological space and we have an equivalence relation on X, it will always be assumed that when we discuss topological ideas on the quotient space we are discussing the quotient topology. Note that because the quotient map is continuous, it follows that when X is either compact or connected, so is X∕ ∼ .

Example 2.8.6.

(a)

Let X be the closed unit square in the plane: ![
$$X = \[0,1\] \\times \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq771.gif). Define an equivalence relation ∼ on X by identifying the two vertical sides. Precisely, (0, y) ∼ (1, y) for 0 ≤ y ≤ 1, while the remaining points are identified only with themselves. The quotient space ![
$$X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq772.gif) is homeomorphic to a closed hollow cylinder in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{3}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq773.gif).

(b)

Let X be as in part (a), but define the equivalence relation ∼ by identifying the two vertical sides and by identifying the two horizontal sides. Precisely, (i) define (0, y) ∼ (1, y) for 0 ≤ y ≤ 1; (ii) define (x, 0) ∼ (x, 1) for 0 ≤ x ≤ 1; (iii) apply the transitive law to these relations to get (x, y) ∼ (w, z) when x, y, w, z ∈ { 0, 1}; (iv) the remaining points are identified only with themselves. Show that the quotient space X∕ ∼ is homeomorphic to the hollow torus (or doughnut) in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{3}$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq774.gif).

Proposition 2.8.7.

If X and Z are topological spaces, ∼ is an equivalence relation on X with quotient map ![
$$q: X \\rightarrow X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq775.gif) , and ![
$$f: X/\\sim \\ \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq776.gif) , then f is continuous if and only if ![
$$f \\circ q: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq777.gif) is continuous.

Proof.

If f is continuous, then f ∘ q is the composition of two continuous maps and so must be continuous. If f ∘ q is continuous and V is an open subset of Z, then ![
$${q}^{-1}\[{f}^{-1}\(V \)\] = {\(f \\circ q\)}^{-1}\(V \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq778.gif) and is therefore open in X. By definition, this implies f −1(V ) is open in X∕ ∼ . Therefore, f is continuous. ■

As we proceed through the rest of the book and encounter new properties of a topological space, we will address the issue of whether these properties are preserved by taking quotients.

## Exercises

(1)

Verify that each of the relations in Example 2.8.2 is an equivalence relation.

(2)

For each of the equivalence relations in Example 2.8.2 describe the corresponding partition of the underlying set X as in Proposition 2.8.3.

(3)

Prove Proposition 2.8.3.

(4)

For each equivalence relation in Example 2.8.2, describe the corresponding partition, the quotient space, and the natural map.

(5)

Prove Proposition 2.8.5.

(6)

Let X be a topological space with an equivalence relation ∼ such that X∕ ∼ is a Hausdorff space. If X is locally connected, show that X∕ ∼ is locally connected.

(7)

Define a semimetric on a set X to be a function ![
$$\\rho: X \\times X \\rightarrow \[0,\\infty \)$$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq779.gif) satisfying the following conditions for all x, y, z in X: (i) ρ(x, x) = 0; (ii) ρ(x, y) = ρ(y, x); (iii) ρ(x, y) ≤ ρ(x, z) +ρ(z, y). If ρ is a given semimetric on X, define an equivalence relation on X by x ∼ y when ρ(x, y) = 0. (a) Verify that this is an equivalence relation, and describe the equivalence classes. (b) If ![
$$q: X \\rightarrow X/\\sim $$
](A316906_1_En_2_Chapter_IEq780.gif) is the natural map, show that d(q(x), q(y)) = ρ(x, y) is a well-defined metric on X∕ ∼ . (c) Show that the metric defined in (b) defines the quotient topology on X∕ ∼ .

(8)

If X is a pathwise connected space and ∼ is an equivalence relation on X such that X∕ ∼ is Hausdorff, show that X∕ ∼ is pathwise connected.

(9)

(a) Prove the statement in Example 2.8.6(a). (b) Prove the statement in Example 2.8.6(b). (c) What happens in Example 2.8.6 if all the points on the boundary of X are identified to a single point?

Footnotes

1

Felix Hausdorff was born in 1868 in Breslau, Germany, which became Wrocław, Poland after World War II. When Hausdorff was young, his family moved to Leipzig, where he grew up and was educated. His original interests were in literature and music, but bowing to family pressure he studied astronomy and obtained a doctorate in 1891. He published four papers in the subject and obtained a habilitation in 1895. Nevertheless, he continued to pursue his interests in literature and music. He published his first literary work in 1897 under the name Paul Mongré. Books followed in philosophy in 1898 and poetry in 1900. Clearly he also worked in mathematics because in 1902 he was appointed to an extraordinary professorship of mathematics at Leipzig and turned down the offer of a similar appointment at Göttingen. He continued with literature and published a farce in 1904 that was produced and was an apparent success. After 1904, however, his efforts shifted to topology, introducing the concept of a partially ordered set (§ A.4). In 1910 he went to Bonn, and in 1913 he accepted an ordinary professorship at Greifswalf. (Hausdorff came from a rich family and had no financial worries, hence the willingness to accept a lower position.) In 1914 he published his book Grundzüge der Mengenlehre, in which he set out the theory of topological spaces, building on the work of Fréchet. In a sense, this is the start of point set topology, and the reader can find here the introduction of what we are calling the Hausdorff property. The book was reprinted several times and is a good place to practice your mathematical German. He continued to be active until 1935 when, as a Jew, he was forced by the Nazis to retire. He continued to do research but could not find an outlet for his work in Germany. He tried to emigrate in 1939 but was unsuccessful. In 1941 he was scheduled to be sent to a concentration camp, but he managed to avoid this. Bonn University requested that he and his wife be allowed to remain in their home, and this was granted. He committed suicide together with his wife and her sister in 1942.

2

James Waddell Alexander was born in 1888 in Sea Bright, New Jersey. His father was the American painter John White Alexander. In 1915 he received his doctorate from Princeton University, having previously spent time studying mathematics in Bologna and Paris. He married in 1917. During World War I he entered the army as a lieutenant and at the end of the war left as a captain. He returned to Princeton as an Assistant Professor in 1920 and was promoted in 1928 to Professor. From 1933 on he was a member of the Institute for Advanced Study. During World War II he was a civilian working with the U.S. Army Air Force at their Office of Scientific Research and Development. Because of his leftist political views he came under the scrutiny of the McCarthy Committee; this had the effect of turning him into a recluse after his retirement in 1951. His research focused on topology, particularly algebraic topology, in which he was a pioneer of cohomology theory. His named contributions include the Alexander Duality Theorem, the Alexander horned sphere, the present result, and the Alexander polynomial used in knot theory. There is also Alexander–Spanier cohomology theory, which he introduced in 1935 and that was generalized to its present form by Spanier in 1948. In addition, he impressed those who knew him as a charming man with a fondness for limericks and mountain climbing. His climbing was centered in the Swiss Alps and Colorado Rockies. Alexander's Chimney, in the Rocky Mountain National Park, is named after him. He died in 1971 in Princeton, New Jersey.

3

Andrei Nikolaevich Tikhonov was born in 1908 in Smolensk, Russia. (His name is often written Tychonoff.) He entered Moscow State University in 1922 and published his first paper in 1925 while still an undergraduate. He received his doctorate in 1927 and was appointed to the faculty of the university in 1933. He first proved the present theorem for the product of an arbitrary infinity of copies of the unit interval and in 1935 stated the full result with the comment that the proof was the same as in the special case. In 1936 he received his habilitation for work on Volterra functional equations and then was made Professor at Moscow State University. Three years later he became a Corresponding Member of the USSR Academy of Sciences. His work now concentrated on differential equations and mathematical physics. In 1966 he was awarded the Lenin Prize and was elected to full membership in the Soviet Academy of Sciences. He had a long and distinguished career including administrative positions as Dean of the Faculty of Computing and Cybernetics at Moscow State University and later as Deputy Director of the Institute of Applied Mathematics of the USSR Academy of Sciences. He died in 1993.
John B. ConwayUndergraduate Texts in MathematicsA Course in Point Set Topology201410.1007/978-3-319-02368-7_3

© Springer International Publishing Switzerland 2014

# 3. Continuous Real-Valued Functions

John B. Conway1

(1)

Department of Mathematics, The George Washington University, Washington, DC, USA

Abstract

In this chapter we will focus on continuous functions from a topological space into the space of real numbers, ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq0011.gif). We have already seen some such results in § 2.3, but in this chapter we will extend the exposure and deepen the probing.

In this chapter we will focus on continuous functions from a topological space into the space of real numbers, ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1.gif). We have already seen some such results in § 2.3, but in this chapter we will extend the exposure and deepen the probing.

## 3.1 Convergence of Functions

We want to start thinking of functions as points in a topological space. For any topological space X let C(X) denote the vector space of all continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq2.gif). [Recall Proposition 2.3.3, where it is shown that C(X) is closed under sums and products. So C(X) is not only a vector space but also an algebra. We also refer the reader to Exercise 1.3.13.] A function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq3.gif) is said to be bounded if there is a constant M with | f(x) | ≤ M for all x in X. Let C b (X) denote the space of all bounded continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq4.gif). It is easy to check that C b (X) is also closed under forming sums and products so that it too is an algebra. Of course, when X is compact, ![
$$C\(X\) = C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq5.gif).

The constant functions belong to ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq6.gif), but are there any nonconstant functions? That is a problem, and in subsequent sections we will address this question and obtain satisfactory answers. For now suffice it to say that when X is a metric space, we know from Urysohn's Lemma (Theorem 1.3.9) that C b (X) has a rich supply of continuous functions.

If ![
$$f,g \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq7.gif), define

![
$$\\displaystyle{ \\rho \(f,g\) =\\sup \\{ \\vert f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert: x \\in X\\}. }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ1.gif)

(3.1.1)

Proposition 3.1.2.

The function defined on ![
$$C_{b}\(X\) \\times C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq8.gif) in (3.1.1) is a metric on ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq9.gif) .

Proof.

If ![
$$f,g,h \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq10.gif) and x ∈ X, then ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert f\(x\) - h\(x\)\\vert + \\vert h\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq11.gif). Hence ![
$$\\rho \(f,g\) \\leq \\sup \\{\\vert f\(x\)-h\(x\)\\vert +\\vert h\(x\)-g\(x\)\\vert: x \\in X\\} \\leq \\sup \\{\\vert f\(x\)-h\(x\)\\vert;x \\in X\\}+\\sup \\{\\vert h\(x\)-g\(x\)\\vert: x \\in X\\} =\\rho \(f,h\)+\\rho \(h,g\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq12.gif). This establishes the triangle inequality. The proof that the other properties of a metric hold is routine. ■

Whenever we discuss the topological properties of C b (X) ​​, it is assumed we are discussing the topology defined by this metric. The preceding proposition is the reason we made the statement earlier that the reader should start thinking of functions in C b (X) as points; they are indeed points in a metric space. Also, see Exercise 8, where a topology is defined on C(X) when X is not assumed to be compact.

Definition 3.1.3.

If {f n } and f are bounded functions from a set X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq13.gif), then say that {f n } converges uniformly to f if for every ε > 0 there is an N such that ![
$$\\vert f_{n}\(x\) - f\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq14.gif) for all x in X and all n ≥ N. Say that {f n } is a uniformly Cauchy sequence if for every ε > 0 there is an integer N such that ![
$$\\vert f_{n}\(x\) - f_{m}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq15.gif) for all x in X and all m, n ≥ N.

This notion is possibly introduced in basic courses on calculus when the functions are defined on a subset of the real line. In any case, we have the following proposition.

Proposition 3.1.4.

Let X be a topological space, and let {f n } be a sequence in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq16.gif) . If ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq17.gif) , then {f n } converges to f in the metric of C b (X) if and only if the sequence converges uniformly to f. The sequence {f n } is a Cauchy sequence in the metric space C b (X) if and only if it is a uniformly Cauchy sequence.

Proof.

The proof is straightforward. If ![
$$\\rho \(f_{n},f\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq18.gif) and ![
$$\\epsilon> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq19.gif), then there is an N with ρ(f n , f) < ε for n ≥ N. By the definition of the metric, this implies | f n (x) − f(x) | < ε for n ≥ N for all x in X; that is, ![
$$f_{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq20.gif) uniformly on X. Now assume we have uniform convergence, ε > 0 is given, and N is as in the definition. Formula 3.1.1 implies ρ(f n , f) ≤ ε for n ≥ N, and so ![
$$f_{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq21.gif) in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq22.gif).

The proof regarding Cauchy sequences is similar to the last paragraph and is left to the reader. ■

Theorem 3.1.5.

For any topological space X, C b (X) is a complete metric space.

Proof.

Let {f n } be a Cauchy sequence in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq23.gif). The definition of the metric implies that for each x in X, ![
$$\\vert f_{n}\(x\) - f_{m}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\rho \(f_{n},f_{m}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq24.gif). Therefore, for each x in X, {f n (x)} is a Cauchy sequence in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq25.gif); define ![
$$f\(x\) =\\lim _{n}f_{n}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq26.gif). The task now is to show that ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq27.gif) and ![
$$\\rho \(f_{n},f\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq28.gif). Pay heed to the argument used here because similar arguments occur frequently.

The fact that ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq29.gif) is a bounded function is the easiest part. Let n ≥ 1 such that ρ(f n , f m ) < 1 for m, n ≥ N. Since each of the functions ![
$$f_{1},\\ldots,f_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq30.gif) is bounded, there is one constant M such that | f k (x) | ≤ M for 1 ≤ k ≤ N and all x in X. If n > N and x ∈ X, then ![
$$\\vert f_{n}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\rho \(f_{n},f_{N}\) + \\vert f_{N}\(x\)\\vert <1 + M$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq31.gif). Hence for each x the sequence ![
$$\\{f_{n}\(x\)\\} \\subseteq \[-\(M + 1\),M + 1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq32.gif), and so | f(x) | ≤ M \+ 1; that is, f is a bounded function.

Once again, fix an ε > 0, and now let N be such that ρ(f n , f m ) < ε∕3 for all m, n ≥ N. If x is an arbitrary point in X and n ≥ N, then ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f_{n}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert f\(x\) - f_{m}\(x\)\\vert +\\rho \(f_{m},f_{n}\) <\\vert f\(x\) - f_{m}\(x\)\\vert + \\epsilon /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq33.gif) for all m ≥ N. Letting ![
$$m \\rightarrow \\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq34.gif), we get that ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f_{n}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\epsilon /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq35.gif) for all n ≥ N and an arbitrary x. Thus, ![
$$\\sup \\{\\vert f\(x\) - f_{n}\(x\)\\vert: x \\in X\\} \\leq \\epsilon /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq36.gif) for all n ≥ N. Note that once we establish that f is continuous, this shows that ![
$$\\rho \(f_{n},f\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq37.gif).

To show that f is a continuous function, we continue the notation from the preceding paragraph. Fix x and fix n ≥ N. Since f n is continuous, there is a neighborhood G of x such that ![
$$\\vert f_{n}\(y\) - f_{n}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq38.gif) for all y in G. Hence, when y ∈ G, ![
$$\\vert f\(y\) - f\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert f\(y\) - f_{n}\(y\)\\vert + \\vert f_{n}\(y\) - f_{n}\(x\)\\vert + \\vert f_{n}\(x\) - f\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq39.gif). Therefore, ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq40.gif); as we mentioned before, this shows that ![
$$f_{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq41.gif) in the metric space ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq42.gif), and C b (X) is complete. ■

Recall the ordering of the functions in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq43.gif). I have always found the next result intriguing; on the other hand, I have never used it. See Exercise 6.

Proposition 3.1.6 (Dini's1 Theorem).

If X is compact, {f n } is an increasing sequence in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq44.gif) , and ![
$$f \\in C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq45.gif) such that ![
$$f_{n}\(x\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq46.gif) for all x in X, then ![
$$f_{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq47.gif) uniformly on X.

Proof.

Let ε > 0, and for each n ≥ 1 let ![
$$U_{n} =\\{ x \\in X: f\(x\) <f_{n}\(x\) + \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq48.gif). Because the functions f n are increasing with n, it follows that ![
$$U_{n} \\subseteq U_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq49.gif) for all ![
$$n \\geq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq50.gif). Since ![
$$f_{n}\(x\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq51.gif) for every x in X, we have that ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq52.gif). Finally, since both f and f n are continuous, each U n is open. Because X is compact, the open cover {U n } has a finite subcover; by the increasing property of these sets, this means there is a single integer N such that U N = X. Thus, for n ≥ N, ![
$$U_{n} \\subseteq U_{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq53.gif), and we have ![
$$f_{n}\(x\) \\leq f\(x\) <f_{n}\(x\) + \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq54.gif). By definition, ![
$$f_{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq55.gif) uniformly. ■

Let us look at an example that illustrates an instance where we have a Hausdorff quotient space.

Example 3.1.7.

Let X be a topological space, and assume F is a closed subset. Consider C b (X) ​​, and let ![
$$\\mathcal{M} =\\{ f \\in C_{b}\(X\): f\(x\) = 0\\text{ for all }x \\in F\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq56.gif). Note that ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq57.gif) is a linear subspace of the vector space C b (X) and that it is a closed subset of the metric space ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq58.gif). (Verify!) We define an equivalence relation on C b (X) by f ∼ g when ![
$$f - g \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq59.gif) and denote the quotient space by ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq60.gif). Because of the vector space structure, this is precisely the vector space quotient from linear algebra. So ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)/\\mathcal{M} =\\{ f + \\mathcal{M}: f \\in C_{b}\(X\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq61.gif) and ![
$$f + \\mathcal{M} = g + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq62.gif) if and only if ![
$$f - g \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq63.gif); equivalently, if and only if ![
$$f\(x\) = g\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq64.gif) for all x in F. We want to show that ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq65.gif) is Hausdorff. To do this, we first show that ![
$$q: C_{b}\(X\) \\rightarrow C_{b}\(X\)/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq66.gif) is an open mapping.

If Ω is any subset in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq67.gif), then we claim that

![
$$\\displaystyle{{q}^{-1}\[q\(\\Omega \)\] = \\Omega + \\mathcal{M} =\\{ h + f: h \\in \\Omega \\text{ and }f \\in \\mathcal{M}\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equa.gif)

In fact, if ![
$$g \\in {q}^{-1}\[q\(\\Omega \)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq68.gif), then ![
$$q\(g\) \\in q\(\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq69.gif), which means that there is an h in Ω such that ![
$$g - h \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq70.gif); that is, ![
$$g \\in h + \\mathcal{M}\\subseteq \\Omega + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq71.gif). Conversely, if ![
$$g = h + f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq72.gif) with ![
$$h$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq73.gif) in Ω and f in ![
$$\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq74.gif), then q(g) = q(h), so that ![
$$g \\in {q}^{-1}\[q\(\\Omega \)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq75.gif), and we have that ![
$${q}^{-1}\[q\(\\Omega \)\] = \\Omega + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq76.gif).

Therefore, if Ω is an open subset of ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq77.gif), then ![
$${q}^{-1}\[q\(\\Omega \)\] =\\bigcup \\{ \\Omega + f: f \\in \\mathcal{M}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq78.gif); since this is the union of open sets, ![
$$q\(\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq79.gif) is open.

Now suppose ![
$$f + \\mathcal{M}\\neq g + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq80.gif). Thus, there is a point ![
$$x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq81.gif) in F such that f(x) ≠ g(x); without loss of generality we may assume that f(x) < g(x). Pick any real number a satisfying f(x) < a < g(x) and define ![
$$\\Omega =\\{ u \\in C_{b}\(X\): u\(x\) <a\\},\\varLambda =\\{ v \\in C_{b}\(X\): v\(x\)> a\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq82.gif). It is left to the reader to show that Ω and Λ are open subsets of ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq83.gif) with f in Ω and g in ![
$$\\varLambda$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq84.gif). So ![
$$q\(\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq85.gif) and ![
$$q\(\\varLambda \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq86.gif) are open subsets of ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq87.gif). If ![
$$h + \\mathcal{M}\\in q\(\\Omega \) \\cap q\(\\varLambda \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq88.gif), then there must be functions u in Ω and ![
$$v$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq89.gif) in Λ such that ![
$$h - u,h - v \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq90.gif). Thus, h(x) = u(x) < a and h(x) = v(x) > a, a contradiction. Hence ![
$$q\(\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq91.gif) and ![
$$q\(\\varLambda \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq92.gif) are disjoint open sets that separate ![
$$f + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq93.gif) and ![
$$g + \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq94.gif). Therefore, the quotient space is Hausdorff.

## Exercises

(1)

Show that ![
$$C_{b}\(\\mathbb{N}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq95.gif) can be identified with the space ℓ ∞ , which was examined in Exercises 1.1.12, 1.2.11, and 1.4.11.

(2)

Say that a series ![
$$\\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }f_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq96.gif) of functions f n in C b (X) converges to f if the sequence of finite sums ![
$$\\left \\{\\sum _{k=1}^{n}f_{k}\\right \\}_{n}$$
