](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq97.gif) converges in the metric of C b (X) to f. Prove the Weierstrass M-test: if {f n } is a sequence in C b (X) such that there are constants {M n } with | f n (x) | ≤ M n for all n ≥ 1 and ![
$$\\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }M_{n} <\\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq98.gif), then there is a function f in C b (X) such that the infinite series ![
$$\\sum _{n=1}^{\\infty }f_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq99.gif) converges to f.

(3)

If (X, d) is a noncompact metric space, show that there is an unbounded continuous function from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq100.gif).

(4)

If (X, d) is a metric space and C u (X) denotes the set of all bounded uniformly continuous functions from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq101.gif), show that C u (X) is a closed subset of ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq102.gif).

(5)

Consider C b (X) and define the usual order on it: f ≤ g means f(x) ≤ g(x) for all x in X. Give ![
$$\(C_{b}\(X\),\\leq \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq103.gif) the order topology (Exercise 2.2.5). (a) Show that the order topology on C b (X) is a Hausdorff topology. (b) How does the order topology compare to the topology defined by the metric?

(6)

Give an example of a sequence {f n } in C([0, 1]) such that ![
$$f_{n}\(x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq104.gif) for every x in [0, 1] , but where {f n } does not converge uniformly on ![
$$\[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq105.gif). Note that this does not contradict Dini's Theorem.

(7)

Here is an abstraction of part of Example 3.1.7. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{X}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq106.gif) be a vector space over ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq107.gif) that has a topology such that: (i) the map ![
$$\(x,y\)\\mapsto x + y$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq108.gif) is continuous from ![
$$\\mathcal{X}\\times \\mathcal{X} \\rightarrow \\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq109.gif); (ii) the map ![
$$\(a,x\)\\mapsto ax$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq110.gif) is continuous from ![
$$\\mathbb{R} \\times \\mathcal{X} \\rightarrow \\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq111.gif). (Such a space ![
$$\\mathcal{X}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq112.gif) is called a topological vector space.) (a) If ![
$$y \\in \\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq113.gif), show that the map of ![
$$\\mathcal{X}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq114.gif) into itself defined by ![
$$x\\mapsto x + y$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq115.gif) is a homeomorphism. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq116.gif) be a vector subspace of ![
$$\\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq117.gif), and consider the quotient subspace ![
$$\\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq118.gif) with quotient map ![
$$q: \\mathcal{X} \\rightarrow \\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq119.gif). (b) Show that the quotient map is an open mapping. (c) Show that ![
$$\\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq120.gif) is Hausdorff if and only if, when ![
$$x\\notin \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq121.gif), there are disjoint open sets U and V in ![
$$\\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq122.gif) such that ![
$$\\mathcal{M} = q\(0\) \\in U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq123.gif) and ![
$$x + \\mathcal{M} = q\(x\) \\in V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq124.gif). (d) Show that if ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq125.gif) is not a closed subset of ![
$$\\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq126.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq127.gif) cannot be Hausdorff. (e) If we take ![
$$\\mathcal{X} = C_{b}\(\\mathbb{N}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq128.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{M} =\\{ f \\in C_{b}\(\\mathbb{N}\): \\text{ there is an integer }N \\geq 2\\text{ with }f\(n\) = 0\\text{ for }n \\leq N\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq129.gif), show that ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq130.gif) is a linear subspace of ![
$$\\mathcal{X}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq131.gif). Is ![
$$\\mathcal{X}/\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq132.gif) Hausdorff?

(8)

Let X and Z be topological spaces, and denote by C(X, Z) the set of all continuous functions from X into Z. When K is a compact subset of X and G is an open subset of Z, let

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\Omega \(K,G\) =\\{ h \\in C\(X,Z\): h\(K\) \\subseteq G\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equb.gif)

(a) Show that the collection of all such sets ![
$$\\Omega \(K,G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq133.gif) is a subbase for a topology on C(X, Z). This topology is called the compact-open topology, denoted by (co). (b) If the space X consists of a single point x 0, show that there is a homeomorphism between Z and the space ![
$$C\(\\{x_{0}\\},Z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq134.gif) with (co). (c) Show that C([0, 1], [0, 1]) with (co) is not a compact space. (Hint: examine the sequence {t n } in C([0, 1], [0, 1]).) (d) Let ![
$$Y = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq135.gif) so that ![
$$C\(X,Y \) = C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq136.gif). For each f in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq137.gif), each compact subset K of X, and each ![
$$\\epsilon> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq138.gif), let

![
$$\\displaystyle{S_{K,\\epsilon }\(f\) = \\left \\{g \\in C\(X\):\\sup \\{ \(f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert: x \\in K\\} <\\epsilon \\right \\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equc.gif)

Show that the collection of all the sets ![
$$S_{K,\\epsilon }\(f\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq139.gif) is a subbase for the (co) on ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq140.gif). (e) If X is a compact space, show that the (co) topology C(X) is the same as the topology defined by the metric ρ given at the start of this section. (f) Show that a net {f i } in C(X) converges in (co) to f if and only if for every compact subset K of X, ![
$$f_{i}\(x\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq141.gif) uniformly on K.

## 3.2 Separation Properties

Here we begin to explore various ways of separating disjoint closed subsets of a topological space by disjoint open sets as well as by functions in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq142.gif). In a sense, we incorporated one such separation into the definition of a topological space when we assumed all spaces had the Hausdorff property, which says that singleton sets can be separated. In this section, we introduce two levels of separation, and in the next section we will take separation to a further level.

Definition 3.2.1.

A topological space X is regular if for every closed set F and any point x not belonging to F there are disjoint open sets U and V such that x ∈ U and ![
$$F \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq143.gif).

We are not that interested in regular spaces, though we will prove some results about them below. Suffice it to say that there are topological spaces that are not regular (Exercise 1), but they tend to be pathological.

Proposition 3.2.2.

If X is a topological space, then the following statements are equivalent.

(a)

X is regular.

(b)

If G is an open set and x ∈ G, then there is an open set U such that ![
$$x \\in U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq144.gif) .

(c)

If F is a closed set and ![
$$x\\notin F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq145.gif) , then there is an open set ![
$$V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq146.gif) such that ![
$$F \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq147.gif) and ![
$$x\\notin \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq148.gif) .

Proof.

Assume (a). If G and x are as in the statement of (b), then ![
$$F = X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq149.gif) is closed and x∉F. If U and V are disjoint open sets such that x ∈ U and ![
$$F \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq150.gif), then ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq X\\setminus V \\subseteq X\\setminus F = G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq151.gif). Now assume (b) holds and x and F are as in (c). Apply (b) with ![
$$G = X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq152.gif) to obtain an open set U such that ![
$$x \\in U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq153.gif). Put ![
$$V = X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq154.gif). It follows that ![
$$V \\subseteq X\\setminus U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq155.gif), so that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V \\subseteq X\\setminus U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq156.gif); in particular, ![
$$x\\notin \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq157.gif). Now assume (c). If F is closed and ![
$$x\\notin F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq158.gif), then (c) implies the existence of an open set V as stated there. Thus, ![
$$U = X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq159.gif) is open, x ∈ U, and ![
$$U \\cap V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq160.gif). Thus, X is regular. ■

Proposition 3.2.3.

(a)

If X is regular and ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq161.gif) , then E with its relative topology is regular.

(b)

If ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq162.gif) , then X is regular if and only if and each X i is regular.

Proof.

(a)

The proof of this part is Exercise 2.

(b)

Assume each X i is regular. If G is an open set in X and x ∈ G, then the definition of the topology on X there are indices ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq163.gif) and open sets ![
$$V _{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq164.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq165.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}p_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(V _{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq166.gif). Since each ![
$$X_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq167.gif) is regular, Proposition 3.2.2 implies there is an open subset ![
$$U_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq168.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq169.gif) with ![
$$p_{i_{k}}\(x\) \\in U_{i_{k}} \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U_{i_{k}} \\subseteq V _{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq170.gif). Thus, ![
$$x \\in U =\\bigcap _{ k=1}^{n}p_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(U_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}p_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(\\text{cl}\\,U_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}p_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(V _{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq171.gif). By the preceding proposition X is regular.

The proof of the converse is Exercise 7. ■

Proposition 3.2.4.

Every compact space and every metric space is regular.

Proof.

Assume X is compact, F is a closed subset of X, and ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq172.gif). For each y in F let U y and V y be disjoint open sets in X such that x ∈ U y and y ∈ V y . It follows that ![
$$\\{V _{y}: y \\in F\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq173.gif) is an open cover of F. Since F is compact, there are ![
$$y_{1},\\ldots,y_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq174.gif) in Y such that ![
$$F \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}V _{y_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq175.gif). Put ![
$$V =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}V _{y_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq176.gif) and ![
$$U =\\bigcap _{ k=1}^{n}U_{y_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq177.gif). It follows that U and V are disjoint open sets with x in U and F contained in V.

Now assume we have a metric space ![
$$\(X,d\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq178.gif). If F is closed and ![
$$x\\notin F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq179.gif), then F and {x} are disjoint closed sets. By Urysohn's Lemma (Theorem 1.3.9) there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq180.gif) with f(x) = 1 and f(y) = 0 for every y in F. Hence ![
$$U =\\{ y: f\(y\)> \\frac{1} {2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq181.gif) and ![
$$V =\\{ y: f\(y\) <\\frac{1} {2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq182.gif) are disjoint open sets that separate x from F. ■

The proof just given, that metric spaces are regular, anticipates the next level of separation.

Definition 3.2.5.

A topological space X is completely regular if for any closed subset F and any point x in ![
$$X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq183.gif) there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq184.gif) such that ![
$$f\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq185.gif) and f(y) = 0 for all points y in F.

Clearly, every completely regular space is regular, as shown by the argument used to prove that a metric space is regular. Constructing an example of a regular space that is not completely regular takes some work. We will not do this because such spaces are atypical of the kind most mathematicians will encounter, but the interested reader can see [9] or Example 3 on page. 154 of [4].

Many define a space to be completely regular if there is a continuous function ![
$$h: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq186.gif) such that h(x) = 1 and h(y) = 0 for all y in F. This is equivalent to the definition. In fact, suppose that ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq187.gif) is as in the preceding definition. If we put ![
$$g = f \\vee 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq188.gif) and then h = g ∧ 1, we see that ![
$$h: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq189.gif) is a continuous function (Proposition 2.3.11) with the required properties. Let us record this.

Proposition 3.2.6.

A topological space X is completely regular if and only if for any closed subset F and any point x in ![
$$X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq190.gif) there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq191.gif) such that f(x) = 1 and f(y) = 0 for all y in F.

Example 3.2.7.

(a)

If X is a topological space such that for every closed set F and every point x in ![
$$X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq192.gif) there is a function ![
$$g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq193.gif) such that g(y) = 0 for all y in F and g(x) ≠ 0, then X is completely regular. In fact, if such a function exists, then ![
$$f\(y\) = g{\(x\)}^{-1}g\(y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq194.gif) shows that X satisfies the definition of completely regular.

(b)

Every metric space (X, d) is completely regular. This follows from (a) since ![
$$g\(y\) = \\text{dist}\\,\(y,F\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq195.gif) is continuous. Also, Urysohn's Lemma readily proves this, as we saw in Proposition 3.2.4.

Proposition 3.2.8.

If X is completely regular and Y is a subset of X and has its relative topology from X, then Y is completely regular.

Proof.

Let D be a relatively closed subset of Y, and let ![
$$y \\in Y \\setminus D$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq196.gif). There is a closed subset F of X such that ![
$$F \\cap Y = D$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq197.gif); so y∉F. Thus, there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq198.gif) with f(y) = 1 and f(z) = 0 for all z in F. If g = f | Y, then g ∈ C(Y ), g(y) = 1, and g(z) = 0 for all z in D. Therefore, Y is completely regular. ■

Completely regular spaces guarantee the existence of many nonconstant continuous functions. This is underlined by the next theorem. Recall the definition of the weak topology on a set defined by a collection of functions (Proposition 2.6.2). We will need the following lemma.

Lemma 3.2.9.

If ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq199.gif) is the weak topology defined on X by the collection of functions ![
$$\\{f_{i}: X \\rightarrow X_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq200.gif) , then a net ![
$$\\{x_{\\alpha }\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq201.gif) in X converges to x in ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq202.gif) if and only if ![
$$f_{i}\(x_{\\alpha }\) \\rightarrow f_{i}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq203.gif) for all i in I.

Proof.

Since each function ![
$$f_{i}: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq204.gif) is continuous, the proof of half the corollary is immediate. For the other half assume that ![
$$f_{i}\(x_{\\alpha }\) \\rightarrow f_{i}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq205.gif) for all i in I. If ![
$$G \\in \\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq206.gif) and x ∈ G, then the definition of a subbase implies there are ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq207.gif) in I and open sets ![
$$U_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq208.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq209.gif) for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq210.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}f_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(U_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq211.gif). Since ![
$$f_{i_{k}}\(x_{\\alpha }\) \\rightarrow f_{i_{k}}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq212.gif), there is an ![
$$\\alpha _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq213.gif) with ![
$$f_{i_{k}}\(x_{\\alpha }\) \\in U_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq214.gif) for all ![
$$\\alpha \\geq \\alpha _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq215.gif). If we let ![
$$\\alpha _{0} \\geq \\alpha _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq216.gif) for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq217.gif), then when ![
$$\\alpha \\geq \\alpha _{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq218.gif), we have that ![
$$x_{\\alpha } \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}f_{i_{k}}^{-1}\(U_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq219.gif). Hence ![
$$x_{\\alpha } \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq220.gif) in ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T} \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq221.gif). ■

Theorem 3.2.10.

If X is a topological space, then the following statements are equivalent.

(a)

X is completely regular.

(b)

The topology on X is the weak topology defined by the functions in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq222.gif) .

(c)

The topology on X is the weak topology defined by the functions in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq223.gif) .

(d)

A net {x i } in X converges to x if and only if ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq224.gif) for each continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq225.gif) .

Proof.

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{T}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq226.gif) denote the topology on X, ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{c}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq227.gif) the weak topology on X defined by ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq228.gif), and ![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{b}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq229.gif) the weak topology on X defined by ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq230.gif).

(a) and (d) are equivalent. If ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq231.gif), then ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq232.gif) for every continuous function, even without the assumption of complete regularity. Now assume X is completely regular and ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq233.gif) for each continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq234.gif). If G is a neighborhood of x, then Proposition 3.2.6 implies there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq235.gif) with f(x) = 1 and f(y) = 0 for all y in ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq236.gif). Thus, there is an i 0 such that for ![
$$i \\geq i_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq237.gif) we have that ![
$$f\(x_{i}\)> \\frac{1} {2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq238.gif), which says that ![
$$x_{i} \\in G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq239.gif).

(a) implies (b). We want to show that when X is completely regular, ![
$$\\mathcal{T} = \\mathcal{T}_{c}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq240.gif); this means we want to show that if ![
$$\\tau: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow \(X,\\mathcal{T}_{c}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq241.gif) is the identity map, then τ is a homeomorphism. If {x i } is a net in X and ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq242.gif) (![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq243.gif)), then ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq244.gif) for every ![
$$f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq245.gif) in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq246.gif). By Lemma 3.2.9, this implies ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq247.gif) (![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{c}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq248.gif)), so τ is continuous. Conversely, if ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq249.gif) (![
$$\\mathcal{T}_{c}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq250.gif)), then ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq251.gif) for every f in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq252.gif). Since (a) and (d) are equivalent, this implies that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq253.gif) (![
$$\\mathcal{T}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq254.gif)). Thus, τ is a homeomorphism.

(b) implies (c). As in the preceding paragraph, we want to show that the identity map ![
$$\\tau: \(X,\\mathcal{T} \) \\rightarrow \(X,\\mathcal{T}_{b}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq255.gif) is a homeomorphism. Clearly, τ is continuous. Now assume ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq256.gif) in ![
$$\(X,\\mathcal{T}_{b}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq257.gif). Since (b) holds, we want to show that ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq258.gif) for every f in ![
$$C\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq259.gif). Let (a, b) be an arbitrary bounded open interval in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq260.gif) such that a < f(x) < b. For each y in X, define ![
$$g\(y\) =\\min \\{ f\(y\),b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq261.gif) and h(y) = max{g(y), a}. Since ![
$$h \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq262.gif) and h(x) = f(x), there is an i 0 such that a < h(x i ) < b when i ≥ i 0. Fix i ≥ i 0. From the definition of h we get that ![
$$a <h\(x_{i}\) = g\(x_{i}\) <b$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq263.gif). Again from the definition of g we have that a < g(x i ) = f(x i ) < b. Since (a, b) was arbitrary, we have that ![
$$f\(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq264.gif), completing the proof of this part.

(c) implies (d). Since each continuous function from X into the closed unit interval belongs to ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq265.gif), this is immediate by the lemma. ■

So the preceding theorem says, in vivid terms, that the topology of a completely regular space is determined by its real-valued continuous functions.

Theorem 3.2.11.

If ![
$$\\{X_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq266.gif) is a collection of topological spaces and ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq267.gif) , then X is completely regular if and only if each X i is completely regular.

Proof.

First assume that X is completely regular, fix a j in I, let F j be a closed subset of X j , and let ![
$$x_{j} \\in X_{j}\\setminus F_{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq268.gif). If x is any point in X whose jth coordinate is x j , then ![
$$x\\notin \\pi _{j}^{-1}\(F_{j}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq269.gif), and this latter set is closed. Let ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq270.gif) be a continuous function with f(x) = 1 and f(y) = 0 for all y in π j −1(F). Now define the map from ![
$$X_{j} \\rightarrow X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq271.gif) by ![
$$z\\mapsto \\{z_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq272.gif), where z j = z and z i = x i for all i ≠ j. Using nets it follows that this map is continuous. (Verify!) Thus ![
$$f_{j}: X_{j} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq273.gif) defined by ![
$$f_{j}\(z\) = f\(\\{z_{i}\\}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq274.gif) is the composition of two continuous functions and is therefore continuous. Also, ![
$$f_{j}\(x_{j}\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq275.gif) and f j (y j ) = 0 for all y j in F j . Therefore, X j is completely regular.

Now assume each X i is completely regular, let F be a closed subset of X, and let ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq276.gif). From the definition of the product topology this implies there are indices ![
$$i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq277.gif) in I and, for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, there is a neighborhood ![
$$G_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq278.gif) of the point ![
$$x_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq279.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in \\bigcap _{k=1}^{n}\\pi _{i_{k}}^{-1}\(G_{i_{k}}\) \\subseteq X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq280.gif). For 1 ≤ k ≤ n, let ![
$$f_{i_{k}}: X_{i_{k}} \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq281.gif) such that ![
$$f_{i_{k}}\(x_{i_{k}}\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq282.gif) and ![
$$f_{i_{k}}\(y_{i_{k}}\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq283.gif) for all ![
$$y_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq284.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{k}}\\setminus G_{i_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq285.gif). Define ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq286.gif) by ![
$$f\(\\{y_{i}\\}\) = f_{i_{1}}\(y_{i_{1}}\)\\cdots f_{i_{n}}\(y_{i_{n}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq287.gif). This function f is continuous (Exercise 12), f(x) = 1, and f(y) = 0 for y in F. ■

## Exercises

(1)

If X is the real line with the topology generated by the subbase consisting of all the open intervals and the set ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq288.gif), show that X is Hausdorff but not regular.

(2)

Prove that if X is a regular topological space and ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq289.gif), then E with its relative topology is regular.

(3)

Suppose ![
$$X = F_{1} \\cup F_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq290.gif), where F 1 and F 2 are closed, and F 1 ∩ F 2 = ∅. Show that if both F 1 and F 2 with their relative topologies are regular, then X is regular.

(4)

If X is a topological space and for each point x in X there is an open set G such that x ∈ G and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq291.gif) with its relative topology is a regular space, then X is regular.

(5)

Show that X is regular if and only if for any two distinct points x and y there are open sets U and V such that x ∈ U, y ∈ V, and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U \\cap \\text{cl}\\,V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq292.gif).

(6)

If X is regular and A is a closed subset of X, show that

![
$$\\displaystyle{A =\\bigcap \\{ U: U\\text{ is open and }A \\subseteq U\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equd.gif)

Is the converse true?

(7)

Complete the proof of Proposition 3.2.3(b). (Hint: use Exercise 2.6.1.)

(8)

As in Example 2.8.2(e), for a topological space X and a subset F define the equivalence relation on X by x ∼ y when x = y or when x, y ∈ F. Denote the resulting quotient space by X∕F. (a) Show that if F is a closed set and X is regular, then X∕F is Hausdorff. (b) Show that if X∕F is Hausdorff for every closed subset F of X, then X is regular.

(9)

Let X be a topological space, and define a relation ∼ on X by declaring that x ∼ y if f(x) = f(y) for every continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq293.gif). (a) Show that ∼ is an equivalence relation on X. (b) Show that X∕ ∼ is completely regular.

(10)

If X is a connected completely regular space that is not a singleton, show that X has uncountably many points.

(11)

Say that a topological space Z has Property P if for any two distinct points z, w in Z there is a continuous function ![
$$f: Z \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq294.gif) such that f(z) ≠ f(w). Show that X is completely regular if and only if for every closed subset F of X the quotient space X∕F (Exercise 8) has Property P.

(12)

Show that the function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq295.gif) defined in the second half of the proof of Theorem 3.2.11 is continuous.

## 3.3 Normal Spaces

This section continues the last one with the final separation property. The reader should be alert because this section contains a disproportionately large number of significant results.

Definition 3.3.1.

A topological space X is normal if for any pair of disjoint closed subsets A and B there are disjoint open sets U and V such that ![
$$A \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq296.gif) and ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq297.gif).

Unlike normal subgroups in algebra, normal topological spaces are the usual occurrence in most parts of mathematics. Exceptions can be found, but perhaps the title "normal" is deserved for these topological spaces. It is immediate that a normal space is regular because every singleton set is a closed set. It is far from clear that a normal space is completely regular, but this follows from one of the deeper results on normal spaces, an extension of Urysohn's Lemma to this setting. (See Theorem 3.3.4 below.)

Proposition 3.3.2.

If X is a topological space, then the following statements are equivalent.

(a)

X is normal.

(b)

If A is a closed set and G is an open set with ![
$$A \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq298.gif) , then there is an open set U with ![
$$A \\subseteq U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq299.gif) .

(c)

If A and B are disjoint closed sets, then there is an open set V such that ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq300.gif) and ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq301.gif) .

Proof.

Assume (a) and let A and G be as in (b). Let ![
$$B = X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq302.gif), and apply the definition of normality to find disjoint open sets U and V with ![
$$A \\subseteq U,B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq303.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq X\\setminus V \\subseteq X\\setminus B = G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq304.gif). Now assume (b) and let A and B be as in (c). If ![
$$G = X\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq305.gif), then G is open and ![
$$A \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq306.gif). Thus, there is an open set U with ![
$$A \\subseteq U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq X\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq307.gif). Put ![
$$V = X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq308.gif); clearly, ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq309.gif). Since ![
$$V \\subseteq X\\setminus U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq310.gif), we have that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V \\subseteq X\\setminus U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq311.gif), and so ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq312.gif). Now assume that (c) holds and A and B are disjoint closed sets. If V is as in (c) and ![
$$U = X\\setminus \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq313.gif), then U and V are disjoint open sets, ![
$$A \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq314.gif), and ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq315.gif). Therefore, X is normal. ■

Proposition 3.3.3.

(a)

Every metric space is normal.

(b)

Every compact space is normal.

(c)

If X is normal and F is a closed subset of X, then F with its relative topology is normal.

Proof.

(a)

This is an easy application of Theorem 1.3.9.

(b)

By Proposition 3.2.4, X is regular. Thus, if A and B are disjoint closed sets, then for every point a in A there are disjoint open sets U a and V a such that a ∈ U a and ![
$$B \\subseteq V _{a}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq316.gif). Now {U a : a ∈ A} is an open cover of A, which, being a closed subset of a compact space, is compact. Thus, there are points ![
$$a_{1},\\ldots,a_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq317.gif) with ![
$$A \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}U_{a_{k}} \\equiv U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq318.gif); put ![
$$V =\\bigcap _{ k=1}^{n}V _{a_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq319.gif). It follows that U and V are disjoint open sets, ![
$$A \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq320.gif), and ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq321.gif).

(c)

Exercise 2.

■

It is not true that every subspace of a normal space is normal, nor is it the case that the product of even two normal spaces is normal. The reader can consult Example 3 on page. 144 of [4] for two normal spaces whose product is not normal. (This reference also contains an example of a regular space that is not normal.) Finding an example of a subspace of a normal space that is not normal requires additional effort. See Example 4 on page. 145 of [4], where an open subset of a compact space is shown not to be normal. (This uses spaces of ordinal numbers, which is discussed subsequently in § 3.6.) We might mention that every subspace of a normal space is, however, completely regular. (See Exercise 7.)

Recall Urysohn's Lemma proved for metric spaces (Theorem 1.3.9). Here we get the same conclusion for normal spaces, though the proof is more difficult. Before stating and proving this result we need some facts about dyadic rational numbers. Let D 0 = { 0, 1}, and for n ≥ 1 let ![
$$D_{n} =\\{ \\frac{a} {{2}^{n}}: a \\in \\mathbb{N},a\\text{ is odd,}\\text{ and }0 <a <{2}^{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq322.gif). Put ![
$$D =\\bigcup _{ n=0}^{\\infty }D_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq323.gif); this is the set of dyadic rational numbers in the closed unit interval. This set has many interesting properties, including the fact that it is dense in the unit interval (Exercise 5). There is more on dyadic rational numbers in § A.5.

Theorem 3.3.4 (Urysohn's Lemma).

If X is normal and A and B are disjoint closed subsets, then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq324.gif) such that f(a) = 1 for all a in A and f(b) = 0 for all b in B.

Proof.

We let ![
$$G = X\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq325.gif); so ![
$$A \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq326.gif). The strategy of the proof is as follows: if D is the set of dyadic rational numbers in [0, 1], then for every t in D we will construct a subset U t of X satisfying the following:

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\left \\{\\begin{array}{@{}l@{\\quad }l@{}} \\text{\(i\)}\\quad \\quad &U_{t}\\text{ is open when }t <1 \\\\ \\text{\(ii\)}\\quad \\quad &\\text{cl}\\,U_{t} \\subseteq U_{s}\\text{ for }s <t.\\end{array} \\right.& &{}\\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ2.gif)

(3.3.5)

We will then use this family to construct the continuous function, but first let us get the sets. For this we use induction where of each n ≥ 0 we will construct the sets {U t } when t belongs to the set D n defined before the proof.

We start with n = 0 and the set D 0. Let U 1 = A and ![
$$U_{0} = G = X\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq327.gif). [Given that ![
$$U_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq328.gif) is closed, we note that (3.3.5)(i) is not violated.] When n = 1, we have ![
$$D_{1} =\\{ \\frac{1} {2}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq329.gif). Since X is normal, we can find an open set ![
$$U_{\\frac{1} {2} }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq330.gif) with ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{1} = A \\subseteq U_{\\frac{1} {2} } \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U_{\\frac{1} {2} } \\subseteq U_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq331.gif). Let ![
$$n \\geq 2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq332.gif), and assume we have constructed the sets U t for t in ![
$$\\bigcup _{k=0}^{n-1}D_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq333.gif) and that they satisfy (3.3.5). Let ![
$$t = \\frac{a} {{2}^{n}} \\in D_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq334.gif), with a odd. Since a is an odd integer, ![
$$\\alpha = \\frac{a-1} {{2}^{n}},\\beta = \\frac{a+1} {{2}^{n}} \\in \\bigcup _{k=0}^{n-1}D_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq335.gif), and so ![
$$U_{\\alpha }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq336.gif) and ![
$$U_{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq337.gif) are already defined with ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{\\beta } \\subseteq U_{\\alpha }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq338.gif). Since X is normal, there is an open set U t with ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{\\beta } \\subseteq U_{t} \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U_{t} \\subseteq U_{\\alpha }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq339.gif). In this way, we define U t satisfying (3.3.5) for all t in ![
$$\\bigcup _{k=0}^{n}D_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq340.gif). By mathematical induction we have the sets U t for all dyadic rational numbers.

Now to define f. When ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus U_{0} = B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq341.gif), let f(x) = 0; when ![
$$x \\in U_{0} = G = X\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq342.gif), define ![
$$f\(x\) =\\sup \\{ t \\in D: x \\in U_{t}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq343.gif). It is clear that f(x) = 1 for x in A = U 1 and 0 ≤ f(x) ≤ 1 for all x in X. What must be proven is that f is continuous. For this let ![
$$0 \\leq \\alpha <\\beta \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq344.gif). Note that ![
$$\\alpha <f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq345.gif) if and only if x ∈ U t for some ![
$$t> \\alpha$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq346.gif); thus,

![
$$\\displaystyle{{f}^{-1}\\left \(\(\\alpha,1\]\\right \) =\\bigcup \\{ U_{ t}: \\alpha <t\\},}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Eque.gif)

an open set. Also, ![
$$f\(x\) \\geq \\beta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq347.gif) if and only if x ∈ U t for every ![
$$t <\\beta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq348.gif); thus,

![
$$\\displaystyle{{f}^{-1}\(\[\\beta,1\]\) =\\bigcap \\{ U_{ t}: t <\\beta \\} =\\bigcap \\{ \\text{cl}\\,U_{s}: s <\\beta \\},}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equf.gif)

a closed set. Hence, ![
$${f}^{-1}\(\[0,\\beta \)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq349.gif) is open, so that ![
$${f}^{-1}\(\(\\alpha,\\beta \)\) = {f}^{-1}\\left \(\(\\alpha,1\]\\right \) \\cap {f}^{-1}\(\[0,\\beta \)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq350.gif) is open. Therefore, ![
$$f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq351.gif) is continuous. (Why?) ■

Corollary 3.3.6.

If X is normal, A and B are disjoint closed subsets, and α and β are real numbers with ![
$$\\alpha <\\beta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq352.gif) , then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[\\alpha,\\beta \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq353.gif) such that ![
$$f\(a\) = \\alpha$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq354.gif) for all a in A and ![
$$f\(b\) = \\beta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq355.gif) for all b in B.

Proof.

Note that ![
$$\\tau \(t\) = t\\beta + \(1 - t\)\\alpha$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq356.gif) is a homeomorphism of [0, 1] onto ![
$$\[\\alpha,\\beta \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq357.gif), with ![
$$\\tau \(0\) = \\alpha,\\tau \(1\) = \\beta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq358.gif). According to Urysohn's Lemma there is a continuous function ![
$$g: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq359.gif) with g(a) = 0 for all a in A and g(b) = 1 for all b in B. The function f = τ ∘ g is the desired function. ■

Corollary 3.3.7.

Every normal space is completely regular.

Next we show that normal spaces enjoy a special property—continuous functions defined on a closed subset can be extended to the entire space. It is a deep property, and its establishment starts with a lemma.

Lemma 3.3.8.

If X is a normal space, C is a closed subset of X, and ![
$$f: C \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq360.gif) is a continuous function with ![
$$\\vert f\(c\)\\vert \\leq \\gamma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq361.gif) for all c in C, then there is a continuous function ![
$$g: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq362.gif) satisfying the following for all x in X and all c in C:

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\mathrm{\(i\)}\\quad & & \\vert g\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\gamma /3 {}\\\\ \\mathrm{\(ii\)}\\quad & & \\vert f\(c\) - g\(c\)\\vert \\leq 2\\gamma /3. {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ3.gif)

Proof.

Let ![
$$A =\\{ c \\in C: f\(c\) \\leq -\\gamma /3\\},B =\\{ c \\in C: f\(c\) \\geq \\gamma /3\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq363.gif). Since f is continuous on the closed set C, A and B are disjoint closed subsets of X. By Corollary 3.3.6, there is a continuous function ![
$$g: X \\rightarrow \[-\\gamma /3,\\gamma /3\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq364.gif) with ![
$$g\(a\) = -\\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq365.gif) for all a in A and ![
$$g\(b\) = \\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq366.gif) for all b in B. So g satisfies (i). We show that it also satisfies (ii) on a case-by-case basis. Let c ∈ C. First note that if c ∈ A, then ![
$$-\\gamma \\leq f\(c\) \\leq -\\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq367.gif) and ![
$$g\(c\) = -\\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq368.gif), so (ii) holds. Similarly, (ii) holds for c in B. If ![
$$c\\notin \(A \\cup B\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq369.gif), then ![
$$\\vert f\(c\)\\vert <\\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq370.gif); since ![
$$\\vert g\(c\)\\vert \\leq \\gamma /3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq371.gif), (ii) is also valid in this case. ■

Theorem 3.3.9 (Tietze's2 Extension Theorem).

If X is a normal topological space, C is a closed subset of X, and ![
$$f: C \\rightarrow \[\\alpha,\\beta \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq372.gif) is a continuous function, then there is a continuous function ![
$$F: X \\rightarrow \[\\alpha,\\beta \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq373.gif) such that ![
$$F\(c\) = f\(c\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq374.gif) for every c in C.

Proof.

Using the map τ in the proof of Corollary 3.3.6, it suffices to assume that ![
$$f: C \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq375.gif). Applying the preceding lemma we can find a continuous function ![
$$g_{0}: X \\rightarrow \[-{3}^{-1},{3}^{-1}\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq376.gif) such that ![
$$\\vert f\(c\) - g_{0}\(c\)\\vert \\leq 2/3$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq377.gif) for all c in C. Now apply the lemma again to the function ![
$$\(f - g_{0}\): C \\rightarrow \[-2/3,2/3\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq378.gif) to obtain a continuous function ![
$$g_{1}: X \\rightarrow \[-{3}^{-1}\(2/3\),{3}^{-1}\(2/3\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq379.gif) such that ![
$$\\vert \(f - g_{0}\)\(c\) - g_{1}\(c\)\\vert \\leq {\(2/3\)}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq380.gif). Continuing this line of reasoning and applying induction, we can establish the following claim.

Claim.

For each integer n ≥ 1 there is a continuous function ![
$$g_{n}: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq381.gif) satisfying the following: (i) ![
$$\\vert g_{n}\(x\)\\vert \\leq {3}^{-1}{\(2/3\)}^{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq382.gif) for all x in X; (ii) ![
$$\\left \\vert f\(c\) -\\sum _{k=0}^{n}g_{k}\(c\)\\right \\vert \\leq {\(2/3\)}^{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq383.gif) for all c in C.

The details of the induction argument required to establish this claim are left to the reader (Exercise 8).

Because of (i) in the claim, the Weierstrass M-test (Exercise 3.1.2) implies ![
$$g =\\sum _{ n=0}^{\\infty }g_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq384.gif) converges in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq385.gif); so ![
$$g \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq386.gif) and, summing the appropriate geometric series, for every x in X we have | g(x) | ≤ 1. Also (ii) in the claim implies that g is an extension of f. The only thing lacking is that g may take on negative values. If, however, we put ![
$$F = g \\vee 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq387.gif); then F is continuous (Proposition 2.3.11), F(x) ∈ [0, 1] for all x, and, because f is nonnegative, F is an extension of f. ■

A fact worth emphasizing in the Tietze Extension Theorem is that the image of the extension F is contained in the same interval that contains the image of f. This is not to say, however, that the functions f and F have the same image. (Can you furnish an example where the ranges differ? You might find one with ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq388.gif).)

The next result is an important application of Urysohn's Lemma.

Theorem 3.3.10 (Partition of Unity).

If X is normal and ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq389.gif) is an open cover of X, then there are continuous functions ![
$$\\phi _{1},\\ldots,\\phi _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq390.gif) from X into ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq391.gif) with the following properties:

(a)

![
$$0 \\leq \\phi _{k}\(x\) \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq392.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n;

(b)

ϕ k (x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq393.gif) and 1 ≤ k ≤ n;

(c)

![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\phi _{k}\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq394.gif) for all x in X.

Proof.

We prove this by induction. First assume n = 2: ![
$$X = G_{1} \\cup G_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq395.gif). ![
$$X\\setminus G_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq396.gif) is a closed set that is contained in G 1 and so Urysohn's Lemma implies there is a continuous function ![
$$\\phi _{1}: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq397.gif) with ![
$$\\phi _{1}\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq398.gif) on ![
$$X\\setminus G_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq399.gif) and 0 on ![
$$X\\setminus G_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq400.gif). Put ![
$$\\phi _{2} = 1 -\\phi _{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq401.gif). It is routine to check that the three conditions are satisfied. Now assume the theorem is true for some n ≥ 2, and let ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n},G_{n+1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq402.gif) be an open cover of X. The induction hypothesis applied to the open cover ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n-1},G_{n} \\cup G_{n+1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq403.gif) implies the existence of continuous functions ![
$$\\psi _{k}: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq404.gif) for ![
$$1 \\leq k \\leq n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq405.gif) such that ![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\psi _{k} = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq406.gif), ψ k (x) = 0 for x∉G k and 1 ≤ k ≤ n − 1, and ψ n (x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin G_{n} \\cup G_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq407.gif). Now consider the open cover of X consisting of the two sets ![
$$G_{1} \\cup \\cdots \\cup G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq408.gif) and G n+1, and find continuous functions ![
$$\\theta _{1},\\theta _{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq409.gif) with ![
$$\\theta _{1} + \\theta _{2} = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq410.gif), ![
$$\\theta _{1}\(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq411.gif) when ![
$$x\\notin G_{1} \\cup \\cdots G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq412.gif), and ![
$$\\theta _{2}\(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq413.gif) when x∉G n+1. Put ![
$$\\phi _{k} = \\theta _{1}\\psi _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq414.gif) when 1 ≤ k ≤ n and ![
$$\\phi _{n+1} = \\theta _{2}\\psi _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq415.gif). Clearly, each of these functions has its range in the closed unit interval, so (a) is satisfied. Also ![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n+1}\\phi _{k} = \\theta _{1}\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\psi _{k} + \\theta _{2}\\psi _{n} = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq416.gif), so (c) holds. If 1 ≤ k ≤ n − 1 and ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x\)> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq417.gif), then ψ k (x) > 0; so it must be that x ∈ G k . If ![
$$\\phi _{n}\(x\)> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq418.gif), then ![
$$\\theta _{1}\(x\)> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq419.gif) and ψ n (x) > 0. Hence ![
$$x \\in G_{1} \\cup \\cdots \\cup G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq420.gif) and ![
$$x \\in G_{n} \\cup G_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq421.gif); thus, x ∈ G n . If ![
$$\\phi _{n+1}\(x\)> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq422.gif), then ![
$$\\theta _{2}\(x\)> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq423.gif), and so x ∈ G n+1. By induction, this completes the proof. ■

For the open cover ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq424.gif) and the functions ![
$$\\phi _{1},\\ldots,\\phi _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq425.gif) as in the preceding theorem, we say that these functions are a partition of unity subordinate to the cover. The reason this result is called a partition of unity is that it divides the constantly 1-function into parts that reside inside the open sets G k . Later (Theorem 3.7.17) we will see a more sophisticated partition of unity theorem.

Corollary 3.3.11.

If K is a closed subset of the normal space X and ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq426.gif) G n } are open sets in X that cover K, then there are continuous functions ![
$$\\phi _{1},\\ldots,\\phi _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq427.gif) on X with the following properties:

(a)

for 1 ≤ k ≤ n and all x in X, ![
$$0 \\leq \\phi _{k}\(x\) \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq428.gif) ;

(b)

for 1 ≤ k ≤ n, ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq429.gif) when ![
$$x\\notin G_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq430.gif) ;

(c)

![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\phi _{k}\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq431.gif) for all x in K;

(d)

![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\phi _{k}\(x\) \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq432.gif) for all x in ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq433.gif) .

Proof.

Note that if we put ![
$$G_{n+1} = X\\setminus K$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq434.gif), then ![
$$\\{G_{1},\\ldots,G_{n+1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq435.gif) is an open cover of X. Let ![
$$\\{\\phi _{1},\\ldots,\\phi _{n+1}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq436.gif) be a partition of unity subordinate to this cover. The reader can check that the functions ![
$$\\phi _{1},\\ldots,\\phi _{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq437.gif) satisfy (a), (b), and (c) since ![
$$\\phi _{n+1}\(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq438.gif) for x in K. For any x in X, ![
$$\\sum _{k=1}^{n}\\phi _{k}\(x\) = 1 -\\phi _{n+1}\(x\) \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq439.gif), giving (d). ■

## Exercises

(1)

Supply the missing details in the proof of Proposition 3.3.2.

(2)

Prove Proposition 3.3.3(c).

(3)

Show that if X is normal and ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow Y$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq440.gif) is a continuous surjection that is also a closed map, then Y is normal. (A mapping is closed if the image of every closed set is closed.)

(4)

For disjoint subsets A and B of X and x, y in X, define x ∼ y to mean that one of the following holds: x = y, x, y ∈ A, x, y ∈ B. (See Exercise 3.2.8 for a related exercise.) (a) Verify that this is an equivalence relation on X. Denote the resulting quotient space by X∕{A, B}. (b) If X is normal and A and B are disjoint closed subsets, show that the quotient topology on X∕{A, B} is Hausdorff. (c) If X is a topological space such that X∕{A, B} is Hausdorff for every pair of disjoint closed subsets A, B, then X is normal.

(5)

Show that the dyadic rational numbers are dense in the unit interval.

(6)

If X is normal, use Urysohn's Lemma to show that if F is a closed set and G is an open set with ![
$$F \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq441.gif), then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq442.gif) such that ![
$$f\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq443.gif) when ![
$$x \\in F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq444.gif) and ![
$$\\{x \\in X: f\(x\)> 0\\} \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq445.gif). (Also see Exercise 11, parts (d) and (e).)

(7)

Use Urysohn's Lemma to show that every subspace of a normal space is completely regular.

(8)

Use induction to establish the claim in the proof of Tietze's Extension Theorem.

(9)

(a) Show that ![
$$\\phi \(x\) =\\arctan x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq446.gif) defines a homeomorphism between ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq447.gif) and ![
$$\(-\\pi /2,\\pi /2\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq448.gif). (b) Prove the following extension of Tietze's Extension Theorem. If X is normal, A is a closed subset of X, and ![
$$f: A \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq449.gif) is a continuous function, then there is a continuous function ![
$$F: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq450.gif) with F(a) = f(a) for all a in A.

(10)

Prove the following extension of Tietze's Extension Theorem. If X is normal, A is a closed subset of X, and ![
$$f: A \\rightarrow {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq451.gif) is a continuous function, then there is a continuous function ![
$$F: X \\rightarrow {\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq452.gif) with ![
$$F\(a\) = f\(a\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq453.gif) for all a in A. (Hint: use Exercise 9.)

(11)

A subset A of a topological space X is called a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq454.gif) -set if it is the intersection of a sequence of open sets. A is called an ![
$$F_{\\sigma }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq455.gif) -set if it is the union of a sequence of closed sets. (a) Show that A is a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq456.gif)-set if and only if ![
$$X\\setminus A$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq457.gif) is an ![
$$F_{\\sigma }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq458.gif)-set. (b) Show that the set of irrational numbers is a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq459.gif) set. (c) In a metric space show that every closed set is a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq460.gif)-set and every open set is an ![
$$F_{\\sigma }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq461.gif)-set. (d) In Exercise 6 show that the function f can be chosen with ![
$$F =\\{ x \\in X: f\(x\) = 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq462.gif) if and only if F is a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq463.gif)-set. (e) In Exercise 6 show that the function f can be chosen with ![
$$G =\\{ x \\in X: f\(x\)> 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq464.gif) if and only if G is an ![
$$F_{\\sigma }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq465.gif)-set. (f) Is ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq466.gif) a ![
$$G_{\\delta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq467.gif)-set in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq468.gif)?

## 3.4 The Stone–C̆ech Compactification*

Theorem 3.4.1 (The Stone3–C̆ech4 Compactification).

If X is completely regular, then there is a compact space ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq469.gif) and a homeomorphism τ from X onto a dense subset of ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq470.gif) such that for every bounded continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq471.gif) there is a continuous function ![
$${f}^{\\beta }: \\beta X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq472.gif) with ![
$${f}^{\\beta }\\circ \\tau = f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq473.gif) . Moreover, ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq474.gif) is unique in the sense that if Z is a compact space with a homeomorphism σ of X onto a dense subset of Z such that for each bounded continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq475.gif) there is a continuous ![
$${f}^{Z}: Z \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq476.gif) with ![
$${f}^{Z} \\circ \\sigma = f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq477.gif) , then Z is homeomorphic to ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq478.gif) .

The proof of this theorem is involved. Before starting the proof, let us ruminate on it. If we identify X with its homeomorphic image in ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq479.gif), then the result asserts that each completely regular space X is densely contained in a compact space ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq480.gif) in such a way that each bounded continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq481.gif) has a continuous extension ![
$${f}^{\\beta }: \\beta X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq482.gif). The use of the word contained in this last statement is amply justified by the fact that the compact space ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq483.gif) is unique up to a homeomorphism having special properties as listed in the theorem. Another phrasing of this idea of containment that can be and is used is to say that ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq484.gif) is densely embedded in ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq485.gif).

The space ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq486.gif) is called the Stone–C̆ech compactification of X. This compactification can be very complicated. We will see some exercises below that show this, but consider one example: X = (0, 1]. You might be tempted to think that the Stone–C̆ech compactification of this set is the closed unit interval since X is densely contained in it. This is not the case. For example, if we let ![
$$f: \(0,1\] \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq487.gif) be the bounded continuous function ![
$$f\(t\) =\\sin {t}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq488.gif), then f must have a continuous extension to ![
$$\\beta \(0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq489.gif), but there is no such extension to the closed interval (Exercise 3). So if we take a compact space Z and let X be a dense subset of Z, then X is completely regular (Proposition 3.2.8), but Z may fail miserably to be ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq490.gif). Giving a specific representation of ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq491.gif) for the most innocent noncompact spaces X is usually impossible.

Proof.

Existence. Let ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq492.gif) denote all the continuous functions from X into [0, 1], and for each f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq493.gif) let X f = [0, 1], a copy of the closed unit interval. Let ![
$$\\Omega =\\prod \\{ X_{f}: f \\in \\mathcal{F}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq494.gif), and define ![
$$\\tau: X \\rightarrow \\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq495.gif) by ![
$$\\tau \(x\) =\\{ f\(x\): f \\in \\mathcal{F}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq496.gif); that is, the coordinate of τ(x) corresponding to each f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq497.gif) is given by ![
$$\\tau \(x\)_{f} = f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq498.gif). Let ![
$$\\beta X = \\text{cl}\\,\[\\tau \(X\)\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq499.gif).

Claim.

![
$$\\tau: X \\rightarrow \\tau \(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq500.gif) is a homeomorphism.

In fact, τ is injective since, if x and y are two distinct points in X, then Proposition 3.2.6 implies there is an f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq501.gif) with f(x) = 1 and f(y) = 0; so τ(x) ≠ τ(y). τ is surjective by definition. If {x i } is a net in X and x ∈ X, then an application of Theorem 3.2.10 shows that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq502.gif) if and only if ![
$$\\tau \(x_{i}\)_{f} \\rightarrow \\tau \(x\)_{f}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq503.gif) for every f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq504.gif). By Exercise 2.7.7, this says that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq505.gif) if and only if ![
$$\\tau \(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow \\tau \(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq506.gif) in the product space ![
$$\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq507.gif). Hence we have established the claim.

Claim.

If ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq508.gif), then there is an ![
$${f}^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq509.gif) in ![
$$C\(\\beta X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq510.gif) such that ![
$${f}^{\\beta }\\circ \\tau$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq511.gif).

First note that if ![
$$f \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq512.gif) and we define ![
$${f}^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq513.gif) to be the restriction of the projection map π f to ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq514.gif), then ![
$${f}^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq515.gif) has the desired property. Now if ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq516.gif) and a < f(x) < b for all x in X, then ![
$$g = {\(b - a\)}^{-1}\(f - a\) \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq517.gif). Therefore, ![
$${g}^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq518.gif) exists, and it is a simple matter to verify that defining ![
$${f}^{\\beta } = \(b - a\){g}^{\\beta } + a$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq519.gif) has all the properties we desire. (Verify!)

Uniqueness. Let Z and ![
$$\\sigma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq520.gif) be as in the statement of the theorem, and let Ω be the product space in the proof of existence. Define ![
$$\\omega: Z \\rightarrow \\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq521.gif) by ![
$$\\omega \(z\) =\\{ {f}^{Z}\(z\): f \\in \\mathcal{F}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq522.gif). Note that for ![
$$x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq523.gif) in X and f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq524.gif), ![
$$\\omega \(\\sigma \(x\)\)_{f} = f\(x\) =\\tau \(x\)_{f}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq525.gif). Hence, ![
$${f}^{Z}\[\\omega \(\\sigma \(x\)\)\] = f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq526.gif) for every x in X, ![
$$\\omega \(Z\) \\subseteq \\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq527.gif), and ![
$$\\tau = \\omega \\circ \\sigma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq528.gif). This results in the following commutative mapping diagram.

(3.4.2)

The meaning of the term commutative here is that if we begin with a point x in the space X, no matter which path of maps in this diagram we follow to reach ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq529.gif) or ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq530.gif), we obtain the same result. The fact that the diagram is commutative was established by the equations given prior to the diagram. Commutative diagrams are frequently used in topology. The purpose of presenting this one is to help the reader keep track of the mappings and spaces.

Claim.

![
$$\\omega: Z \\rightarrow \\omega \(Z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq531.gif) is a homeomorphism.

First we note that ω is continuous since for every f in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq532.gif), ![
$$\\pi _{f} \\circ \\omega = {f}^{Z}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq533.gif), and we can invoke Proposition 2.6.5. Let z and w be distinct points in Z; because compact spaces are normal, there is a continuous function ![
$$h: Z \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq534.gif) such that h(z) = 0 and h(w) = 1. If ![
$$f = h \\circ \\sigma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq535.gif), then ![
$$f \\in \\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq536.gif). Since ![
$$\\sigma \(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq537.gif) is dense in ![
$$Z$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq538.gif), it must be that h = f Z . Thus, ![
$$\\omega \(z\)_{f} = {f}^{Z}\(z\) = 0\\neq 1 = {f}^{Z}\(w\) = \\omega \(w\)_{f}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq539.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\omega \(z\)\\neq \\omega \(w\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq540.gif), and ω is injective. So ω is an injective continuous mapping onto its image with a compact domain; by Exercise 2.4.3, the claim is established.

Since ![
$$\\omega \\circ \\sigma =\\tau$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq541.gif) on X, ![
$$\\tau \(X\) \\subseteq \\omega \(Z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq542.gif). Therefore, ![
$$\\beta X \\subseteq \\omega \(Z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq543.gif) because ![
$$\\omega \(Z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq544.gif) is compact. On the other hand, the image of σ is dense in Z, and this implies that ![
$$\\omega \(Z\) \\subseteq \\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq545.gif), so that these two compact spaces are equal. This completes the proof. ■

The notation used in the statement of the theorem is standard. From now on, including in the exercises, we will assume that ![
$$X \\subseteq \\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq546.gif) and that for each f in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq547.gif), ![
$${f}^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq548.gif) is the extension of f to ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq549.gif).

It is not just real-valued continuous functions that can be extended to the Stone–C̆ech compactification.

Theorem 3.4.3.

If X is completely regular and Z is a compact space, then every continuous mapping ![
$$\\eta: X \\rightarrow Z$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq550.gif) has a continuous extension ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }:$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq551.gif) ![
$$\\beta X\\,\\rightarrow \\,Z$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq552.gif) .

Proof.

If ![
$$\\xi \\in \\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq553.gif), then let ![
$$\\{x_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq554.gif) be a net in X such that ![
$$x_{i} \\rightarrow \\xi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq555.gif). Since Z is compact, the net {η(x i )} has a cluster point z in Z. If h ∈ C(Z), then ![
$$h\(\\eta \(x_{i}\)\) \\rightarrow _{\\text{cl}\\,}h\(z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq556.gif). But ![
$$h\\circ \\eta \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq557.gif), so it must be that ![
$$h \\circ \\eta \(x_{i}\) \\rightarrow {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq558.gif). Thus, ![
$$h\(z\) = {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq559.gif) for every h in C(Z). Since compact spaces are normal, this implies that z is the unique cluster point of the net ![
$$\\{\\eta \(x_{i}\)\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq560.gif). By Theorem 2.7.10, this net converges to z. So any net in X that converges in ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq561.gif) is mapped by η to a net that converges in Z. We want to define

![
$$\\displaystyle{{ \\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi \) =\\lim _{i}\\eta \(x_{i}\). }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ4.gif)

(3.4.4)

We must be sure, however, that ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq562.gif) is well defined and does not depend on the choice of net in X that converges to ξ. But if {y j } is a second net in X that converges to ξ and ![
$$\\eta \(y_{j}\) \\rightarrow w$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq563.gif) in Z, then for every h in C(Z) we have that ![
$$h\(w\) =\\lim _{j}h\(\\eta \(y_{j}\)\) = {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi \) =\\lim _{i}h\(\\eta \(x_{i}\)\) = h\(z\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq564.gif). Once again, Urysohn's Lemma implies w = z. Therefore, ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq565.gif) is well defined.

Clearly, ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq566.gif) is an extension of η, so it remains to show that this extension is continuous. Let us start by recouping from the last paragraph the fact that for each h in C(Z),

![
$$\\displaystyle{{\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta } = h {\\circ \\eta }^{\\beta }.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equg.gif)

In fact. this follows from (3.4.4): if {x i } is a net in X that converges to ξ, then, by definition, ![
$$\\eta \(x_{i}\) {\\rightarrow \\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq567.gif); since h is continuous and ![
$$h\\circ \\eta \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq568.gif), ![
$$h{\(\\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)\) =\\lim _{i}h\(\\eta \(x_{i}\)\) =\\lim _{i}\(h\\circ \\eta \)\(x_{i}\) = {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq569.gif). Hence. if {ξ i } is a net in ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq570.gif) such that ![
$$\\xi _{i} \\rightarrow \\xi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq571.gif) and h ∈ C(Z),

![
$$\\displaystyle{h{\(\\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi _{i}\)\) = {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi _{i}\) \\rightarrow {\(h\\circ \\eta \)}^{\\beta }\(\\xi \) = h{\(\\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)\).}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equh.gif)

But, by Theorem 3.2.10 and the fact that Z is completely regular, this implies that ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi _{i}\) {\\rightarrow \\eta }^{\\beta }\(\\xi \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq572.gif). Therefore, ![
$${\\eta }^{\\beta }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq573.gif) is continuous, and the proof is complete. ■

## Exercises

(1)

For a topological space X show that the weak topology defined on X by C b (X) is the same as the weak topology defined by ![
$$\\mathcal{F} =\\{ f \\in C_{b}\(X\): 0 \\leq f \\leq 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq574.gif).

(2)

If X is a normal space and there is a point ξ in ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq575.gif) such that ![
$$\\xi =\\lim _{n}x_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq576.gif) for some sequence {x n } in X, show that ξ ∈ X. Hence the Stone–C̆ech compactification is metrizable only if X is already a compact metric space.

(3)

Show that if X is completely regular and ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq577.gif) is metrizable, then X is a compact metric space so that ![
$$\\beta X = X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq578.gif).

(4)

Here we consider the completely regular space ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq579.gif) and its Stone–C̆ech compactification ![
$$\\beta \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq580.gif). Note that if ![
$$E \\subseteq \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq581.gif), then the characteristic function of E, ![
$$\\chi _{E}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq582.gif) (Exercise 1.5.4), is in ![
$$C_{b}\(\\mathbb{N}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq583.gif). (a) Show that every component of ![
$$\\beta \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq584.gif) reduces to a single point. (b) Show that ![
$$\\beta \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq585.gif) is uncountable. (Actually, the cardinality of ![
$$\\beta \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq586.gif) is 2 c , where c is the cardinality of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq587.gif). This is more difficult to prove.)

## 3.5 Locally Compact Spaces

Here we explore another of the "local" properties that plays a central role in many areas of mathematics.

Definition 3.5.1.

A topological space X is locally compact if for every point x in X and every neighborhood G of x there is another neighborhood U of x such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq588.gif) is compact and contained in G.

Locally compact spaces are everywhere and are the most common spaces discussed in analysis. Here are some examples.

Example 3.5.2.

(a)

Note that a metric space (X, d) is locally compact if and only if for every x in X and every r > 0 there is an ε with ![
$$0 <\\epsilon <r$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq589.gif) such that ![
$$B\(x;\\epsilon \) \\subseteq B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq590.gif) and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;\\epsilon \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq591.gif) is compact.

(b)

![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq592.gif) is locally compact.

(c)

Every compact space is locally compact.

(d)

![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq593.gif) is not locally compact. This follows from Example 1.4.9.

(e)

Let Y be any compact space and fix a point y 0 in Y. If ![
$$X = Y \\setminus \\{y_{0}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq594.gif) has its relative topology, then ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq595.gif) is locally compact (Exercise 2).

Proposition 3.5.3.

(a)

If X is a topological space, then X is locally compact if and only if for every point x in X there is a neighborhood G of x such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq596.gif) is compact.

(b)

If X is a locally compact topological space and ![
$$E \\subseteq X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq597.gif) such that E is either open or closed, then E with its relative topology is locally compact.

(c)

If ![
$$\\{X_{i}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq598.gif) is a collection of topological spaces and ![
$$X =\\prod _{i}X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq599.gif) , then X is locally compact if and only if each X i is locally compact and all but a finite number of the spaces X i are compact.

(d)

If (X,d) is a metric space, then X is locally compact if and only if for every x in X there is an r > 0 such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq600.gif) is compact.

Proof.

(a)

The proof of one direction in this statement is trivial. For the other direction, let G be as in the statement of (a), and let U be any neighborhood of x; we must show that there is a neighborhood V of x such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq601.gif) is compact and contained in U. Note that ![
$$W = G \\cap U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq602.gif) is a neighborhood of x and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq603.gif) is compact; however, it may not satisfy ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,W \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq604.gif). For each y in ![
$$\\partial W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq605.gif), let V y and H y be disjoint open sets such that ![
$$x \\in V _{y} \\subseteq W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq606.gif) and y ∈ H y . Since ![
$$\\partial W \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq607.gif), ![
$$\\partial W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq608.gif) is compact. Let ![
$$y_{1},\\ldots,y_{n} \\in \\partial W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq609.gif) such that ![
$$\\partial W \\subseteq \\bigcup _{j=1}^{n}H_{y_{j}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq610.gif), and let ![
$$V =\\bigcap _{ j=1}^{n}V _{y_{j}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq611.gif). Thus, V is a neighborhood of x, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq612.gif) is compact, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V \\subseteq X\\setminus \\bigcup _{j=1}^{n}H_{y_{j}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq613.gif) since V is a subset of this closed set. Also, ![
$$V \\subseteq W$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq614.gif) and ![
$$V \\cap \\partial W = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq615.gif), so ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V \\subseteq W \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq616.gif), as required.

(b)

Assume that E is closed and x ∈ E. There is an open subset G in X such that x ∈ G and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq617.gif) is compact. Thus ![
$$U = G \\cap E$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq618.gif) is relatively open in E and contains x. Since E is closed, the closure of U in E is contained in ![
$$E \\cap \\text{cl}\\,G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq619.gif) and hence is compact. It follows from part (a) that E is locally compact. The proof of the case where E is open is Exercise 3.

(c)

Suppose that X is locally compact, and fix an index i 0; we will show that ![
$$X_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq620.gif) is locally compact. Let ![
$$x_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq621.gif) in ![
$$X_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq622.gif), and let x be any point in X with ![
$$x_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq623.gif) as its i 0-coordinate. Let ![
$$G_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq624.gif) be a neighborhood of ![
$$x_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq625.gif), and consider ![
$$G =\\pi _{ i_{0}}^{-1}\(G_{i_{0}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq626.gif), a neighborhood of x in X. Since X is locally compact, there is a neighborhood U of x such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq627.gif) is compact and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq628.gif). Thus, ![
$$U_{i_{0}} =\\pi _{i_{0}}\(U\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq629.gif) is open in ![
$$X_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq630.gif) (Proposition 2.6.5(a)), ![
$$U_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq631.gif) contains ![
$$x_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq632.gif), ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq633.gif) is compact since it is contained in ![
$$\\pi _{i_{0}}\(\\text{cl}\\,U\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq634.gif), and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{i_{0}} \\subseteq G_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq635.gif) (Why?). Thus, ![
$$X_{i_{0}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq636.gif) is locally compact. Also, note that the definition of the topology on X implies that there is a finite number of indices ![
$$i_{0},i_{1},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq637.gif) such that when ![
$$i\\neq i_{0},1_{i},\\ldots,i_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq638.gif), ![
$$\\pi _{i}\(\\text{cl}\\,U\) = X_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq639.gif); hence each of these spaces X i is compact.

The proof of the converse is Exercise 4.

(d)

This is an easy consequence of (a). (Exercise 5.)

■

We should note that some restriction on the subset E in part (b) of the preceding proposition is required. For example, we have noted that ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq640.gif) is not locally compact, even though ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq641.gif) is. Also, see Exercise 1. So there are subsets of a locally compact space that are not locally compact when furnished with their relative topology. Look at Exercise 2 for further information. As a comment on part (d), note that ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq642.gif) with the metric ![
$$d\(x,y\) = \\vert x - y\\vert {\(1 + \\vert x - y\\vert \)}^{-1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq643.gif) is locally compact since this metric is equivalent to the usual one; however, it is not true that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq644.gif) is compact for every value of r (for example, r = 1).

The next lemma, which is almost just an observation because of its easy proof, has a use beyond its application in the subsequent theorem.

Lemma 3.5.4.

If X is a locally compact space, K is a compact subset of X, and G is an open set that contains K, then there is an open set U such that ![
$$K \\subseteq U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq645.gif) and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq646.gif) is compact.

Proof.

For each x in K, let U x be a neighborhood such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq647.gif) is compact and contained in G. Since K is compact, there are points ![
$$x_{1},\\ldots,x_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq648.gif) in K such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}U_{x_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq649.gif). If ![
$$U =\\bigcup _{ k=1}^{n}U_{x_{k}}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq650.gif), it has the required properties. ■

Theorem 3.5.5.

If X is a locally compact space, K is a compact subset of X, and G is an open set that contains K, then there is a continuous function ![
$$f: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq651.gif) such that f(x) = 1 for all x in K and f(x) = 0 when x∉G.

Proof.

Invoke the preceding lemma to find an open set with ![
$$K \\subseteq U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq652.gif) and ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq653.gif) compact. Since ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq654.gif) is compact, Urysohn's Lemma implies there is a continuous function ![
$$g: \\text{cl}\\,U \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq655.gif) with g(x) = 1 for x in K and g(x) = 0 for x in ![
$$\\partial U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq656.gif). If we define ![
$$h: X\\setminus U \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq657.gif) to be the identically 0 function, then we can use Proposition 2.3.4 to get the desired continuous function f. ■

There is a temptation to call the preceding theorem Urysohn's Lemma, and some do. After all, if ![
$$B = X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq658.gif), then B is a closed set disjoint from K and the function equals 0 on B. So it certainly has the flavor of Urysohn's Lemma. Nevertheless, it adds an extra condition to the hypothesis by requiring that K be compact since we do not know that X is normal. Similarly, there is a temptation to call the Theorem 3.5.7 below Tietze's Extension Theorem, but we will also resist such an impulse for the same reason.

Corollary 3.5.6.

A locally compact space is completely regular.

Proof.

If F is a closed subset of X and ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq659.gif), put K = { x}, ![
$$G = X\\setminus F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq660.gif), and apply the preceding theorem. ■
