A locally compact space is not necessarily normal. In fact, we pointed out that Example 4 on page. 145 of [4] gives an open subset of a compact space that is not normal. By Proposition 3.5.3(b), such an open set with the relative topology is locally compact. (In fact, that example uses the space of ordinal numbers, which is defined in the next section.)

Theorem 3.5.7.

If X is a locally compact space, K is a compact subset, G is an open set with ![
$$K \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq661.gif) , and ![
$$f: K \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq662.gif) is a continuous function, then there is a continuous function ![
$$F: X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq663.gif) such that F(x) = f(x) for all x in K and F(x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq664.gif) .

Proof.

Again use Lemma 3.5.4 to find an open set U such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq665.gif) is compact and ![
$$K \\subseteq U \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,U \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq666.gif). As in the proof of the preceding theorem, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq667.gif) is normal, so Tietze's Extension Theorem implies there is a continuous function ![
$$F_{1}: \\text{cl}\\,U \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq668.gif) such that ![
$$F_{1}\(x\) = f\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq669.gif) for all x in K and F 1(x) = 0 when ![
$$x \\in \\partial U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq670.gif). Now we proceed as in the proof of Theorem 3.5.5 and obtain the sought-after function F. ■

See Exercise 7.

There is a Baire Category Theorem for locally compact spaces, and this time we succumb to the temptation and call it precisely that.

Theorem 3.5.8 (Baire Category Theorem).

If X is locally compact and {U n } is a sequence of open subsets of X each of which is dense, then ![
$$\\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq671.gif) is dense.

The proof of this is along the lines of the proof of the Baire Category Theorem for a complete metric space (Theorem 1.6.1), with neighborhoods having compact closures replacing the open balls used there. See Exercise 6.

Definition 3.5.9.

If X is locally compact and ![
$$\\phi: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq672.gif) is a continuous function, say that ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq673.gif) vanishes at infinity if for every ε > 0, ![
$$\\{x \\in X: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\geq \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq674.gif) is compact. For any continuous function ![
$$\\phi: X \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq675.gif), define the support of ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq676.gif), in symbols ![
$$\\text{spt}\\,\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq677.gif), as the set

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\text{spt}\\,\\phi = \\text{cl}\\,\\{x \\in X: \\phi \(x\)\\neq 0\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equi.gif)

A continuous function ϕ on X is said to have compact support if ![
$$\\text{spt}\\,\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq678.gif) is a compact set. We denote by C 0(X) the set of all continuous functions on X that vanish at infinity and by C c (X) the set of continuous functions on X having compact support.

The reader may have noticed that the definition of a function vanishing at infinity does not need the underlying topological space to be locally compact. It follows that if there are many continuous functions that vanish at infinity, then there are many open sets with compact closures; and this is close to having X locally compact. In fact, if there is a nonzero function ϕ on X with compact support, then for every ε > 0, ![
$$\\{x \\in X: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\geq \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq679.gif) is a compact set with an interior that at least includes the set ![
$$\\{x \\in X: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert> \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq680.gif). So if we want to assume that there are plenty of continuous functions that vanish at infinity, there must be many open sets with compact closure. So to simplify our lives, we will never discuss C 0(X) unless X is locally compact. The same remarks apply to ![
$$C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq681.gif).

Example 3.5.10.

Let X be a locally compact space.

(a)

The nonzero constant functions on X do not have compact support and do not vanish at infinity unless X is compact.

(b)

![
$$C_{c}\(X\) \\subseteq C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq682.gif), and Theorem 3.5.5 shows the existence of many functions in ![
$$C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq683.gif).

(c)

A function ϕ belongs to ![
$$C_{0}\(\\mathbb{R}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq684.gif) if and only if ![
$$\\lim _{x\\rightarrow \\pm \\infty }\\phi \(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq685.gif).

(d)

If ![
$$\\phi \(x\) =\\exp \(-{x}^{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq686.gif) for x in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq687.gif), then ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(\\mathbb{R}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq688.gif), but ![
$$\\phi \\notin C_{c}\(\\mathbb{R}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq689.gif).

Proposition 3.5.11.

Let X be a locally compact space.

(a)

Both C 0 (X) and C c (X) are subalgebras of ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq690.gif) .

(b)

C 0 (X) is closed in ![
$$C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq691.gif) .

(c)

C c (X) is dense in ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq692.gif) .

Proof.

(a)

The fact that ![
$$C_{0}\(X\) \\subseteq C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq693.gif) is straightforward. In fact, if ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq694.gif), let K be a compact subset of X such that ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert <1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq695.gif) when x∉K. Since K is compact, there is a constant M such that ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\leq M$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq696.gif) whenever x ∈ K. Thus, ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\leq \\max \\{ M,1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq697.gif) for all x, and so ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq698.gif). If ![
$$\\phi _{1},\\phi _{2} \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq699.gif) and ε > 0, then there are compact sets ![
$$K_{1},K_{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq700.gif) such that for j = 1, 2 and ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus K_{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq701.gif), ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{j}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq702.gif). Therefore, for x in ![
$$X\\setminus \(K_{1} \\cup K_{2}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq703.gif), ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{1}\(x\) + \\phi _{2}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert \\phi _{1}\(x\)\\vert + \\vert \\phi _{2}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq704.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\phi _{1} + \\phi _{2} \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq705.gif). The proof of the rest of (a) is Exercise 8.

(b)

Let ![
$$\\{\\phi _{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq706.gif) be a sequence in C 0(X) , and suppose ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq707.gif) such that ![
$$\\phi _{n} \\rightarrow f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq708.gif). Let ε > 0, and choose N such that ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{n}\(x\) - f\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq709.gif) when n ≥ N. Let ![
$$K =\\{ x: \\vert \\phi _{N}\(x\)\\vert \\geq \\epsilon /2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq710.gif). If ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus K$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq711.gif), then ![
$$\\vert f\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert f\(x\) -\\phi _{N}\(x\)\\vert + \\vert \\phi _{N}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq712.gif). Hence ![
$$f \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq713.gif).

(c)

Let ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq714.gif), and assume that ![
$$\\phi \(X\) \\subseteq \[a,b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq715.gif). If ε > 0, let K be a compact set such that ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq716.gif) when x∉K. Let G be an open set with compact closure that contains K (Lemma 3.5.4), and let ![
$$\\psi:X \\rightarrow \[0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq717.gif) be a continuous function with ψ(x) = 1 for x in K and ψ(x) = 0 for x in ![
$$X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq718.gif). So ![
$$\\phi \(x\)\\psi \(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq719.gif) when ![
$$x \\in X\\setminus G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq720.gif) and, hence, ![
$$\\phi \\psi \\in C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq721.gif). Now ![
$$\\vert \\psi \(x\)\\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(x\)\\vert = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq722.gif) for x in K and is less than ![
$$\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq723.gif) when x∉G. When ![
$$x \\in G\\setminus K$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq724.gif), ![
$$\\vert \\psi \(x\)\\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(x\)\\vert \\leq \\vert \\psi \(x\)\\phi \(x\)\\vert + \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\leq 2\\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq725.gif). Hence, ![
$$\\rho \(\\phi,\\psi \\phi \) <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq726.gif), and we have that C c (X) is dense in ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq727.gif).

■

When the locally compact space is metrizable, we get a bonus for the functions that vanish at infinity.

Proposition 3.5.12.

If (X,d) is a locally compact metric space, then every function in C 0 (X) is uniformly continuous.

Proof.

Let ε > 0, and put ![
$$L =\\{ x: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\geq \\epsilon /2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq728.gif); so L is compact. Since X is locally compact, for every x in L there is an r x > 0 such that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,B\(x;r_{x}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq729.gif) is compact [Proposition 3.5.3(d)]. Let ![
$$x_{1},\\ldots,x_{m} \\in L$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq730.gif) such that ![
$$L \\subseteq \\bigcup _{j=1}^{m}B\(x_{j};r_{x_{j}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq731.gif). Choose γ > 0 such that ![
$$\\text{dist}\\,\(x,L\) \\leq \\gamma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq732.gif) implies ![
$$x \\in \\bigcup _{j=1}^{m}B\(x_{j};r_{x_{j}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq733.gif) (Exercise 1.4.7), and set ![
$$K =\\{ x: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,L\) \\leq \\gamma \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq734.gif). Note that K is compact since it is contained in ![
$$\\bigcup _{j=1}^{m}\\text{cl}\\,B\(x_{j};r_{x_{j}}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq735.gif). If we only consider ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq736.gif) as a function on K, it is uniformly continuous there; so there exists ![
$$\\delta _{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq737.gif) such that ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(y\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq738.gif) when x, y ∈ K and ![
$$d\(x,y\) <\\delta _{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq739.gif). Choose a positive δ with ![
$$\\delta <\\min \\{ \\delta _{1},\\gamma \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq740.gif). Let x, y ∈ X such that if x, y ∈ L, then it must be that x, y ∈ K, and so ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(y\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq741.gif). If x ∈ L but y∉L, then the fact that ![
$$d\(x,y\) <\\delta <\\gamma$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq742.gif) implies that x, y ∈ K; hence, ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(y\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq743.gif). If neither point belongs to L, then ![
$$\\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(y\)\\vert \\leq \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert + \\vert \\phi \(y\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2 + \\epsilon /2 = \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq744.gif). ■

We now turn our attention to embedding a locally compact space inside a compact one. If the reader has seen § 3.4, that presents one way of doing this since every locally compact space is completely regular. But here we seek a simpler way, which, of course, does not possess all the properties of ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq745.gif) but has other virtues—especially simplicity. The process is related to Example 3.5.3(d). Specifically, if ![
$$X = \(0,1\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq746.gif), then the embedding will be into [0, 1], which, as was pointed out in § 3.4, is far different than ![
$$\\beta X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq747.gif).

Before giving the definition, we prove a result that justifies it. But first, let us take a moment to see how we add a single point to a given abstract set X. What we want to do is just add it: we are given a set X and take some abstract point—for lack of a better name we call it ∞—and we consider the set ![
$$X \\cup \\{\\infty \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq748.gif). If you are comfortable with that, go directly to the next proposition and do not worry about the intervening material. If you are bothered by taking the union of these two sets X and {∞} when they are not both subsets of a common set, let us take a moment to allay your concerns.

There are many ways to do this, and all are equivalent. Here is one way. First take two points anywhere—say, the points 0 and 1 in ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq749.gif)—and look at X ×{ 0, 1}. Now fix a point y in X and consider the set ![
$$Z = X \\times \\{ 0\\} \\cup \\{ \(y,1\)\\} \\subseteq X \\times \\{ 0,1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq750.gif). The set Z contains a copy of X, namely X ×{ 0}, and ![
$$Z\\setminus X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq751.gif) is a single point, {(y, 1)}. So X ×{ 0} and X are identified, and we call the point (y, 1) by the name ∞. Thus we have added a single point to ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq752.gif).

My advice is to be practical, abandon this formality, and just be direct. Adopt the casual approach described previously. That is what we do in what follows.

Proposition 3.5.13.

If X is a locally compact space, then there is a compact space X ∞ having the following properties.

(a)

![
$$X \\subseteq X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq753.gif) and ![
$$X_{\\infty }\\setminus X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq754.gif) is a single point denoted by ![
$$\\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq755.gif) ;

(b)

A subset U of ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq756.gif) is open if and only if the following two conditions are satisfied: (i) ![
$$U \\cap X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq757.gif) is open in X; (ii) if ![
$$\\infty \\in U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq758.gif) , then ![
$$X_{\\infty }\\setminus U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq759.gif) is a compact subset of X;

(c)

If ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq760.gif) and we define ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi }: X_{\\infty }\\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq761.gif) by setting ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi }\(x\) = \\phi \(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq762.gif) when x ∈ X and ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi }\(\\infty \) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq763.gif) , then ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi } \\in C\(X_{\\infty }\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq764.gif) . Conversely, if ![
$$f \\in C\(X_{\\infty }\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq765.gif) such that ![
$$f\(\\infty \) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq766.gif) and ![
$$\\phi = f\\vert X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq767.gif) , then ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq768.gif) and ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi } = f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq769.gif) .

Note that part (b) of the proposition says that the relative topology that X has as a subset of X ∞ is its original topology. Equivalently, X is homeomorphically contained in ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq770.gif).

Proof.

Let ∞ be an abstract point, and let ![
$$X_{\\infty } = X \\cup \\{\\infty \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq771.gif). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq772.gif) be the collection of all subsets U of ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq773.gif) that satisfy the condition stated in (b). We leave it to the reader to verify that ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq774.gif) is a topology on ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq775.gif) (including that it has the Hausdorff property). Now to show that ![
$$\(X_{\\infty },\\,\\mathcal{U}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq776.gif) is compact. If ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq777.gif) is an open cover of ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq778.gif), then there is a set U 0 in the cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq779.gif) such that ![
$$\\infty \\in U_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq780.gif). By definition of the topology ![
$$\\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq781.gif), ![
$$K = X_{\\infty }\\setminus U_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq782.gif) is compact. Thus, there are sets ![
$$U_{n},\\ldots,U_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq783.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq784.gif) such that ![
$$X_{\\infty }\\setminus U_{0} \\subseteq \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}U_{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq785.gif). Therefore, ![
$$\\{U_{0},U_{1},\\ldots,U_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq786.gif) is the sought-after finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq787.gif), and we have established that X ∞ is compact. This proves parts (a) and (b) of the proposition.

To show that (c) holds is not difficult. If ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq788.gif) and ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq789.gif) is as in (c), then for any open subset G of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq790.gif) that does not contain 0 we have that ![
$$\\tilde{{\\phi }}^{-1}\(G\) ={ \\phi }^{-1}\(G\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq791.gif), an open subset of X; thus, ![
$$\\tilde{{\\phi }}^{-1}\(G\) \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq792.gif). If r > 0, then ![
$$X_{\\infty }\\setminus \\tilde{{\\phi }}^{-1}\(\(-r,r\)\) =\\{ x \\in X: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\geq r\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq793.gif) is compact, so ![
$$\\tilde{{\\phi }}^{-1}\(\(-r,r\)\) \\in \\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq794.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq795.gif) is continuous on ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq796.gif). Now assume that ![
$$f \\in C\(X_{\\infty }\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq797.gif) with f(∞) = 0, and put ![
$$\\phi = f\\vert X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq798.gif). It is immediate from the definition of the relative topology on X that ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq799.gif). On the other hand, for any ε > 0, the fact that f(∞) = 0 implies that ![
$$\\{x \\in X: \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\geq \\epsilon \\} = X\\setminus {f}^{-1}\(\(-\\epsilon,\\epsilon \)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq800.gif), which is compact. Thus, ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq801.gif). The fact that ![
$$\\tilde{\\phi } = f$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq802.gif) is clear. ■

Definition 3.5.14.

If X is a locally compact space, the topological space X ∞ in the preceding proposition is called the one-point compactification of X.

See Exercise 10.

Example 3.5.15.

(a)

The one-point compactification of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq803.gif) is homeomorphic to the circle ![
$$\\{\(x,y\) \\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{2}: {x}^{2} + {y}^{2} = 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq804.gif). It is not [−∞, ∞] because this requires adding two points.

(b)

The one-point compactification of (0, 1] is the closed unit interval. The one-point compactification of (0, 1) is the circle.

(c)

The one-point compactification of the plane is the sphere in ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{3}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq805.gif): {(x, y, z) ![
$$\\in {\\mathbb{R}}^{3}: {x}^{2} + {y}^{2} + {z}^{2} = 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq806.gif).

The one-point compactification has many uses. For example, when we want to prove something about a locally compact space, we can first prove it for compact spaces and then show that it holds for the open subset of a compact space that results from removing a single point. In effect, that is what was going on when we proved the analogues of Urysohn's Lemma (Theorem 3.5.5) and Tietze's Theorem (Theorem 3.5.7) to locally compact spaces. See Exercise 11.

Nevertheless, the only topological property we will investigate about the one-point compactification is the question of when it is metrizable. That is, when is there a metric on X ∞ such that the topology defined by the metric is the topology of ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq807.gif)? (See the end of § 2.1.) As it turns out, we will see that this question is related to another question that has importance. Several necessary conditions are revealed after a few minutes' thought. First is that when X ∞ is metrizable, then X is metrizable since the topology of X is the relative topology it gets as a subspace of ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq808.gif). Second, if (X, d) is a locally compact metric space and X ∞ is metrizable, then the metric on X will not necessarily be the restriction of the metric from ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq809.gif). For example, as we pointed out in Example 3.5.15(a), the one-point compactification of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq810.gif) is homeomorphic to the circle. For a compact metric space, the metric must be bounded. That is, if ![
$$\(Z,\\eta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq811.gif) is a compact metric space, then there is a constant M such that ![
$$\\eta \(z,w\) \\leq M$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq812.gif) for all z, w in Z. So if X ∞ is metrizable, then all we can conclude is that the metric on X is equivalent (Definition 1.3.12) to the metric it inherits from ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq813.gif). Finally, note that if X ∞ is metrizable and η is a metric on X ∞ that defines its topology, then for any r > 0, ![
$$X\\setminus B\(\\infty;r\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq814.gif) is a compact subset of X since B(∞; r) is an open subset of X ∞ that contains ![
$$\\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq815.gif). By taking ![
$$r = 1/n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq816.gif), we see that X can be written as the union of a sequence of compact sets. We isolate this property.

Definition 3.5.16.

Say that a topological space X is σ -compact if it is the union of a sequence of compact sets.

We note that ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq817.gif) is σ-compact, as is every open subset of Euclidean space (Exercise 16). If X is locally compact and σ-compact, then we can write X as the union of compact sets K n such that ![
$$K_{n} \\subseteq \\text{int}\\,K_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq818.gif) (Exercise 17).

The proof of the next theorem is long and complicated, though each step is not so difficult. Just take it slow and understand each step. After going through the proof once, go back over it and try to get the big picture. In other words, first examine all the trees and then step back and look at the forrest. You will be better off for the double effort. In this proof, we will see our first significant use of a partition of unity (Corollary 3.5.11) and perhaps begin to appreciate the power of this tool.

Theorem 3.5.17.

If (X,d) is a locally compact metric space, the following statements are equivalent.

(a)

X ∞ is metrizable.

(b)

X is σ-compact.

(c)

The metric space C 0 (X) is separable.

Proof.

(a) implies (b). This was already shown prior to the statement of the theorem.

(b) implies (c). We write ![
$$X =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }K_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq819.gif), where each K n is compact and ![
$$K_{n} \\subseteq \\text{int}\\,K_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq820.gif) for all n ≥ 1 (Exercise 17). Find a decreasing sequence of positive numbers ![
$$\\{\\delta _{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq821.gif) such that ![
$$\\{x: \\text{dist}\\,\(x,K_{n}\) <\\delta _{n}\\} \\subseteq \\text{int}\\,K_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq822.gif). (How?) For each n and each ![
$$k \\geq n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq823.gif), let ![
$$\\{B\(a_{nk}^{j};\\delta _{k}\): 1 \\leq j \\leq m_{nk}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq824.gif) be open disks with ![
$$a_{nk}^{j}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq825.gif) in K n such that ![
$$K_{n} \\subseteq \\bigcup _{j=1}^{m_{nk}}B\(a_{ nk}^{j};\\delta _{ k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq826.gif). Note that for k ≥ n this union of open disks is a subset of ![
$$\\text{int}\\,K_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq827.gif). As in Corollary 3.3.11, for each n ≥ 1 and k ≥ n, let ![
$$\\{\\phi _{nk}^{j}: 1 \\leq j \\leq m_{nk}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq828.gif) be continuous functions on X with ![
$$0 \\leq \\phi _{nk}^{j} \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq829.gif), ![
$$\\phi _{nk}^{j}\(x\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq830.gif) for ![
$$x\\notin B\(a_{nk}^{j};\\delta _{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq831.gif), ![
$$\\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk}}\\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq832.gif) when x ∈ K n , and ![
$$\\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk}}\\phi _{ nk}^{j} \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq833.gif). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq834.gif) be the linear span of the functions ![
$$\\{\\phi _{nk}^{j}: n \\geq 1,k \\geq n,\\text{ and }1 \\leq j \\leq m_{nk}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq835.gif) with coefficients from the rational numbers ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq836.gif). Note that ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq837.gif) is a countable subset of ![
$$C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq838.gif). We will show that ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq839.gif) is dense in ![
$$C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq840.gif) and, hence, in ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq841.gif) (Proposition 3.5.11).

Fix f in ![
$$C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq842.gif), and let ε > 0; so there is an integer n such that f(x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin K_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq843.gif). Since f is uniformly continuous, there is a ![
$$\\delta> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq844.gif) such that ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(y\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq845.gif) whenever ![
$$d\(x,y\) <\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq846.gif). Pick ![
$$k \\geq n$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq847.gif) such that ![
$$\\delta _{k} <\\delta$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq848.gif). For ![
$$1 \\leq j \\leq m_{nk}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq849.gif) let ![
$$q_{nk}^{j} \\in \\mathbb{Q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq850.gif) such that ![
$$\\vert q_{nk}^{j} - f\(a_{nk}^{j}\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq851.gif). Hence, ![
$$g =\\sum _{ j=1}^{m_{nk}}q_{ nk}^{j}\\phi _{ nk}^{j} \\in \\mathcal{M}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq852.gif). Now fix x in K n . Thus (reader: pay attention here to how the partition of unity is used),

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\vert f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert & =& \\left \\vert \\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\[f\(x\) - q_{nk}^{j}\]\\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\)\\right \\vert {}\\\\ &\\leq & \\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\left \\vert f\(x\) - f\(a_{nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert \\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) +\\sum _{ j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\left \\vert f\(a_{nk}^{j}\) - q_{ nk}^{j}\\right \\vert \\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) {}\\\\ & \\leq & \\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\left \\vert f\(x\) - f\(a_{nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert \\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) + \\epsilon /2. {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ5.gif)

Now when ![
$$\\phi _{nk}^{j}\(x\)\\neq 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq853.gif), ![
$$x \\in B\(a_{nk}^{j};\\delta _{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq854.gif), and so ![
$$\\left \\vert f\(x\) - f\(a_{nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq855.gif). That is, each term in the last sum is either ![
$$0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq856.gif) or, if not, smaller than ![
$$\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq857.gif). Thus, the sum is dominated by

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\(\\epsilon /2\)\\phi _{nk}^{j}\(x\) = \\epsilon /2.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equj.gif)

Inserting this inequality into the preceding one yields that ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq858.gif) when x ∈ K n . Suppose now that ![
$$x \\in \\left \[\\bigcup _{j=1}^{m_{nk}}B\(a_{ nk}^{j};\\delta _{ k}\)\\right \]\\setminus K_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq859.gif). So f(x) = 0. If ![
$$x \\in B\(a_{nk}^{j};\\delta _{k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq860.gif), then, by the uniform continuity condition on f, ![
$$\\left \\vert f\(a_{nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert = \\left \\vert f\(a_{nk}^{j}\) - f\(x\)\\right \\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq861.gif). Therefore,

![
$$\\displaystyle\\begin{array}{rcl} \\vert f\(x\) - g\(x\)\\vert & =& \\vert g\(x\)\\vert {}\\\\ & \\leq & \\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\left \\vert q_{nk}^{j}\\right \\vert \\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) {}\\\\ & \\leq & \\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\left \[\\left \\vert q_{nk}^{j} - f\(a_{ nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert + \\left \\vert f\(a_{ nk}^{j}\)\\right \\vert \\right \]\\phi _{ nk}^{j}\(x\) {}\\\\ & <& \\left \(\\epsilon /2 + \\epsilon /2\\right \)\\sum _{j=1}^{m_{nk} }\\phi _{nk}^{j}\(x\). {}\\\\ & \\leq \\epsilon & {}\\\\ \\end{array}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equ6.gif)

Finally, if ![
$$x\\notin \\bigcup _{j=1}^{m_{nk}}B\(a_{ nk}^{j};\\delta _{ k}\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq862.gif), then ![
$$f\(x\) = 0 = g\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq863.gif). Therefore, if ρ denotes the metric on ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq864.gif), then we have that ![
$$\\rho \(f,g\) <\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq865.gif), and so ![
$$\\mathcal{M}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq866.gif) is dense in ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq867.gif).

(c) implies (a). Here is an outline of the proof, which will be followed by the details. First note that when ![
$$\\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq868.gif), ![
$$\\eta _{\\phi }\(x,y\) = \\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi \(y\)\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq869.gif) is symmetric (![
$$\\eta _{\\phi }\(x,y\) =\\eta _{\\phi }\(y,x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq870.gif)) and satisfies the triangle inequality. ![
$$\\eta _{\\phi }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq871.gif) is called a semimetric. Extending ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq872.gif) to X ∞ by setting ![
$$\\phi \(\\infty \) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq873.gif) enables us to see that ![
$$\\eta _{\\phi }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq874.gif) defines a semimetric on ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq875.gif). Now using the fact that C 0(X) is separable we can use a countable dense subset of ![
$$B =\\{ \\phi \\in C_{0}\(X\): \\vert \\phi \(x\)\\vert \\leq 1\\text{ for all }x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq876.gif) to generate a sequence of such semimetrics and sum them up to get a true metric on ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq877.gif). We will then show that this metric defines the topology on ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq878.gif). Now for the details.

Let ![
$$\\{\\phi _{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq879.gif) be a countable dense sequence in the subset B of ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq880.gif) defined in the last paragraph, and define ![
$$\\eta \(x,y\) =\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }{2}^{-n}\\vert \\phi _{n}\(x\) -\\phi _{n}\(y\)\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq881.gif) for all x, y in ![
$$X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq882.gif). Note that for x in X, ![
$$\\eta \(x,\\infty \) =\\sum _{ n=1}^{\\infty }{2}^{-n}\\vert \\phi _{n}\(x\)\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq883.gif) since ![
$$\\phi _{n}\(\\infty \) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq884.gif) for all n. Clearly, η(x, y) = η(y, x). If η(x, y) = 0, then ![
$$\\phi _{n}\(x\) = \\phi _{n}\(y\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq885.gif) for all n ≥ 1. Assume x ≠ y; provided ![
$$x\\neq \\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq886.gif), there is a function ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq887.gif) in C c (X) such that ![
$$\\phi \(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq888.gif) and ![
$$\\phi \(y\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq889.gif). (Why?) Let ![
$$n \\geq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq890.gif) such that ![
$$\\rho \(\\phi,\\phi _{n}\) <\\frac{1} {2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq891.gif). It follows that ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{n}\(x\)\\vert> \\frac{1} {2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq892.gif) and ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{n}\(y\)\\vert <\\frac{1} {2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq893.gif). Thus, it cannot be that η(x, y) = 0. We leave it to the reader to verify that the triangle inequality holds for η so that it is a metric on X.

It remains to prove that this metric η on X ∞ defines its topology. To accomplish this, we will show that the inclusion map ![
$$\(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(X_{\\infty },\\eta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq894.gif) is a homeomorphism onto its image and that ![
$$\\{x:\\eta \(x,\\infty \) \\geq \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq895.gif) is compact for every ![
$$\\epsilon> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq896.gif). (Are you clear that this will do the job?)

Claim.

A sequence {x n } converges to x in ![
$$\(X_{\\infty },\\eta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq897.gif) if and only if ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow \\phi _{k}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq898.gif) for all ![
$$k \\geq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq899.gif).

If ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq900.gif) in (X ∞ , η), then the fact that ![
$${2}^{-k}\\vert \\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) -\\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert \\leq \\eta \(x_{n},x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq901.gif) implies ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow \\phi _{k}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq902.gif) for all k ≥ 1. Now assume that ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow \\phi _{k}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq903.gif) for all k ≥ 1, and let ε > 0. Choose m such that ![
$$\\sum _{k=m}^{\\infty } \\frac{1} {{2}^{k}} <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq904.gif), and choose N such that for n ≥ N and 1 ≤ k ≤ m, ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) -\\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2m$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq905.gif). Thus, for n ≥ N,

![
$$\\displaystyle{\\eta \(x_{n},x\) <\\epsilon /2 +\\sum _{ k=1}^{m} \\frac{1} {{2}^{k}} \\frac{\\epsilon } {2m} <\\epsilon,}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equk.gif)

proving the claim.

Claim.

The inclusion map ![
$$\(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(X_{\\infty },\\eta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq906.gif) is a homeomorphism.

The first claim proves that if ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq907.gif) in X, then ![
$$\\eta \(x_{n},x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq908.gif); that is, the inclusion map ![
$$\(X,d\) \\rightarrow \(X_{\\infty },\\eta \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq909.gif) is continuous. For the converse, suppose that ![
$$\\eta \(x_{n},x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq910.gif), where x and x n belong to X for all n ≥ 1. Suppose that {d(x n , x)} does not converge to 0; then there is an ε > 0 and a subsequence ![
$$\\{x_{n_{j}}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq911.gif) such that ![
$$d\(x_{n_{j}},x\) \\geq \\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq912.gif) for all n j . We can assume that ![
$$\\epsilon$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq913.gif) is small, say ![
$$\\epsilon <\\frac{1} {2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq914.gif). Using Theorem 3.5.5, there is a function ![
$$\\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq915.gif) in C c (X) such that ![
$$0 \\leq \\phi \\leq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq916.gif), ![
$$\\phi \(x\) = 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq917.gif), and ![
$$\\phi \(y\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq918.gif) when ![
$$d\(y,x\) \\geq \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq919.gif). Since ![
$$\\{\\phi _{k}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq920.gif) is dense in B, there is a ![
$$\\phi _{k}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq921.gif) such that ![
$$\\rho \(\\phi _{k},\\phi \) <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq922.gif). Hence, ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{k}\(x_{n_{j}}\)\\vert = \\vert \\phi _{k}\(x_{n_{j}}\) -\\phi \(x_{n_{j}}\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq923.gif). On the other hand, ![
$$1 = \\phi \(x\) \\leq \\vert \\phi \(x\) -\\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert + \\vert \\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert <\\epsilon /2 + \\vert \\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq924.gif), and so ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert> 1 -\\epsilon /2> \\epsilon /2$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq925.gif). This contradicts the assumption that ![
$$\\phi _{k}\(x_{n_{j}}\) \\rightarrow \\phi _{k}\(x\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq926.gif), which, by the first claim, contradicts the assumption that ![
$$\\eta \(x_{n},x\) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq927.gif). This establishes the second claim.

Claim.

![
$$\\{x:\\eta \(x,\\infty \) \\geq \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq928.gif) is compact for every ![
$$\\epsilon> 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq929.gif).

Let ε > 0 and put ![
$$K =\\{ x \\in X:\\eta \(x,\\infty \) \\geq \\epsilon \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq930.gif). Suppose K is not compact. For each n ≥ 1, put ![
$$K_{n} =\\{ x \\in X: \\vert \\phi _{k}\(x\)\\vert \\geq \\frac{1} {n}\\text{ for }1 \\leq k \\leq n\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq931.gif). Since K is closed and each K n is compact, it cannot be that ![
$$K \\subseteq K_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq932.gif). Therefore, there is a point x n in K such that ![
$$x_{n}\\notin K_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq933.gif). That is, ![
$$\\vert \\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\)\\vert <\\frac{1} {n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq934.gif) for 1 ≤ k ≤ n. This says that for every ![
$$k \\geq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq935.gif), ![
$$\\lim _{n}\\phi _{k}\(x_{n}\) = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq936.gif). According to the first claim, this implies that ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow \\infty$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq937.gif) or that ![
$$\\eta \(x_{n},\\infty \) \\rightarrow 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq938.gif). Since each x n ∈ K, this is a contradiction. Therefore, K must be compact.

This completes the proof. ■

Corollary 3.5.18.

If X is a compact topological space, then C(X) is separable if and only if X is a compact metric space.

Proof.

The fact that ![
$$X$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq939.gif) is a compact space implies ![
$$X = X_{\\infty }$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq940.gif). Thus, the corollary is immediate from the theorem. ■

## Exercises

(1)

Show that ![
$$X =\\{ \(0,0\)\\} \\cup \\{ \(x,y\): x> 0,y \\in \\mathbb{R}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq941.gif) with the relative topology from ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{2}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq942.gif) is not locally compact. So an arbitrary subset of a locally compact space is not necessarily locally compact.

(2)

(a) Show that a dense subset of a locally compact space is locally compact if and only if it is open. (This gives another proof that ![
$$\\mathbb{Q}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq943.gif) is not locally compact.) (b) Show that a subset E of a locally compact space X is locally compact if and only if ![
$$E = A\\setminus B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq944.gif), where both A and B are closed subsets of X.

(3)

Finish the proof of Proposition 3.5.3(b).

(4)

Finish the proof of Proposition 3.5.3(c).

(5)

Give the details of the proof of Proposition 3.5.3(d).

(6)

Prove the Baire Category Theorem for locally compact spaces.

(7)

If X is a locally compact space, K is a compact subset, G is an open set with ![
$$K \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq945.gif), and ![
$$f \\in C_{b}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq946.gif) with ![
$$a \\leq f\(x\) \\leq b$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq947.gif) for all x in X, then there is a continuous function ![
$$F: X \\rightarrow \[a,b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq948.gif) such that F(x) = f(x) for all x in K and F(x) = 0 when ![
$$x\\notin G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq949.gif).

(8)

Complete the proof of Proposition 3.5.11(a).

(9)

If X is a locally compact space and ![
$$f,g \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq950.gif), show that ![
$$f \\vee g$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq951.gif) and ![
$$f \\wedge g \\in C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq952.gif). Similarly, if ![
$$f,g \\in C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq953.gif), show that f ∨ g and ![
$$f \\wedge g \\in C_{c}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq954.gif).

(10)

Phrase and prove a proposition that shows that the one-point compactification of a locally compact space is unique up to a homeomorphism.

(11)

If X is a locally compact space, use the one-point compactification of X to give proofs of Theorems 3.5.5 and 3.5.7 as a consequence of Urysohn's Lemma and the Tietze Extension Theorem.

(12)

What is the one-point compactification of ![
$${\\mathbb{R}}^{q}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq955.gif)?

(13)

Describe the one-point compactification of ![
$$\\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq956.gif) by finding a subset of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq957.gif) that is homeomorphic to it.

(14)

Let I be any nonempty set, and for each i in I let X i be a copy of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq958.gif) with the metric ![
$$d_{i}\(x,y\) = \\vert x - y\\vert$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq959.gif). Let X be the disjoint union of the sets X i . (That is a verbal description that can be used in any circumstance, but if you want precision, you can say ![
$$X = \\mathbb{R} \\times I$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq960.gif), the cartesian product, where I has the discrete topology.) Define a metric on X by letting d agree with d i on each X i ; and when x ∈ X i , y ∈ X j , where i ≠ j, then define d(x, y) = 1. (a) Show that d is indeed a metric on X. (b) Show that {X i : i ∈ I} is the collection of components of X and each of these components is an open subset of X. (c) Show that (X, d) is separable if and only if I is a countable set.

(15)

Consider the metric space (X, d) defined in Exercise 14, and assume that I is not countable. (a) Show that (X, d) is locally compact. (b) Show that (X, d) is not σ-compact and, hence, X ∞ is nonmetrizable. (c) Find an infinite subset A of X such that ∞ is in the closure of A in X ∞ but no sequence of points from A converges to ∞ in the one-point compactification.

(16)

If X is a locally compact, σ-compact topological space, show that every open subset and every closed subset with the relative topology is also σ-compact. [See Proposition 3.5.3(b).]

(17)

Assume that X is locally compact and σ-compact. (a) Show that we can write X as the union of compact sets K n such that ![
$$K_{n} \\subseteq \\text{int}\\,K_{n+1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq961.gif). (b) Show that there is a sequence of functions ![
$$\\{\\phi _{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq962.gif) in C c (X) such that for every ϕ in ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq963.gif), ![
$$\\phi _{n}\\phi \\rightarrow \\phi$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq964.gif) in the metric of ![
$$C_{0}\(X\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq965.gif).

(18)

If X is locally compact, show that X is σ-compact if and only if there is a function ϕ in C 0(X) such that ![
$$X =\\{ x: \\phi \(x\)\\neq 0\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq966.gif).

## 3.6 Ordinal Numbers*

The purpose of this short section is to introduce two topological spaces that are very useful for constructing examples. The building of these spaces relies on the concept of an ordinal number. For a precise exposition of this topic the reader is referred to [4] and to the appendix of [6]. There are many other sources. The approach here is to outline the definition of ordinal numbers and then state the basic properties needed for the progress of this section. We omit the proofs of those properties that would take us too far from our main objective. The reader can take these unproven statements as axioms or consult the references for the proofs.

The definition of ordinal numbers is similar to that of cardinal numbers. We consider all well-ordered sets, that is, a linearly ordered set (§ A.4) ![
$$\(S,\\leq \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq967.gif) such that if E is a nonempty subset of S, then E contains a least element. Say that two such sets are equivalent if there is an order isomorphism between them; that is, there is a bijection between the two sets that preserves the order. An ordinal number is an equivalence class of such sets. (There are some logical problems here in considering the collection of all such sets. Nevertheless, ignoring such problems will not cause any difficulty for us. If you want to delve into this, consult a friendly logician.) If two well-ordered sets are equivalent, then we may say that they have the same order type.

It is not difficult to give examples of such sets. Every finite set is easily made into a well-ordered set, and no matter how we do this, two finite, well-ordered sets are equivalent if and only if they have the same number of elements. The set ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq968.gif) is a well-ordered set, but ![
$$\\mathbb{Z}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq969.gif) and ![
$$\\mathbb{R}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq970.gif) with their natural ordering are not. (Why?) Other examples of well-ordered sets are ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq971.gif), ![
$$\\{2 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq972.gif), ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 2 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 1,2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq973.gif), and ![
$$\\{m - \\frac{1} {n}: m,n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq974.gif), with the order they have as subsets of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq975.gif).

For the rest of this section we only consider well-ordered sets ![
$$\(S,\\leq \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq976.gif).

We might pause to look at these examples and observe that nonempty subsets of a well-ordered set do not always contain a largest element even though they contain a smallest one. Nevertheless, we want to define supE for a nonempty subset E of S. When ![
$$\\{y \\in S: y \\geq x\\text{ for all }x \\in E\\} = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq977.gif), this is not possible, as a moment's reflection will show. When this is not the case, however, we define ![
$$\\sup E$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq978.gif) as the smallest element in the set ![
$$\\{y \\in S: y \\geq x\\text{ for all }x \\in E\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq979.gif). What we just observed prior to this definition is that when supE exists, it may not belong to E. We will denote the always extant smallest element of E as infE or minE.

We note that the sets ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq980.gif) and ![
$$\\{2 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq981.gif) have the same order type, while ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 1\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq982.gif) and ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 2 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 1,2\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq983.gif) do not. While the positive of these two statements is easy to see and the negative one is intuitively clear, one way to prove the negative statement is as follows.

We give a well-ordered set ![
$$\(S,\\leq \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq984.gif) the order topology (Exercise 2.2.5). In this situation the order topology on S is Hausdorff. In fact, if x < y in S, then U = { z ∈ S: z < y} and V = { z ∈ S: x < z} are disjoint open sets, x ∈ U and y ∈ V. Now note that an order isomorphism between two well-ordered sets is a homeomorphism when they are given their order topology. Since the set ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq985.gif) has one limit point while the set ![
$$\\{1 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\} \\cup \\{ 2 - \\frac{1} {n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq986.gif) has two, they cannot have the same order type.

When we have a well-ordered set ![
$$\(S,\\leq \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq987.gif), we will often refer to its elements as ordinals. In fact, if x ∈ S, then the set {y ∈ S: y ≤ x} is a well-ordered set and we can take x as its order type. The last paragraph illustrates that there are two distinct types of ordinals in (S, ≤ ): limit ordinals and nonlimit or discrete ordinals. Say that x is a limit ordinal in (S, ≤ ) precisely when it is a limit point in the order topology. Using the order this means x is a limit ordinal if and only if for any y in S with y < x there is a z in S with y < z < x. Say that x is a discrete ordinal when it is not a limit point in the order topology or, equivalently, when it is an isolated point of the topological space. Thus, x is a discrete ordinal if and only if the singleton {x} is an open set. Using the order, this means that x is a discrete ordinal if and only if there is a y < x such that (y, x) = ∅; in other words, x has an immediate predecessor in the order. We denote the immediate predecessor by x − 1.

Some additional notation is useful. In (S, ≤ ), if a ≤ b, we will give the sets (a, b), [a, b], (a, b], and [a, b) their obvious meaning. Also, if x ∈ S and {y ∈ S: x < y} ≠ ∅, then this set has a least element and we denote it by x \+ 1. That is, x \+ 1 is the first element in S bigger than x.

It is also true that the set of ordinal numbers is itself a well-ordered set—a fact that needs a proof that will not be given here. If we consider all the ordinal numbers that are uncountable, then it follows that there is a least such ordinal number and we denote it by ![
$$\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq988.gif). (Giving an example of an uncountable ordinal is difficult. It can be shown that there is a well ordering of ![
$$\\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq989.gif), but that is very difficult. Another example is the set of all countable ordinals.) The number Ω is called the first uncountable ordinal.

The spaces ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq990.gif) of all ordinal numbers that are less than or equal to Ω and [0, Ω) of all countable ordinals, as topological spaces, are the object of study in this section. We record some fundamental order properties of [0, Ω] and ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq991.gif).

Proposition 3.6.1.

(a)

The spaces ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq992.gif) and [0,Ω) are uncountable, well-ordered spaces such that if ![
$$x \\in \[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq993.gif) , then ![
$$\\{y \\in \[0,\\Omega \]: y \\leq x\\} =\\{ y \\in \[0,\\Omega \): y \\leq x\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq994.gif) is a countable set.

(b)

If ![
$$\\varnothing \\neq E \\subseteq \[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq995.gif) , then ![
$$\\sup E \\in \[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq996.gif) .

(c)

If ![
$$E$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq997.gif) is a countable subset of ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq998.gif) , then ![
$$\\sup E <\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq999.gif) .

The spaces [0, Ω] and [0, Ω) have many interesting topological properties. We begin with some of the most basic.

Proposition 3.6.2.

(a)

The collection of sets ![
$$\\{\(a,b\]: a,b \\in \[0,\\Omega \]\\text{ and }a <b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1000.gif) is a base for the topology of ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1001.gif) .

(b)

The collection of sets ![
$$\\{\(a,b\]: a,b \\in \[0,\\Omega \)\\text{ and }a <b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1002.gif) is a base for the topology of ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1003.gif) .

(c)

If {x n } is an increasing sequence in ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1004.gif) and x = sup {x n }, then ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1005.gif) in the order topology.

(d)

A subset F of [0,Ω) is closed if and only if it is sequentially closed.

Proof.

The proofs of (a) and (b) are similar, and we only prove (b). Note that when a < b, then ![
$$\(a,b\] = \(a,b + 1\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1006.gif), so each set in ![
$$\\mathcal{B} =\\{ \(a,b\]: a,b \\in \[0,\\Omega \)\\text{ and }a <b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1007.gif) is open in ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1008.gif). Now assume G is an open set in [0, Ω) and x ∈ G. Since the order intervals ![
$$\\{\(a,b\): a,b \\in X\\text{ and }a <b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1009.gif) form a base for the topology (Exercise 2.2.5), there is one such interval such that ![
$$x \\in \(a,b\) \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1010.gif). But then ![
$$x \\in \(a,x\] \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1011.gif).

(c) If G is a neighborhood of x, then by (a) there is a point a with a < x such that ![
$$\(a,x\] \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1012.gif). From the definition of supremum there is an integer N such that x n > a when n ≥ N. Thus x n ∈ G for all n ≥ N, proving the statement.

(d) Since one implication is obvious, it is only required to show that F is closed when it is sequentially closed. Let ![
$$x \\in \\text{cl}\\,F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1013.gif). If x is a discrete ordinal, then {x} is open, and it follows that x ∈ F. Assume that x is a limit ordinal and G is a neighborhood of x. By (b), there is a point a < x such that ![
$$\(a,x\] \\subseteq G$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1014.gif). By Proposition 3.6.1(a), (a, x) is a countable set; thus, we can find a sequence {x n } in (a, x) such that x n < x n+1 and x = supx n . (Why?) By (c), ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1015.gif). Thus, x ∈ F. ■

Theorem 3.6.3.

The following conditions hold.

(a)

[0,Ω] is a compact topological space.

(b)

A subset of [0,Ω) is compact if and only if it is closed and order bounded.

(c)

[0,Ω) is locally compact but not compact.

(d)

[0,Ω) is a normal space.

Proof.

We begin this proof by establishing the following claim.

Claim.

If ![
$$a <b <\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1016.gif), then [a, b] is compact.

From the properties of the ordinal numbers we know that [a, b] is a countable set, so any open cover of [a, b] has a countable subcover. Thus, we need only show that a countable open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G} =\\{ G_{n}: n \\in \\mathbb{N}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1017.gif) of [a, b] has a finite subcover. Suppose ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1018.gif) has no finite subcover; then for each n in ![
$$\\mathbb{N}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1019.gif) we have that ![
$$F_{n} = \[a,b\]\\setminus \\bigcup _{k=1}^{n}G_{k}\\neq \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1020.gif). If ![
$$x_{n} =\\inf F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1021.gif), then x n ∈ F n . Also, ![
$$F_{n+1} \\subseteq F_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1022.gif). (Why?) So for n ≥ N, x n ∈ F N and x n ≥ x N . If x = supx n , then it follows that x ∈ [a, b] and x ∈ F n for all n in ![
$$\\mathbb{N}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1023.gif). That is, ![
$$x \\in \[a,b\] \\cap \\bigcap _{n=1}^{\\infty }F_{n} = \[a,b\]\\setminus \\bigcup _{n=1}^{\\infty }G_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1024.gif), contradicting the fact that ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1025.gif) is a cover of [a, b].

(a)

Using Proposition 2.4.5 we need only show that every cover by sets from the base of the topology has a finite subcover. If ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1026.gif) is an open cover of ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1027.gif) by basic open sets of the form (a, b], fix ![
$$\(a_{0},\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1028.gif) in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1029.gif). Now ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]\\setminus \(a_{0},\\Omega \] = \[0,a_{0}\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1030.gif), and the claim says that this latter set is compact. Thus, we can find a finite number of sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1031.gif) that cover [0, a 0], and, together with ![
$$\(a_{0},\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1032.gif), this provides the sought-after finite subcover of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1033.gif). Thus, [0, Ω] is compact.

(b)

If K is closed and bounded in ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1034.gif), then there are ![
$$a <b <\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1035.gif) such that ![
$$K \\subseteq \[a,b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1036.gif). Using the claim we have that K is a closed subset of a compact set.

(c)

If ![
$$x \\in \[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1037.gif), then each set ![
$$\(a,x\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1038.gif) is a neighborhood of ![
$$x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1039.gif), which, by the claim, has compact closure.

(d)

Suppose A and B are two disjoint closed sets in ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1040.gif). For each a in A put ![
$$b_{a} =\\sup \\{ b \\in B: b <a\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1041.gif). Note that (b a , a] is an open neighborhood of a that is disjoint from B. Thus, if ![
$$U =\\bigcup \\{ \(b_{a},a\]: a \\in A\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1042.gif), then U is an open set, ![
$$A \\subseteq U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1043.gif), and U ∩ B = ∅. Similarly for each b in B put ![
$$a_{b} =\\sup \\{ a \\in A: a <b\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1044.gif), so that a b ∈ A, and if ![
$$V =\\bigcup \\{ \(a_{b},b\]: b \\in B\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1045.gif), then V is an open set that contains B and is disjoint from A. Now to show that ![
$$U \\cap V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1046.gif). In fact, if this is not the case, then there is an a in A and a b in B such that ![
$$\(b_{a},a\] \\cap \(a_{b},b\]\\neq \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1047.gif). Let ![
$$c \\in \(b_{a},a\] \\cap \(a_{b},b\]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1048.gif); so ![
$$b_{a} <c \\leq a$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1049.gif) and ![
$$a_{b} <c \\leq b$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1050.gif). Now either b < a or a < b. If b < a, then, by the definition of b a , we have that ![
$$b \\leq b_{a} <c \\leq a$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1051.gif). Since we also have that ![
$$a_{b} <c \\leq b$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1052.gif), we have a contradiction. Similarly, the assumption that a < b leads to a contradiction. Thus, ![
$$U \\cap V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1053.gif).

■

Now for a truly surprising fact about ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1054.gif).

Proposition 3.6.4.

If ![
$$f: \[0,\\Omega \) \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1055.gif) is a continuous function, then there is a point a in [0,Ω) such that f is constant on ![
$$\\{x \\in \[0,\\Omega \): x \\geq a\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1056.gif) .

Proof.

We assume the proposition is false and establish the following claim.

Claim.

For every n ≥ 1 there is an a n in [0, Ω) such that whenever x > a n , we have that ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(a_{n}\)\\vert <\\frac{1} {n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1057.gif).

If this claim is false, then it follows that there is an n 0 ≥ 1 such that for every z in [0, Ω) there exists an x > z with ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(z\)\\vert \\geq 1/n_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1058.gif). We now define a sequence ![
$$x_{0},x_{1},\\ldots$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1059.gif) inductively. Let x 0 = 0. If we have x n for some n ≥ 0, let ![
$$x_{n+1} =\\inf \\{ x \\in \(x_{n},\\Omega \): \\vert f\(x\) - f\(x_{n}\)\\vert \\geq 1/n_{0}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1060.gif), where we know this set is nonempty because we assumed the claim was false. Note that x n < x n+1 and, because f is continuous, ![
$$\\vert f\(x_{n+1}\) - f\(x_{n}\)\\vert \\geq 1/n_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1061.gif). It follows that ![
$$c =\\sup _{n}x_{n} <\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1062.gif) [Proposition 3.6.1(c)] and ![
$$x_{n} \\rightarrow c$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1063.gif). Again using the continuity of f, ![
$$f\(x_{n}\) \\rightarrow f\(c\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1064.gif), and so {f(x n )} is a Cauchy sequence. But we have that ![
$$\\vert f\(x_{n+1}\) - f\(x_{n}\)\\vert \\geq 1/n_{0}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1065.gif), contradicting the Cauchy condition. This establishes the claim.

If ![
$$\\{a_{n}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1066.gif) is the sequence whose existence is given by the claim, then put ![
$$a =\\sup _{n}a_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1067.gif); again, ![
$$a <\\Omega$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1068.gif) and ![
$$a_{n} \\rightarrow a$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1069.gif). But for any x in ![
$$\[a,\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1070.gif) we have that x > a n , and so ![
$$\\vert f\(x\) - f\(a_{n}\)\\vert <\\frac{1} {n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1071.gif). Since ![
$$f\(a_{n}\) \\rightarrow f\(a\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1072.gif), we have that ![
$$f\(x\) - f\(a\)\\vert = 0$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1073.gif). That is, f(x) = f(a) for all ![
$$x \\geq a$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1074.gif). ■

Corollary 3.6.5.

If ![
$$f: \[0,\\Omega \) \\rightarrow \\mathbb{R}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1075.gif) is a continuous function, then f is bounded.

Corollary 3.6.6.

The Stone–C̆ech compactification of [0,Ω) is the space ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1076.gif) , which is also its one-point compactification.

Proof.

By the proposition every f in ![
$$C\(\[0,\\Omega \)\) = C_{b}\(\[0,\\omega \)\)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1077.gif) is eventually constant. Thus letting ![
$$\\tilde{f}\(\\Omega \)$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1078.gif) be this constant implies that this gives a continuous extension of ![
$$\\tilde{f}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1079.gif) to ![
$$\[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1080.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\beta \[0,\\Omega \) = \[0,\\Omega \]$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1081.gif) by the uniqueness part of Theorem 3.4.1. ■

## 3.7 Paracompactness

The definition of paracompactness requires two preliminary concepts.

Definition 3.7.1.

If X is a topological space and ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1082.gif) is a collection of subsets of X, then ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1083.gif) is locally finite if for each x in X there is a neighborhood U of x such that U meets only a finite number of sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1084.gif). If ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1085.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1086.gif) are two collections of subsets of X, then ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1087.gif) is said to be a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1088.gif) if each D in ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1089.gif) is contained in some set S that belongs to ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1090.gif).

Let us underline a few points about this last definition. First, the sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1091.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1092.gif) are not assumed open or closed and neither is assumed to be a cover of X. Most of the time when we use the concepts of locally finite and refinement we will be discussing open covers, but there will be times, especially as we develop this topic, when we want extra leeway.

Note that every finite collection of sets is locally finite and a subcollection of ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1093.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1094.gif). Also, if ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1095.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{S}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1096.gif) and ![
$$\\mathcal{E}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1097.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1098.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{E}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1099.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{S}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1100.gif).

Definition 3.7.2.

A topological space X is paracompact if for every open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1101.gif) of X there is an open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1102.gif) of X that is locally finite and a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1103.gif).

Example 3.7.3.

(a)

Every compact space is paracompact.

(b)

If X can be written as the pairwise disjoint union of open subsets {G i : i ∈ I} such that each G i with its subspace topology is paracompact, then X is paracompact. In fact, if ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1104.gif) is an open cover of X and ![
$$\\mathcal{D}_{i} =\\{ C \\cap G_{i}: C \\in \\mathcal{C},i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1105.gif), then ![
$$\\mathcal{D}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1106.gif) is a cover of G i and a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1107.gif). Since G i is paracompact, there is an open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{E}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1108.gif) of G i that is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{D}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1109.gif). If ![
$$\\mathcal{E} =\\bigcup _{i}\\mathcal{E}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1110.gif), then it can be verified (do it) that ![
$$\\mathcal{E}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1111.gif) is a cover of X that is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{D} =\\bigcup _{i}\\mathcal{D}_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1112.gif) and, hence, of the original cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1113.gif).

We will see many more examples as we progress. You can interpret paracompactness as a generalization of compactness. This will become amplified as we progress. Far less apparent is that paracompactness is a generalization of metrizability. In fact, we have some work to do before this becomes even a meaningful comment, let alone a verifiable one.

Proposition 3.7.4.

If X is paracompact and F is a closed subset of X, then F is paracompact.

Proof.

If ![
$$\\mathcal{D}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1114.gif) is an open cover of F, then ![
$$\\mathcal{C} = \\mathcal{D}\\cup \\{ X\\setminus F\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1115.gif) is an open cover of X. Thus, there is a locally finite open cover of X, ![
$$\\mathcal{C}_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1116.gif), that is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1117.gif). If ![
$$\\mathcal{D}_{1} =\\{ U \\in \\mathcal{C}_{1}: U \\cap F\\neq \\varnothing \\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1118.gif), then it can be verified that ![
$$\\mathcal{D}_{1}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1119.gif) is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{D}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1120.gif) that covers F. ■

An open subset of a paracompact space is not necessarily paracompact. In fact, the space [0, Ω] (§ 3.6) is compact, [0, Ω) is an open subset, but [0, Ω) is not paracompact (Example 3.7.13).

Proposition 3.7.5.

A paracompact space is normal.

Proof.

Assume X is a paracompact topological space. This proof proceeds in stages. We first show that X is regular and then use this to show it is normal.

To show that X is regular, we will use Proposition 3.2.2 and show that if F is a closed subset of X and ![
$$c\\notin F$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1121.gif), then there is an open set V such that ![
$$F \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1122.gif) and ![
$$c\\notin \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1123.gif). To see this, use the fact that X is Hausdorff to obtain that for every point x in F there is a neighborhood G x of x such that ![
$$c\\notin \\text{cl}\\,G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1124.gif) (Exercise 2.1.7). Thus, {G x : x ∈ F} is an open cover of F. By the preceding proposition, there is an open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1125.gif) of F that is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\{G_{x}: x \\in F\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1126.gif). Put ![
$$V =\\bigcup \\{ U: U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1127.gif). Note that V is an open set that contains F. Exercise 1 implies that since ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1128.gif) is locally finite, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V =\\bigcup \\{ \\text{cl}\\,U: U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1129.gif), something that is far from true for arbitrary unions. By the refinement property, for each U in ![
$$\\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1130.gif) there is an x in F such that ![
$$U \\subseteq G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1131.gif). Since ![
$$c\\notin \\text{cl}\\,G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1132.gif), ![
$$c\\notin \\text{cl}\\,U$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1133.gif); hence we have that ![
$$c\\notin \\text{cl}\\,V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1134.gif).

We now prove that X is normal. By Proposition 3.3.2, it suffices to show that if A and ![
$$B$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1135.gif) are disjoint closed sets, then there is an open set V such that ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1136.gif) and ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1137.gif). For each b in B the regularity of X implies there is an open set O b such that ![
$$b \\in O_{b} \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,O_{b} \\subseteq X\\setminus A$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1138.gif). Let ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1139.gif) be a locally finite refinement of the open cover ![
$$\\{O_{b}: b \\in B\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1140.gif) of B, and put ![
$$V =\\bigcup \\{ U: U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1141.gif); clearly, ![
$$B \\subseteq V$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1142.gif). As in the preceding paragraph, it follows that ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V =\\bigcup \\{ \\text{cl}\\,U: U \\in \\mathcal{U}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1143.gif); and, since ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,O_{b} = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1144.gif) for every b in B, it follows that ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,U = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1145.gif) for every U in ![
$$\\mathcal{U}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1146.gif) and so ![
$$A \\cap \\text{cl}\\,V = \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1147.gif). ■

See Example 3.7.13 below for a normal space that is not paracompact. The next result can be found in [7], and some extensions appear in [8].

Theorem 3.7.6 (Michael's5 Theorem).

If X is a regular topological space such that every open cover of X has a refinement cover ![
$$\\mathcal{A}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1148.gif) that can be written as ![
$$\\mathcal{A} =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }\\mathcal{A}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1149.gif) , where each ![
$$\\mathcal{A}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1150.gif) is a locally finite collection, then X is paracompact.

The proof requires a few lemmas, but before we start that process, a few remarks about the condition and the nature of the result would be helpful. First, note that, even though we started with an open cover of X, the collections ![
$$\\mathcal{A}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1151.gif) are not required to be covers, nor are the sets in them required to be open; ![
$$\\mathcal{A}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1152.gif), however, is required to be a cover. Second, since the refinement ![
$$\\mathcal{A}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1153.gif) is not required to be locally finite but only the countable union of locally finite collections, this builds even more flexibility into the condition. It is this lack of restrictions that gives the theorem merit. This result does not give insight into the structure of paracompact spaces but rather provides us a tool for showing that a space is paracompact. Finally, the reader may have observed that the condition here is far weaker than the stated definition, so that we could have stated this as a necessary and sufficient condition for paracompactness. Later we will use this theorem to give several important examples of classes of paracompact spaces.

Here is the first lemma, whose purpose here is to facilitate the proof of the second lemma.

Lemma 3.7.7.

If X is a topological space such that every open cover of X has a locally finite closed cover that is a refinement, then X is paracompact.

Proof.

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1154.gif) be an open cover of X. By hypothesis, there is a closed cover refinement ![
$$\\mathcal{F} =\\{ F_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1155.gif) of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1156.gif) that is locally finite. By the definition of locally finite, for each x in X there is a neighborhood W x of x such that ![
$$W_{x} \\cap F_{i}\\neq \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1157.gif) for only a finite number of the closed sets F i in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1158.gif). Note that ![
$$\\mathcal{W} =\\{ W_{x}: x \\in X\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1159.gif) is an open cover of X. Once again, apply the hypothesis to obtain a closed cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1160.gif) that is locally finite and a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{W}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1161.gif). Now for each ![
$$F_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1162.gif) define

![
$$\\displaystyle{V _{i} = X\\setminus \\bigcup \\{C: \\text{ such that }C \\in \\mathcal{C}\\text{ and }C \\cap F_{i} = \\varnothing \\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equl.gif)

By Exercise 1, V i is open. Moreover, ![
$$F_{i} \\subseteq V _{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1163.gif) (Verify!), so it follows that ![
$$\\mathcal{V} =\\{ V _{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1164.gif) is an open cover of X.

Claim.

![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1165.gif) is a locally finite open cover.

To see this, examine what it means to have a C in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1166.gif) such that ![
$$V _{i} \\cap C\\neq \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1167.gif). In fact, from the definition of V i this can only happen when ![
$$F_{i} \\cap C\\neq \\varnothing$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1168.gif). On the other hand, each C in ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1169.gif) is contained in some W x ; since each W x intersects only a finite number of the sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{F}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1170.gif), C can intersect only a finite number of F i . Because ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1171.gif) is a locally finite cover of X, this implies ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1172.gif) is locally finite. Now we need to adjust the sets in ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1173.gif) to obtain a locally finite open refinement of the original cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1174.gif); this is not hard. For each i in I consider V i and the corresponding F i in the closed locally finite cover. Recall that ![
$$F_{i} \\subseteq V _{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1175.gif). Since ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1176.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1177.gif), there is a set G i in ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1178.gif) with ![
$$F_{i} \\subseteq G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1179.gif). Look at the collection of open sets ![
$$\\mathcal{U} =\\{ V _{i} \\cap G_{i}: i \\in I\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1180.gif). This is an open cover since each ![
$$F_{i} \\subseteq V _{i} \\cap G_{i}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1181.gif), and it is locally finite by the claim. Clearly ![
$$\\mathcal{U}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1182.gif), from the way it was defined, is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1183.gif), so this completes the proof. ■

Lemma 3.7.8.

If X is a regular topological space such that for every open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1184.gif) of X there is a cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1185.gif) that is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1186.gif) , then X is paracompact, even though it is not required that ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1187.gif) contain either open or closed sets.

Proof.

Let ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1188.gif) be an open cover of X. By the previous lemma we need only show that there is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1189.gif) by a closed locally finite cover. For x in X, let ![
$$G_{x} \\in \\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1190.gif) such that ![
$$x \\in G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1191.gif). Since X is regular, there is a neighborhood ![
$$V _{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1192.gif) of ![
$$x$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1193.gif) with ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,V _{x} \\subseteq G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1194.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\mathcal{V} =\\{ V _{x}: x \\in X\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1195.gif) is an open cover that refines ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1196.gif). ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1197.gif) itself may not be locally finite, but, according to the hypothesis, there is a cover ![
$$\\mathcal{C}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1198.gif) that is a locally finite refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{V}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1199.gif). Now Exercise 2 implies that ![
$$\\mathcal{F} =\\{ \\text{cl}\\,C: C \\in \\mathcal{C}\\}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1200.gif) is also locally finite. But if ![
$$C \\in \\mathcal{C}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1201.gif), then there is a V x in ![
$$\\mathcal{V}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1202.gif) with ![
$$C \\subseteq V _{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1203.gif). Thus, ![
$$\\text{cl}\\,C \\subseteq \\text{cl}\\,V _{x} \\subseteq G_{x}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1204.gif), so that ![
$$\\mathcal{F}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1205.gif) is a locally finite closed cover that is a refinement of the original open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1206.gif). By the preceding lemma, X is paracompact. ■

Proof of Michael's Theorem.

For an open cover ![
$$\\mathcal{G}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1207.gif) of X let ![
$$\\mathcal{A} =\\bigcup _{ n=1}^{\\infty }\\mathcal{A}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1208.gif) be a cover of X, where each ![
$$\\mathcal{A}_{n}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1209.gif) is a locally finite collection of subsets of X and such that ![
$$\\mathcal{A}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1210.gif) is a refinement of ![
$$\\mathcal{G}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1211.gif). By the last lemma, to show that X is paracompact, we need only show that ![
$$\\mathcal{A}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1212.gif) has a locally finite refinement cover by not necessarily open sets.

For each ![
$$n \\geq 1$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1213.gif) let

![
$$\\displaystyle{B_{n} =\\bigcup \\{ A: A \\in \\mathcal{A}_{n}\\}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equm.gif)

Put B 0 = ∅; since ![
$$\\mathcal{A}\\;$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_IEq1214.gif) is a cover, so is {B n : n ≥ 1}. Let

![
$$\\displaystyle{C_{n} = B_{n}\\setminus B_{n-1}.}$$
](A316906_1_En_3_Chapter_Equn.gif)

Note that: (1) {C n : n ≥ 1} is a cover of X since {B n : n ≥ 1} is; (2) the sets ![
$$C_{1},C_{2},\\ldots$$
